Approval
This thesis has been prepared over five months at DTU Construct - Department of Civil
and Mechanical Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU), as part of the
requirements for obtaining the degree Master of Science in Design and Innovation, MSc
Eng.
It is assumed that the reader has a general understanding of engineering principles, in-
cluding basic knowledge of mechanical systems, electronics, and programming.

ii
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
==PAGE:5==
Abstract
This thesis presents the design and development of an automated pH measurement sys-
tem tailored for monitoring milk acidification during dairy fermentation. The system aims
to reduce manual workload and improve process efficiency by enabling increased walk-
away time. Developed through iterative design sprints, the prototype integrates mechan-
ical, electronic, and software components into a compact and modular architecture. Key
features include a multi-channel pH measurement, automated calibratio, cleaning func-
tions, and data logging capabilities. Collaboration with Novonesis and DTU’s DALSA Lab
ensured the system addresses real-world industrial and research needs, offering a cost-
effective alternative to existing solutions such as the iCinac system.
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification

==PAGE:11==
1
Learning Objectives
The following learning objectives outline the key competencies and outcomes targeted
and used throughout the course of the project. They reflect both the technical and method-
ological skills developed, as well as the collaborative and problem-solving capabilities
required to carry out a structured, iterative design process in close cooperation with aca-
demic and industrial partners.
1. Use user-centric design methods to research and develop solutions.
2. Understand the needs of the involved stakeholders.

future solution should be shaped.
4. The final prototype should be functional to the highest possible degree within the
time frame.

production of additional units, depending on the functionality and success.

lution.
8. Select and apply relevant methods for an iterative design process.
9. Plan and execute a full design process, from research to final concept.

2
Introduction
2.1
Problem definition
This project originates from a clearly defined industrial need formulated by Novonesis - a
leading biosolutions company focused on food innovation and fermentation technologies
(see Section 2.3.1). In collaboration with DTU’s DALSA Lab2.3.2, Novonesis presented
a challenge centered around the automation of pH monitoring during dairy fermentation,
a key part of their research and quality control pipeline. A detailed description of their
problem definition is provided in Appendix A.1.
Currently, pH measurement in their workflows relies heavily on the iCinac system, which
consists of up to 32 individual glass electrodes manually mounted into a large multime-
ter interface. Each electrode must be prepared, calibrated, maintained, and cleaned by
hand, creating a time-consuming and labor-intensive process that also introduces variabil-
ity. While effective, the system lacks scalability, automation compatibility, and ergonomic
usability - particularly when moving toward higher-throughput fermentation screening.
Based on this input, our own project objective was defined and submitted at the begin-
ning of the development phase (see Appendix A.2). The goal was to create an automated,
multi-channel pH measurement system specifically designed for compatibility with com-
mon labware such as microtiter plates (MTPs) and deep-well plates (DWPs). The system
would serve as a proof-of-concept for a more scalable and modular alternative to existing
solutions, with the long-term potential to reduce manual workload while improving repro-
ducibility and efficiency in pH data collection.
The proposed system by Novonesis integrates:
• A modular array of 12–24 micro pH electrodes embedded in a protective, 3D-printed
holder designed to fit standard 12-, 24-, or 96-well plates.
• An interface for periodic voltage-based data acquisition (5–10 second intervals),
with flexibility to later expand to channel switching or multi-input configurations.
• An automated cleaning and calibration station, featuring pressurized rinsing and
electrode maintenance functionality.
• Design elements allowing the device to be handled by a robotic arm or liquid handler,
enhancing compatibility with modern lab automation setups.
• Data logging functionality and cost-effective design considerations compared to the
existing iCinac platform.
• Preference for industry-standard glass pH electrodes, though ISFET alternatives
may be explored in the future if performance criteria are met.
This challenge spans disciplines - from mechanical design and embedded electronics to
software, control systems, and user-centered design - and provides an ideal framework for
applying polytechnical methods to create a functional, testable prototype. As this report
will show, the first stage of development has resulted in a working concept that fulfills the
main objectives and is prepared for further refinement and potential field validation.

2.2
Scope
Establishing a clear scope is essential for managing complexity and maintaining focus
throughout the development process. In the early stages of the project, a preliminary
boundary was defined to clarify which system components would be actively developed,
and which were considered outside the current scope. This boundary was shaped by
resource constraints, time limitations, and relevance to the project’s core objectives.
To support this, we created a scope table outlining what is considered in scope and out
of scope. As the project progressed, the table was revisited to accommodate new ideas
and unforeseen challenges. This structured approach enabled the team to evaluate each
potential addition to the system in terms of feasibility and impact, and ensured that the
final solution remained aligned with the primary goal: to develop an automated pH mea-
surement system. Furthermore each design sprint was initiated with a scope overview,
this helped us align expectations while also limiting scope creep.
The table below summarizes the finalized scope and serves as a reference point for what
has been included or excluded from the current iteration of the design.

2.3
Stakeholders
Throughout this project we worked in close cooperation with our stakeholders. The two
most important stakeholders was Novonesis and DALSA.
2.3.1
Novonesis
Novonesis is a global biosolutions company formed in 2024 through the merger of Novozymes
and Chr. Hansen. Combining deep expertise in enzymes, microbes, and fermentation,
Novonesis develops sustainable solutions across food, agriculture, and industrial biotech-
nology.
Their expertise in fermentation, biological workflows, and industry-scale application makes
Novonesis an ideal stakeholder and partner for our project-offering critical insight, real-
world testing needs, and the potential for downstream implementation of automated pH
measurement systems in biotech production.

Their expertise in fermentation, biological workflows, and industry-scale application makes
Novonesis an ideal stakeholder and partner for our project - offering critical insight, real-
world testing needs, and the potential for downstream implementation of automated pH
measurement systems in biotech production. A central figure in this collaboration has
been Kim Friis Olsson, Senior Scientist in Automation at Novonesis, who not only intro-
duced the original problem definition but also acted as our primary contact throughout the
project. His technical input, knowledge of the iCinac process, and constructive feedback
have been great in shaping both the direction and relevance of the work.
2.3.2
DALSA
DALSA (DTU Arena for Life Science Automation) [1] is a collaborative research and de-
velopment facility at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU), dedicated to advancing
automation in life science research and manufacturing. Located at DTU Lyngby, DALSA
brings together experts from bioengineering, robotics, and data science to design, test,
and deploy cutting-edge automation solutions.
The arena serves as both a research hub and a proof-of-concept lab, supporting applica-
tions such as liquid handling, cell-free biology, and diagnostic workflows. It is equipped
with state-of-the-art platforms like the Hamilton Microlab, and is open to academic and
industrial collaborators.
Through hands-on education, shared infrastructure, and interdisciplinary partnerships,
DALSA aims to accelerate innovation in life science automation and lay the foundation for
a future national centre.
This strong focus on practical automation, open collaboration, and cross-disciplinary de-
velopment makes DALSA an ideal stakeholder and partner for projects like ours, which
seek to combine engineering innovation with real-world biological workflows in an attempt
to automate a normally manual process. Throughout the project, Aske Unger, Automa-
tion Specialist at DTU’s Institute for Biotechnology and Medicine, has played a key role in
guiding us through the lab environment. He provided insights into automation in the life
sciences, demonstrated equipment, and supported us in conducting relevant biological
experiments that grounded our engineering work in real application contexts.
2.4
Time plan
To manage and structure the project effectively, we used OpenProject, a project manage-
ment tool that we self-hosted on a home server. This platform allowed us to break the
project down into individual tasks, track progress collaboratively, and visualize the overall
timeline using a Gantt chart. From the beginning, our plan was organized into a series
of four main sprints 3.1.1, each defined as a phase in OpenProject and concluded with
a milestone. This setup provided a clear overview of the entire workflow, including when
to focus on early prototyping, system integration, and final refinement of both the product
and the written report. Having this structure in place from the start gave us a concrete
sense of when to focus on what, and it was crucial in maintaining momentum and bal-
ancing development efforts with documentation and analysis throughout the five-month
project. A detailed version can be seen here A.3.
2.5
Current process
To be able to understand the problem, it is first important to understand the current work-
flow. To gain a thorough understanding of the current workflow, a visit was conducted to
the Novonesis facilities in Hørsholm, Denmark. During the visit, a laboratory technician
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
5
==PAGE:16==
guided us through the experimental setup process. This provided valuable insights into
each individual step, as well as the overall structure of the workflow.
Although the individual tasks involved are relatively simple and not technically challeng-
ing, the process is highly time-consuming, especially when handling a large number of
samples. Repetition and manual handling significantly extend the total experiment time.
The most critical and time-intensive steps identified include:
• Preheating of the water bath used for warming the milk.
• Cleaning of the pH-probe between steps.
• Calibration of the electrodes prior to each experiment.
These key steps are illustrated in figure 2.1, which outlines the current experimental pro-
cedure and highlights the potential for automation.
Based on the insights provided by the laboratory technician, one of the most critical initial
steps when preparing for an experiment is to begin preheating the water bath as early
as possible. The water baths used at Novonesis are significantly larger than standard
household devices like electric kettles and thus require substantial time to reach the target
temperature. Since the test cannot begin until the water bath has reached the correct
temperature, early initiation of heating is essential to avoid delays.
The next step involves checking the day’s scheduled experiments. Barcodes assigned
to each sample are printed in advance, enabling the lab technician to quickly scan them
during subsequent steps of the process.
Following this, the lab technician retrieves the milk from a refrigerated storage room. At
this point, it is verified whether sufficient milk is available to conduct all planned experi-
ments. This step is particularly important, as milk is typically ordered once per week, and
the actual experimental needs may differ from those initially anticipated. As such, there
are occasions where the wrong quantity of milk is available. It’s important to note that
in the current setup, milk is ordered in individual bottles, each corresponding to one ex-
periment (e.g., 50 bottles for 50 samples). The experiments are also done in these glass
bottles. If an insufficient quantity is available, a decision must be made on whether to
proceed with a reduced number of tests or postpone the entire experiment.
Once the milk has been retrieved, calibration of the pH-probe can begin. As this is a critical
step for the system under development, it is described in more detail in Section 2.5.1.
While the pH-probe are being calibrated, the necessary cultures are prepared and after-
wards inoculated into the milk. The experiments can vary from being completely unique
(e.g., for research and development - R&D purposes) to identical (e.g., for quality con-
trol - QC). The specifics of this depend on the department in which the experiments are
conducted.
After the calibration is complete, the pH-probe are flushed and inserted into the milk sam-
ples. During the calibration process the milk is inserted into the preheated water bath
and are acclimated. When the probes are done being calibrated, then the milk sample
is inoculated and placed back in the water bath, where they will remain for the duration
of the experiment. It is crucial that the pH-probe be inserted into the milk and the milk
placed into the water bath simultaneously, or as close in timing as possible. This step be-
comes increasingly challenging when working with more than 18 samples. It should also
6
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
==PAGE:17==
be noted that special rubber lids are used to seal off the bottles during the experiment to
ensure that no additional oxygen is added and no moisture escapes.
Upon completion of the experiment, the system must be cleaned. This involves removing
the pH-probe from the milk and cleaning them using one of two methods:
• Flushing with demineralised water, followed by ethanol, then wiping with a napkin
and placing the pH-probe back into a potassium chloride (here after KCl) storage
solution.
• Alternatively, wiping the pH-probe with a 70% ethanol-soaked napkin (e.g., Chicopee
Veraclean Polish Plus), followed by placement in KCl.
Once the pH-probe are back in the KCl solution, they are either ready for the next exper-
iment, after the cycle has been repeated again, or they can be stored until future use.
Figure 2.1: Flowchart showing the current process of pH measurement and sample han-
dling.
2.5.1
Detailed description of calibration process
Within the system we are trying to design, the calibration of the pH-probe are one of the
critical features. It was therefore important to clarify the steps in this part of the process.
At Novonesis the calibration process consisted of multiple steps. Firstly, the pH-probe
had to be stabilized in pH buffer. A pH buffer is a solution that resists changes in pH when
small amounts of acid or base are added. It consists of a weak acid and its conjugate base
or a weak base and its conjugate acid, maintaining a stable pH within a specific range.
Buffers are essential in biological systems, chemical reactions, and industrial processes
where pH stability is crucial.
Calibration is done using the water bath, where a testing tube for each of the pH probes
is filled with buffer. The bath is then preheated to the same temperature at which the
experiment is performed. Once the pH-probes, in the testing tubes, reach the desired
temperature, the pH is measured and the pH-probes are automatically adjusted by the
iCinac. Based on at least two measurements in two different buffers, a slope value is
calculated. This value is a percentile deviation of the standard value. At Novonesis the
slope value must be within ±3% of the normal values at the different pH levels. If the value
is higher or lower the probe is not approved for the experiment. When this happens, there
are two possibilities: either the probe is tested again, or another probe is used. One thing
to note is that the experiment cannot be started until all pH-probe have been cleared. It is
therefore normal to calibrate an extra probe alongside the one that has failed. By doing
so, if the probe fails again, the extra one can be used as a replacement. This requires
that the iCinac is not running at full capacity - meaning not all channels are already in use.
When all pH-probe have passed the first calibration, they are flushed with demineralised
water, and then added to the other calibration buffer where the process is repeated. pH
buffer number one is pH 4,00 and the second is pH 7,00. In some cases, a third buffer is
used (pH 2,00), however, this is only in the research and development department.

3
Methodology
3.1
Design Method
Designing complex products requires a structured approach, particularly when multiple
subsystems and disciplines are involved. Given the scope of this project, and the com-
plexity of the process being automated, a structured design process was necessary to
manage both progress and integration across technical domains.
To support this, the design work was organized into four iterative sprints, each aligned with
the phased methodology proposed by Ulrich and Eppinger in their product development
framework [2]. As shown in Figure 3.1, the framework outlines six main phases: planning,
concept development, system-level design, detail design, testing and refinement, and
production ramp-up. The first phase, planning, sets the foundation for the process, while
the remaining phases gradually move the design toward a complete and manufacturable
product. Since this project is limited to a prototype-level outcome, the final phase related
to manufacturing ramp-up is excluded from the scope.
Figure 3.1: Ulrich and Eppinger’s six-phase product development process [2].
To fit the limited project duration, the design process was divided into four focused sprints.
Each sprint incrementally increased the design scope by addressing additional subsys-
tems (defined in Section 6.1), allowing for early validation of partial solutions while en-
abling continuous iteration. The first sprint targeted only a select few core subsystems,
while later sprints progressively built on this foundation, both in technical depth and in-
tegration, ultimately moving the design closer to a cohesive system architecture. This
staged approach not only mirrors the structure of Ulrich and Eppinger’s model, but also
reflects an agile, iterative mindset, which is essential for exploring trade-offs and reacting
to stakeholder feedback during development.
3.1.1
Structure of sprints
To get as much value as possible out of each sprint, a consistent set of tasks was assigned
across all sprints. These included a few tasks at the beginning and others at the end. At
the start of each sprint, the scope or goal was clarified to ensure a shared understanding
of what needed to be done and what was expected during that specific sprint. This helped
align the team and set a clear direction for the work.
Each sprint was then concluded with three main tasks. First, a sprint evaluation was
carried out to reflect on what worked well, what needed improvement, and what elements
should be carried over or changed in the next sprint. Second, the design specification was
reviewed and updated if necessary-based on new findings, test results, or realizations
made during the sprint. Finally, a short conclusion was written to summarize the progress
made, highlight key results, and document any challenges encountered. This structured
approach follows the iterative mindset described by Ulrich and Eppinger [2], and helps
ensure that each sprint contributes meaningfully to the development of the final system.
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification

==PAGE:20==
3.2
Prototyping
Prototyping is a crucial part of most product design, but prototyping comes in many vari-
ants. As described by Ulrich and Eppinger: ”[...] it includes such diverse forms of pro-
totypes as concept sketches, mathematical models, simulations, test components, and
fully functional preproduction versions of the product.” [2] With most of the technical ele-
ments being selected, we got closer to start making the system itself. This meant that we
attempted to make a functional prototype, with a goal of testing if our designs would func-
tion. Ulrich and Eppinger explain that prototyping can be classified along two dimensions.
The first dimension is the degree to which a prototype is physical or analytical. With phys-
ical prototypes as the name suggest being tangible products or components that can help
understand how it looks, feels, or functions. These are used to determine whether the
product has the right dimensions or surfaces or if it functions as expected. On the other
end of this dimension is the analytical prototypes. These on the other hand are non tangi-
ble and are often displayed as mathematical or visual, such as mathematical simulations,
computer drawing (in 2D and 3D) or equations encoded within a spreadsheet.
The other dimension is the degree to which a prototype is comprehensive as opposed to
focused. A comprehensive prototype, is a full-scale, fully operational version of the prod-
uct, these can be used to test if anything is missing, which can be done trough a user test.
Focused prototypes aims to explore (as the name suggest) more focused elements of the
design. Here the prototype is a single component, and by using a few focused prototypes
a better understanding of the overall performance of a product can be gathered.
For this project, the prototypes are in all ends of the prototyping dimensions were used.
With a goal of making a fully functional system, we would have to make both physical and
analytical prototypes, while exploring focused prototypes in an attempt to combine it all
into a comprehensive product that can be tested in the end. In the following chapters,
we will go trough the different design considerations while also describing our prototyp-
ing journey. As displayed in figure 3.2, Ulrich and Eppinger outlines how the different
combinations of the prototyping dimensions can be used for different things in the design
process. This aligns with how we used prototyping, with the main use being learning and
integration.
Figure 3.2: ”Appropriateness of different types of prototypes for different purposes” [2]

4
Stakeholder needs and design
specification
4.1
Identifying Customer Needs
The foundation of a successful design lies in understanding the users’ true needs. In this
project, customer needs were identified through close collaboration with stakeholders at
Novonesis, supplemented by internal analysis of laboratory workflows and technical con-
straints specific to dairy fermentation. The identified needs largely centre around enhanc-
ing process efficiency, ensuring compatibility with existing lab infrastructure, maintaining
affordability, and enabling broader accessibility. Overall, this should result in longer walk-
away time, allowing the lab technician to focus on other tasks.
4.1.1
Gathering Raw Data
Data was collected through informal interviews and hands-on observations with lab tech-
nicians and developers experienced in using the current pH monitoring system, such as
the iCinac (described in section 5.3.1). Particular attention was paid to physical workflows,
equipment handling, cleaning protocols, and software limitations. Additional insights were
gathered from requirement documents and internal presentations made available during
the early project stages. This approach reflects the first stage in identifying customer
needs as described by Ulrich and Eppinger [2].
4.1.2
Interpreting the Needs
The raw data was synthesized into structured, solution-neutral needs. For instance, com-
ments regarding calibration accuracy at room temperature evolved into a broader require-
ment: calibration should match the actual operating temperature of the experiment. Sim-
ilarly, technician feedback on cleaning routines translated into a need for a standardized,
repeatable method using ethanol and soft materials to clean pH-probe without causing
damage. This process of converting customer language into actionable, interpreted needs
aligns with the structured methodology outlined in the product development literature [2].
4.1.3
Organizing and Prioritizing Needs
Each interpreted need was formalized and rated on a 1-5 scale (where 5 is most im-
portant), based on its impact on user satisfaction, feasibility, and overall system perfor-
mance. Table 4.1 presents the resulting list of structured customer needs, including their
interpreted form and associated importance score. This prioritization is consistent with
best practices for focusing development efforts on the most critical aspects of customer
satisfaction [2].

4.1.4
Reflections on the Process
While customer feedback was gathered from a focused group within Novonesis, the re-
sulting needs reflect broader industry challenges commonly faced in laboratory pH moni-
toring. Interpreting customer statements into generalized, actionable needs helped avoid
jumping to premature solutions. Additionally, the prioritization step provided a clear foun-
dation for focusing subsequent concept generation and system architecture efforts on the
most impactful elements [2].

4.2
Preliminary Design Specification
The preliminary design specification (PDS) is an evolving document that outlines the key
technical and functional expectations of the system based on customer needs and early
feasibility considerations. As emphasized by Ulrich and Eppinger, the PDS is rarely static
and is expected to be refined iteratively throughout the development process [2]. As the
design specification aims to specify as many requirements early in the design process, it
often results in requirements that are not all feasible to include in the final product, this
is especially true for more complex products or systems where multiple elements must
function together.
In this project, the PDS will be reviewed and updated at the conclusion of each develop-
ment sprint to reflect new insights, validated assumptions, and changing priorities. This
ensures alignment with both stakeholder expectations and the realities uncovered during
prototyping and testing.
Table 4.2 presents the initial version of the design specification based on current knowl-
edge and interpreted customer needs. While it provides a foundational guide for develop-
ment, several aspects - particularly performance tolerances, manufacturability, and user
interface features - are subject to change as design sprints progress. In the table, the
specifications are described by a series of elements. In the first column, they are assigned
a number, in the second, their relation to the needs found in 4.1 are listed, if multiple are
listed, it means that the specification is based on multiple needs. The next column de-
scribes the metric, following the method of Ulrich and Eppinger, then the specifications
are evaluated based on their importance (with 5 being most important), these are based
on a qualitative assessments. The units describe how the metrics are measured, whether
it is in ml, hours or a qualitative assessment. The marginal column describe the minimum
requirement for success whereas the ideal column describes exactly that, namely what
the ideal result for the metric is.

4.2.1
Looking Ahead
As Ulrich and Eppinger emphasize, the design specification is not a final document at this
stage of development - it is a tool that evolves alongside the product [2]. Throughout the
project, especially during concept selection, prototyping, and testing phases, this specifi-
cation will be used both as a reference point and as a framework for evaluating trade-offs
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
13
==PAGE:24==
and performance.
To maintain this dynamic connection between design and specification, the PDS will sys-
tematically be revisited at the end of each sprint. This ensures that the design continues
to meet high-priority customer needs while adapting to new technical insights and imple-
mentation realities.
The specifications are also expected to evolve as further stakeholder meetings are con-
ducted. As new user feedback is gathered and additional needs are clarified or re-prioritized,
corresponding updates will be made to the specifications ensuring that the system re-
mains aligned with practical use cases, workflows, and expectations from both technical
staff and end-users.
This iterative approach not only increases confidence in decision-making but also sup-
ports transparent communication with stakeholders, ensuring that the final system is both
technically robust and strongly aligned with user expectations.

5
Research
5.1
ISO standard 26323
When working with the acidification of dairy cultures, the ISO standard 26323 is used for
reference. This, as with many other standards, has been developed to ensure a common
way of working with the subject of Determination of the acidification activity of dairy cul-
tures by continuous pH measurement [3]. ISO 26323 allows the use of two different milks
when working with starter cultures: A-milk 9,5 and B-milk 9,5. The naming here implies
the types. A-milk is autoclaved milk (heated to 115° Celsius) with a 9,5% mass fraction
dry matter and B-milk is boiled milk (heated to 99° Celsius)with a 9,5% mass fraction dry
matter. With these types of milk it is important to know that with B-milk all enzymes in the
cultures might not have been made inactive, and in these cases, the cultures should be
tested with A-milk.
5.1.1
Apparatus
The ISO standard also describes the equipment needed to perform the tests. Most of the
equipment is standard in laboratories, however, it should be noted that there is certain re-
quirement for some of the equipment that need to be complied with. A full list of apparatus
and their requirements are listed below.
• Analytical balances, capable of weighing to the nearest 0,01 g and 1 mg, respec-
tively.
• Autoclave, capable of operating at 99°C ±1°C and 115°C ±1°C.
• Water baths, capable of being maintained at 21°C ±1°C, 30,0°C ±0,2°C, 37,0°C
±0,2°C, 40,0°C ±0,2°C and 43,0°C ±0,2°C, under thermostat control.
• pH electrodes, suitable for measuring the required pH; e.g. Mettler Toledo 4) 405-
DPAS-SC-K8S/150 or equivalent electrodes.
• Cylindrical bottles, capacity 250 ml, of height 16,5 cm and internal diameter 5,5 cm.
• Temperature pH-probes, calibration of accuracy ±0,1°C.
• Data logger, equipped with pH and temperature channels; connected to a computer
capable of logging data from pH and temperature pH-probe.
5.2
pH - pondus Hydrogenii
pH, an abbreviation of the Latin term ‘pondus hydrogenii’, is a measure of the hydrogen
ion concentration in a water-based solution and represents the degree of that solution’s
acidity or alkalinity [4]. Acids mixed with water create an ”acidic” solution with a high
concentration of hydrogen ions. Alkalies mixed with water create ”base” solutions with
a high concentration of hydroxyl ions. The pH of a solution is defined by the Sørensen
equation as the negative logarithm of the concentration of hydrogen ions (H+).
pH = −log10[H+]
(5.1)
The pH-level is defined by a scale of 1-14 at a room temperature of 25°C, which is known
as the thermodynamic pH scale. Acidic solutions have a pH < 7, alkaline solutions have
a pH > 7, and neutral solutions have a pH = 7. The temperature of a solution can affect
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
15
==PAGE:26==
the dissociation of hydrogen and hydroxyl ions, which is important in the acidification of
dairy products, as different bacteria operate best at different temperatures. Therefore, the
pH probes also need to be calibrated at the same temperature at which the final product
will be produced. If not taken into account, this can lead to a discrepancy between a pH
reading taken under laboratory conditions and one taken at a process site.
5.2.1
pH-probes
A pH-probe consists of two electrodes, which is combined into a single probe. This is
called a combination probe, which is also the most common type. They can be made of
either glass or plastic. One of the electrodes is inside of the probe resting in a constant
pH solution (a buffer) and a conductor, plus a reference electrode containing a reference
electrolyte and a conductor to complete the circuit. Within the probe assembly there is
also often located a thermistor, so temperature compensation can easily be taken into
account. There is an added advantage of the combination probe, since the reference and
pH-probe will always be at the same temperature.
When the probe is used, there is generated an electrical potential in the sample being
tested, that is then measured by an receiving instrument for temperature correction and
amplification.
Figure 5.1: Different parts of a combination probe
5.2.2
Calibration and maintenance
The measured values may be scattered with the increase of the age of the pH electrode.
This does not always mean that there is a fault somewhere - even if the measured media
is stable. The electrodes performance can be determined by measuring an appropriate
pH buffer. If the measurement is within the required specification, recalibration of the
sensor/amplifier is unnecessary. Dirty or aggressive fluids can in certain cases mean that
very short recalibration intervals are needed.
As the buffer solution will absorb CO2 from the atmosphere over time, resulting in in-
creased measurement uncertainty, the fluid should not be stored for more than a few
days once the container has been opened for use. When purchasing buffer solutions, the
quoted ‘Uncertainty’ should be checked against the Standard required for the application
and a Certificate of Calibration obtained [4].
5.3
Market Research
To understand the need for a new solution, it is first necessary to understand the existing
solutions currently available on the market. Today, multiple products are available, and
each has its benefits. Understanding these benefits will allow us to better understand the
most important features and also give us a better foundation for doing the user research,
as we will better understand what options they might select when choosing a system.

5.3.1
iCinac
The iCinac system by KPM Analytics is specifically designed for monitoring the acidifica-
tion activity of lactic acid bacteria (L.A.B.) in dairy fermentation [5]. It can measure pH,
temperature, and oxidation-reduction potential (ORP) in real-time, making it particularly
useful for quality control (QC) and research applications in yogurt, cheese, and probiotic
development. The system supports up to 32 channels wired and 16 channels wireless (if
extension modules are bought), allowing for multiple simultaneous measurements across
different fermentation samples. It provides continuous data logging, ensuring precise
tracking of bacterial acidification. The automation level is limited, requiring manual inter-
vention for cleaning and calibration. Despite these limitations, iCinac remains an industry-
standard tool for high-throughput fermentation monitoring due to its accuracy, scalability,
and established reliability in the dairy sector.
Figure 5.2: KPM Analysis iCinac [6]
5.3.2
Mettler Toledo M800
The Mettler Toledo M800 [7] is a versatile, multi-parameter transmitter that supports pH,
dissolved oxygen (DO), and conductivity measurements, making it suitable for a wide
range of applications, including fermentation, water treatment, and industrial bioprocess-
ing. It offers models with 1, 2, or 4 channels, allowing users to measure multiple pa-
rameters simultaneously. One of its key strengths is its advanced communication capa-
bilities, supporting Ethernet/IP, Profinet, and Profibus DP, enabling seamless integration
with industrial automation and SCADA systems. The M800 features Intelligent Sensor
Management (ISM) technology, which provides predictive diagnostics to minimize sensor
maintenance and downtime. Additionally, it includes data logging functionality to track
historical trends and optimize process conditions. While it is highly capable, it may be
overly complex for simple laboratory setups.

Endress+Hauser Memobase Plus CYZ71D
The Memobase Plus CYZ71D from Endress+Hauser [9] is a multi-parameter software
and hardware system for managing digital Memosens sensors. It enables laboratories
to conduct pH, ORP, conductivity, and dissolved oxygen measurements with high trace-
ability and data integrity, making it particularly useful in regulated environments such as
pharmaceutical, biotech, and food industries. It supports up to 4 channels and connects
via USB, allowing users to use their computer as a measurement station. A major advan-
tage is its automated sensor calibration and documentation, reducing errors and ensuring
compliance with quality standards like ISO and GMP. However, it is not a stand-alone
transmitter, meaning it relies on external software for full functionality. While it is excel-

frequent pH measurements, such as biotech research, chemical synthesis, and quality
control. It is specifically designed for automated workflows, reducing the need for manual
intervention. However, it can only make one measurement at a time and cannot make con-
tinues measurements where the sample needs to be sealed during the test. The system
features real-time data logging and automated electrode calibration, ensuring long-term
stability and reproducibility. Its primary advantage is its ability to integrate into robotic lab
environments, making it ideal for laboratories that require repeatable, high-accuracy pH
measurements with minimal human interaction.

Precision Fermentation BrewIQ Fermentation Hub
The BrewIQ Fermentation Hub by Precision Fermentation [12] is a real-time fermentation

The Hamilton Microlab ApH Module [13] is a compact, on-deck pH measurement add-on
designed for seamless integration with Hamilton’s STARline automated liquid-handling
workstations. It supports up to four parallel pH-probes, covering a pH range of 2–12 with
an accuracy better than ±0,1 pH. The system is designed to fit standard ANSI/SLAS plate
formats, includes dedicated washing and drying stations, and is capable of completing
a 96-well plate run in under 25 minutes. Powered by an internal battery, it allows for
over 10 hours of continuous operation per charge. Communication is handled via Blue-
tooth, with data integration managed through Hamilton’s VENUS software. Calibration
and measurements are carried out using user-replaceable pH-probe. Fully compatible
with Hamilton’s automated platforms, the ApH Module is ideal for high-throughput work-
flows such as screening, fermentation process development, or any application requiring
precise inline pH profiling.

integration. In contrast, the Mettler Toledo M800 and Endress+Hauser Memobase sys-
tems support multiple digital protocols (Ethernet/IP, USB, Profinet, etc.), enabling seam-

such as dissolved oxygen (DO) and conductivity, making them well-suited for broader lab
and industrial applications.

bility, likely tailored for automated lab setups. The BrewIQ Fermentation Hub stands out

need for a custom solution tailored specifically to the constraints and needs identified in
this development effort.
5.4
Principles of linear motion
To be able to control the position of the pH-probe within the system, it is important to anal-
yse relevant methods of motion. Again, inspiration is taken from the world of machining
and 3D printing.
5.4.1
Lead screw
A lead screw converts rotational motion into linear movement using a threaded shaft and
a matching nut. When the screw rotates, the nut moves along its axis, allowing precise
control over positioning. Lead screws are often used in CNC machines, laboratory au-
tomation, and positioning systems where high accuracy is required. There are variations
like ball screws, which incorporate ball bearings between the threads to reduce friction and
improve efficiency. Standard lead screws, however, rely on direct metal-to-metal contact,
which increases friction but provides better self-locking properties, preventing unintended
movement when power is lost. While lead screws offer high precision and load-handling
capability, they are typically slower than belt-driven systems and require lubrication to
maintain efficiency over time. Their mechanical advantage makes them suitable for ap-
plications needing controlled, stable movement, but their efficiency is lower compared to
other methods due to frictional losses. (Reference worksheet A.8).
20
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
==PAGE:31==
5.4.2
Belt Driven
A belt-driven system uses a continuous belt looped around pulleys to transmit motion.
When a motor-driven pulley rotates, the belt moves, translating rotational energy into lin-
ear motion. This mechanism is common in automation, conveyor belts, and 3D printers
due to its ability to provide smooth and fast movement. The main advantage of belt-
driven systems is their high speed and minimal backlash, making them ideal for appli-
cations requiring rapid and quiet operation. However, they may experience stretching
over time, reducing positional accuracy unless tensioners are used. Compared to lead
screws, belt-driven systems offer higher speeds but lower precision, making them more
suitable for applications where speed is prioritized over extreme accuracy. Additionally,
their lightweight and flexible nature allow them to be used in large-scale motion systems
with minimal mechanical complexity. (Reference worksheet A.9).
5.4.3
Rack and pinion system
A rack and pinion system converts rotational motion into linear displacement through a
toothed gear (pinion) that engages with a linear gear strip (rack). As the pinion rotates, its
teeth mesh with the rack, causing it to move in a straight line. This mechanism is widely
used in industrial robotics, steering systems, and machine automation due to its ability to
handle heavy loads with high efficiency. Unlike belt-driven systems, rack and pinion se-
tups provide direct mechanical engagement, ensuring precise motion with minimal energy
loss. The system can achieve high-speed movements while maintaining durability, mak-
ing it suitable for both lightweight and heavy-duty applications. However, regular mainte-
nance, including lubrication, is needed to prevent wear and reduce backlash, where small
gaps between the gear teeth can cause unintended play in the movement. (Reference
worksheet A.10).
5.4.4
Pneumatic actuator
A pneumatic actuator uses compressed air to generate force and motion. When air pres-
sure is applied to one side of a piston inside a sealed cylinder, it moves in a linear di-
rection, producing mechanical motion. Pneumatic systems are widely used in industrial
automation, robotics, and laboratory equipment due to their fast response time and sim-
plicity. Since air is compressible, pneumatic actuators are highly responsive and can
operate at high speeds. However, they have lower force output compared to hydraulic
systems, making them less suitable for applications requiring extreme power. These sys-
tems require an air compressor and pressure regulation, adding to the overall complexity
of the setup. While pneumatics are reliable and low-maintenance, they lack precise posi-
tion control, making them ideal for tasks that require simple on/off movement rather than
highly controlled positioning. (Reference worksheet A.11).
5.4.5
Magnetic actuation
A magnetic actuation system relies on magnetic fields to generate motion without direct
mechanical contact. This can be achieved through electromagnetic fields, permanent
magnets, or a combination of both. Magnetic actuation is widely used in applications re-
quiring clean, wear-free movement, such as laboratory automation, robotic grippers, and
maglev transportation. Unlike traditional mechanical systems, magnetic actuators elimi-
nate friction and reduce maintenance needs. Some systems use linear magnetic tracks
where a moving magnet follows a controlled electromagnetic field, enabling smooth and
precise motion. While offering excellent speed and longevity, magnetic actuation is lim-
ited by the strength of the magnetic field, which decreases with distance. Additionally,
these systems require precise electronic control to manage the interaction between mag-
netic fields and moving components. Despite these challenges, magnetic actuation is
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
21
==PAGE:32==
an innovative solution for applications requiring non-contact motion with minimal wear.
(Reference worksheet A.12).
5.4.6
linear motor
A linear motor, also known as a direct drive motor, transforms electrical energy into linear
motion without the need for mechanical conversion mechanisms such as belts or gears.
It functions like a traditional electric motor that has been ”unrolled,” with a stationary stator
and a moving coil or magnet. When electrical current flows through the stator windings,
it generates a traveling electromagnetic field that directly moves the forcer along a track.
This method provides extremely fast and precise motion, making linear motors a pre-
ferred choice in semiconductor manufacturing, CNC machines, and high-speed automa-
tion systems. The absence of mechanical transmission components eliminates backlash
and wear, increasing efficiency and reliability. However, linear motors require advanced
electronic controls and can be more expensive than other motion solutions. Their energy
consumption is also higher, but their unmatched precision and smoothness make them
ideal for applications where speed and accuracy are critical. (Reference worksheet A.13).
5.4.7
Conclusion on Motion Principles
With the new knowledge on the different motion principles, we decided that it would be
beneficial to investigate which principles where used in the industry.
5.4.8
Field research
None of these principles are new, and many have been around for decades or even cen-
turies. There are benefits and disadvantages to using most of them, we therefore decided
that it would be beneficial to investigate which principles where used in the industry. At
DALSA a series of machines already implement many of these principles. These are
designed to afford high precession, and their mechanical principles are therefore highly
relevant for our system. DALSA has multiple automated liquid handling systems, among
them are Hamilton Microlab Star and Tecan Freedom EVO. These systems move in simi-
lar ways to what we are trying to achieve. Please refer to section 7.4 for more information
on the discoveries.

6
Defining the system
6.1
Functions and Sub-functions
To meet both stakeholder requirements and the ISO standard, the necessary functions
must be clearly defined. This step guarantees that all essential functions are part of the
final system (refer to work sheet A.6).
To ensure completeness and guide the design process, these high-level functions were
further broken down into sub-functions using a function-means tree (see worksheet A.5).
This method provided a structured way to explore alternative solutions and clarify how
each function would be realized in the final system.
The following functions were identified as critical for the system components that interface
with the pH-probes. It is important to note that this excludes functions such as motion
and data collection, and instead focuses on those directly involved with manual pH-probe
interaction.
6.1.1
Storage
Most glass pH-probes must be stored in a salt solution to ensure they do not dry out.
This solution is typically potassium chloride (KCl). As a result, the system must include a
storage function that keeps the pH-probes properly immersed when not in use to maintain
their hydration and functionality.
6.1.2
Cleaning
As part of the process, the pH-probes are cleaned multiple times (see Appendix A.4). It is
therefore crucial to implement a reliable cleaning method for the pH-probes. This is not a
straightforward task. As described in the process flow, the pH-probes are flushed rather
than simply dipped in water, and they are cleaned using more than one solution-typically
both water and ethanol. In some cases, the pH-probes may also be wiped to ensure that
any milk residue or potential biofilm build-up is fully removed. As a result, multiple factors
must be considered when designing this function for integration into the final system.
6.1.3
Calibration
As previously mentioned the pH-probes must be calibrated before use. This is to ensure
that they measure correctly. At Novonesis the pH-probes are typically calibrated at pH
4,00 and at pH 7,00. In some cases a third calibration buffer is used. The system must
be able to calibrate based on at least two different pH buffers. It would be beneficial if
the system could handle 3 buffers, however, this is not a hard requirement. The system
must be able to heat up the buffer to the experiment temperature, to ensure that the pH-
probe are calibrated to measure correctly. Additionally, the system must ensure consistent
temperature in all samples, resulting in an even and uniform heating of the milk.
6.1.4
Test Area
When the pH-probes are cleaned and calibrated, it is time to start the experiment. The
test area must be able to position the milk containers, making it easier for the system to
insert the pH-probes. During the experiment, the milk needs to be heated to a certain
temperature, and maintain it until the test is done. In some cases the temperature varies
during the experiment, this should therefore also be possible.
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
25
==PAGE:36==
6.2
Defining the Coordinate System
Moving away from the pH-probe and toward a broader view of the system, the impor-
tance of movement becomes obvious. Since the system operates across multiple axes,
it is essential to first define the coordinate system being used. This ensures a shared un-
derstanding as the design process progresses and helps avoid confusion when referring
to direction and motion.
In this project, the coordinate system used aligns with conventions from industrial equip-
ment such as CNC machines and 3D printers [14]. In these systems, the X- and Y-axes
define the horizontal plane, while the Z-axis represents vertical movement. This differs
from some mathematical contexts, where the vertical axis is occasionally represented as
Y, and the depth axis as Z.
For this project, the industrial convention was chosen, as it better aligns with the expec-
tations and practices of individuals with a background in engineering or manufacturing.
Figure 6.1: The selected coordinate system
6.3
System size and testing capabilities
Before the system design begins, it is important to define a preliminary system design size
and also decide on how many samples the system should be able to handle. Currently
the setup at Novonesis typically handles between 18 and 24 samples at once. This is a
per-system number, and Novonesis have multiple systems. Based on this information it
was decided that 24 should be the sample size for our system. This would allow the new
system to run the same maximum amount of samples as the existing one. Based on this
decision the design specification was updated - note that the ideal amount of samples is
much higher than 24, this is because in an ideal world the system can handle multiple
sets of samples, this will be discussed further in section 6.4.
Looking into preliminary dimensions, inspiration was drawn from the Hamilton Microlab
Star. This is a piece of equipment that is used at Novonesis. They have multiple of
these models of liquid handlers. This model comes in multiple configurations, some add
volume to the system, while others are integrated into the standard system volume. The
Microlab Star is 1664mm x 903mm x 781mm (WxHxD) in its base configuration [15].
Based on this, it was decided that the new system should have similar dimensions in
depth and height allowing it to fit next to the Microlab Star in the lab without sticking out.
The preliminary design specification for the dimensions was therefore defined as 800mm
x 800mm x 900mm (WxHxD).

6.4
System Automation Levels
Based on the criteria found in the research, we decided to brainstorm on different levels of
system automation. Here we wanted to see how different levels would look like going from
the current manual setup and process to a fully automated system. We ended up with 10
levels that gradually increased in automation level and decreased in human interaction.
The reason behind this was to understand what level would be the best to work towards.
By understanding the levels, it became easier to decide what level our solution should
function at. The levels are listed below. At the moment of writing, no standard or definition
of automation levels exist within the area of laboratory equipment, hence the reason we
created our own.
• Level 1 - Fully Manual
In this initial stage, all procedures are performed manually. Each probe is handled
individually by the lab technician, with no tools or automation to support the process.
• Level 2 - Manual with Probe Frame
The process remains manual, but the pH-probe are now mounted on a frame, allow-
ing the laboratory technician to manage all pH-probe simultaneously. This improves
efficiency and consistency compared to handling individual pH-probe.
• Level 3 - Manual with Frame and Support Holder
Building on the previous level, this stage introduces a support holder for the frame.
This addition improves ergonomics, reduces strain on the technician, and facilitates
more stable handling during use.
• Level 4 - Frame on Rail System
The probe frame is mounted on a rail system, enabling smooth movement between
workstations without the need for manual lifting. This setup further reduces handling
effort and enhances workflow efficiency.
• Level 5 - Semi-Automated Cleaning and Calibration Station
An automated cleaning and calibration station is introduced. When the frame is
docked, the system performs washing and calibration routines. The technician still
handles frame transfer via the rail system.
• Level 6 - Motorized Transfer System
The rail system is now motorized, allowing the frame to be automatically moved be-
tween predefined stations. Manual involvement is limited to initiating and monitoring
the process.
• Level 7 - Motorized with Data Acquisition
In addition to motorized movement, this level includes integrated data collection.
The pH-probe are connected to a system that simultaneously manages motion con-
trol and logs measurement data in real time.
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
27
==PAGE:38==
• Level 8 - Fully Integrated Single-Rack System
A complete stand-alone unit that combines automated cleaning, calibration, temper-
ature control (e.g., water bath), and data acquisition. It is designed to handle one
rack autonomously through the entire test cycle.
• Level 9 - Fully Integrated Multi-Rack System
An expanded version of the previous level, this system can process multiple probe
racks concurrently. It allows for parallel experiments with varied conditions, maxi-
mizing flexibility and throughput.
• Level 10 - Fully Autonomous System
This highest level of automation includes complete preparation and clean-up. The
system automatically fills test bottles, adds cultures, collects data, and empties bot-
tles post-test. The only human input required is uploading the test protocol.
To help decide on the best level a weighted matrix was created. The weights reflect the
relative importance of each criterion in evaluating the system design levels. For example,
criteria like Level of Automation and Feasibility are given higher weights because they
significantly impact efficiency and implementation practicality. Weights are assigned so
that their total adds up to 1,0, ensuring a balanced and normalized evaluation. Each sys-
tem level (from Level 1 to Level 10) is rated on a scale from 0 to 5 for each criterion, where
0 means the feature is not present at all, and 5 indicates excellent performance. These
scores are then multiplied by the corresponding weights, and the results are summed to
compute a Total Score for each level. This allows for an objective comparison of how well
each system configuration meets the defined evaluation criteria.
• Ergonomics
One of the main concerns from the stakeholders at Novonesis was Ergonomics.
Ergonomics was one of the areas of the current process that did not lead to any
challenges, this is also described in table 4.1. Because of this it is critical to take
into consideration when making the assessment. It has been given the weight 0,10,
as ergonomics are important but less than other areas.
• pH-probes
When creating the new solution, it is important to consider how many pH-probes can
be handled at once. The amount of pH-probes directly correlates with the sample
size and is therefore worth considering. It has also been given a weight of 0,10 as
it is important but not crucial.
• Automation The whole reason behind this project is to add automation to the pro-
cess, thus this criteria needs to be a part of the evaluation, for this reason, it has
also been given the weight of 0,20, as this is the most critical criterion. A higher
score is given to a level that has high levels of automation, and vice versa.
• Complexity
Complexity refers to how mechanically, electronically, and operationally complicated
the solution is. While more advanced systems tend to offer greater functionality,
they are also more difficult to develop, maintain, and troubleshoot-especially within
limited project timelines. A highly complex system may reduce feasibility despite
offering higher automation. Complexity has been given a weight of 0,10, reflecting
that while it impacts implementation, it must be balanced with functionality.
• Feasibility
This criterion evaluates how realistically a solution can be implemented within the
28
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
==PAGE:39==
project’s scope, considering available resources, time, and technical capabilities.
A highly feasible solution is more likely to be completed and function as intended.
Given the time-constrained nature of the project, feasibility has been assigned a
relatively high weight of 0,15 to ensure the selected system level is actually deliver-
able.
• Walk-away time
Walk-away time refers to how long the system can operate independently without
human interaction. Reducing manual supervision increases efficiency and frees up
laboratory technicians for other tasks. This is especially valuable in high-throughput
environments. As an important enabler of hands-off operation, this criterion has
been given a weight of 0,10.
• Data collection
Data handling and recording are crucial for both research and quality control. A
system that includes integrated data collection can automatically log pH values and
timestamps, reducing errors and improving traceability. Since this feature adds sig-
nificant value in terms of compliance and efficiency, it has been assigned a weight
of 0,10.
• Ease of cleaning
Ease of cleaning directly affects usability and reliability. In a dairy environment,
ensuring the pH-probe are free of milk residue is essential to avoid contamination
and preserve measurement accuracy. A system that simplifies this process will
reduce cleaning time and improve reproducibility. This criterion has a weight of
0,10.
• Ease of use
The system must be user-friendly for lab technicians. If the solution is too complex
or unintuitive, it may not be used effectively-regardless of its technical capabilities.
This includes interface design, handling, and physical interactions. While important,
it has been given a slightly lower weight of 0,05 due to its subjective nature and the
assumption that proper training will be provided.
Based on the scores presented in Table 6.2, it is clear that the highest-ranking au-
tomation levels, particularly Levels 9 and 10, offer the strongest overall performance
across the majority of evaluation criteria. However, both levels score significantly
lower in terms of complexity and feasibility, highlighting the considerable engineer-
ing and integration challenges they present. While these levels represent desirable
long-term targets for system development, their expanded scope and system size,
especially when compared to Level 8, make them impractical within the constraints
of the current project’s time frame and resources.
Level 8, by contrast, strikes a strong balance between functionality, automation,
and implementation feasibility. It delivers high performance in key areas such as
cleaning, calibration, temperature control, and data handling, while maintaining a
more manageable system scale. As such, the focus of this project will be to design
and develop a system aligned with the Level 8 specification. Higher levels of au-
tomation should remain a strategic objective for future development, building on the
foundation established by this phase.
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
29
==PAGE:40==
6.5
Conclusion
This chapter has laid the foundational framework for the system’s functional defini-
tion, motion principles, and automation architecture. Beginning with the identifica-
tion of core subfunctions, storage, cleaning, calibration, and testing, the system’s
most critical probe-related tasks were clearly mapped. These areas form the back-
bone of the physical interaction design and ensure the reliability of pH measure-
ments throughout the fermentation process.
To support precision and repeatability in probe positioning, multiple motion prin-
ciples were analyzed, drawing on solutions from machining and lab automation.
Field research confirmed the viability of combining mechanical principles, such as
lead screws and belt drives, as seen in commercially available automated systems.
These observations serve as validation for a hybrid motion design tailored to this
application.
In defining a standard coordinate system, inspired by CNC and 3D printer conven-
tions, the development team ensures a shared understanding of movement across
axes-critical for coherent system design.
The exploration of ten automation levels enabled a structured evaluation of system
architectures with varying degrees of complexity and autonomy. A weighted deci-
sion matrix helped assess each level across multiple criteria, including ergonomics,
feasibility, data integration, and ease of cleaning. Although Levels 9 and 10 achieved
the highest overall scores, they also involve significantly more complexity and are
less feasible within the scope of this project.
Their enhanced functionality and
broader system architecture make them strong candidates for future product de-
velopment, but not viable within the project’s current time frame.
Level 8 emerged as the optimal target for this phase. It balances high capability with
manageable complexity, offering automated cleaning, calibration, and temperature
control, as well as integrated data collection. Therefore, the goal of this project is to
design a system corresponding to Level 8, providing a scalable foundation for future
development toward full automation.
This chapter forms a clear transition between system definition and concept genera-
tion. The structured breakdown of functions, motion options, and automation levels
will directly inform the development of initial design concepts and selection criteria
in the following phase.

7
Designing the system - Sprint 1
Based on the discoveries in the initial research and discussions with Novonesis,
the main stakeholder, a series of main functions were defined in chapter 6. The
goal for Sprint 1 was therefore to begin understanding how these functions could be
shaped. With information gathered around different movement principles, cleaning
and calibration processes, and different automation levels, we started creating the
first concepts.
7.1
Scope of the sprint
Sprint 1 served as the foundation for the physical system design, building on the
early research and stakeholder input. The main objective was to begin exploring
how the system’s core functions-such as calibration, cleaning, and movement-could
be realized. To support this, the following areas were addressed during the sprint:
– Identification and breakdown of core system functions (storage, cleaning, cali-
bration, and test area)
– On-site visit to Novonesis to validate assumptions and observe existing work-
flows
– Development of calibration subsystem
– Initial prototyping of the motion system, including Z-, X-, and Y-axis movement
– Integration and tuning of motor control
This sprint focused on laying the mechanical and conceptual groundwork needed
for further subsystem integration in the following development cycles.
7.2
Novonesis visit - Insights
To better understand the real-world challenges involved in pH measurement and
fermentation monitoring, we conducted a site visit to Novonesis. Seeing the labo-
ratory setup in person added essential context to the problem description we had
previously only read about.
At first glance, multiple large stainless-steel water baths were seen, each equipped
with digital Lauda thermostats, which were used to preheat and maintain precise
temperatures during the experiments (fig.7.1). These water baths contained up to 24
pH-probes inserted into individual milk bottles, forming a dense network of samples
and sensors.
One of the clearest takeaways from the visit was the sheer time intensity of the
process. Every probe needed to be individually calibrated, cleaned, and inserted,
which involved multiple repetitive manual steps. In total setup and cleaning of 24
samples took around 7.5 hours if they were efficient. Laboratory technicians used
the industry-standard iCinac system to monitor pH over time (fig. 7.1). While the
system is accurate and reliable, it offers little to no automation in the handling of the
pH-probes.
The workflow also included several bottlenecks:
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
33
==PAGE:44==
Figure 7.1: Water bath and pH-probes in samples under test
– Manual bottle opening: Each milk bottle was opened individually before the
test, which was time-consuming and ergonomically inefficient and exhausting.
– Cleaning challenges: After experiments, pH-probes required thorough clean-
ing. This involved rinsing with demineralised water, ethanol flushing, and finally
drying or wiping. In some cases, a microfibre cloth (e.g., Chicopee Veraclean
Polish Plus, that they used) was needed to remove residual protein biofilm
from the probe tips - suggesting the standard rinsing process was not always
sufficient.
– Cable management issues: The lab setup included dozens of cables running
between the pH-probes, temperature controllers, and measurement interfaces.
This introduced practical difficulties in keeping cables untangled and organized
(fig. 7.2), which could slow down the workflow and added risk of human error
that could lead to damage on the pH-probes.
Another thing we got told was the lack of water pressure when using handheld
squeeze bottles to clean the pH-probe. This likely contributed to insufficient clean-
ing, allowing milk proteins to accumulate and harden on the sensor surface. As a
result, pH-probes sometimes required manual scrubbing to restore proper function.
The visit confirmed many of the assumptions made earlier in our system analysis-
particularly the need for automation in cleaning, calibration, and probe handling. It
also highlighted a few new, unexpected challenges, such as the logistical complexity
caused by cable routing and bottle handling.
These insights have informed both our requirements and design decisions. In par-
ticular, they validated the prioritization of cleaning efficiency, probe repositioning
speed, and ergonomics in our preliminary design specification (see Table 7.3).
34
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
==PAGE:45==
Figure 7.2: One issues with the current setup is the long cables for the pH-probes tangling
together
7.3
Calibration
7.3.1
Initial Concept
In the process of designing the calibration system the following questions were cre-
ated:
– How many pH-probes should be calibrated at once?
– How does the pH buffer change when exposed to air over extended periods?
– Will the buffers contaminate each other if pH-probes are not cleaned well enough
or if buffer drips from the pH-probes?
The initial concept for the calibration system, was to use individual tubs for each pH
buffer. As all experiments are initiated with calibration in buffers with pH 4,00 and
pH 7,00, it was mandatory to have the option to calibrate in two buffers. As some
departments also use a third pH buffer for their calibration, we decided that it should
be possible to also calibrate in that pH buffer in the system.
There is multiple ways of performing the calibration. As mentioned in section 2.5.1,
the lab technician at Novonesis inserts each probe in a test tube with the first pH
buffer, runs the calibration, and then moves the probe to the next pH buffer. They
perform all calibrations at once, utilizing multiple test tubes. This makes sense as
the pH-probes needs to stabilize in each of the buffers, which typically takes 5-10
minutes per pH buffer. Based on this process, when discussing whether to calibrate
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
35
==PAGE:46==
the pH-probes individually, by row or all together at once, it was quickly agreed that
they should all be calibrated together at once. The reasoning behind this was:
1. All the pH-probes must be stabilized at the correct temperature before perform-
ing the calibration. This typically takes 5-10 minutes. It would therefore take
too long to do it individually or by row when using 24 pH-probes.
2. As the pH-probes can be damaged from drying out, it was feared that this could
happen if not all pH-probes were calibrated together as some of the pH-probes
would then not be submerged.
3. All pH-probes are calibrated using the same pH buffer, it therefore makes sense
to calibrate them at once, as there is no need to change the pH buffer between
calibration of the individual pH-probe.
7.3.2
Next Concept
During a supervision meeting, it was discussed whether it was possible to design the
solution to use less pH buffer liquid to perform the calibration. Initially, the thought
was to have three large tubs that could be filled with the pH buffer liquids. The
challenge with this idea was that it would require a large amount of the liquids and
it would also take up a lot of space in the system. The process of redesigning the
shape of the pH buffer tubs was then initiated.
Figure 7.3: New design for calibration tray, each colour illustrate a different liquid
After some brainstorming and discussing back and forth, we came up with a new
design. This new design combined all three calibration tubs into one, while also
incorporating the storage tub. As seen in figure 7.3 the new design has four lanes.
Each lane contains one of the four liquids (either a pH buffer or the KCl storage
liquid), which is then shared across a row of six pH-probes. To illustrate the different
liquid lanes, they have been assigned a different color in the figure. The green lane
is the KCl, the blue is the pH 4,00 buffer, the red is the pH 7,00 buffer and the
yellow, is then an extra lane, that can be utilized when the lab-techs want a third
buffer. An unexpected advantage of this design is a reduction is system footprint.
By combining the four tubs into one, the system’s overall dimensions are greatly
reduced. As seen in figure 7.4, by making this reduction, the system size can be
reduced to half the original size.
36
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
==PAGE:47==
Figure 7.4: New design reduces the footprint of the system. The blue squares illustrate
the calibration and storage tubs.
7.3.3
Contamination
With the new design for the calibration system, a new concern arose - will the dif-
ferent liquids used around the system contaminate the pH buffers if they drip into
them? As the buffers need to be pure to ensure correct measurement and calibra-
tion, it is important that they are not contaminated by other liquids that could change
the pH of the buffers. To test if this was a problem, we constructed an experiment.
In this experiment, we would make a set of samples using three different buffers,
pH 4,00, pH 7,00, and pH 10,00. These would then be contaminated/diluted with
demineralized water, milk, and ethanol, additionally we also had a control sample.
For the contaminants, we added 1ml to 4ml pH buffer. This gives us a 20% contam-
ination, which is much more than we expect to happen if the pH-probe have some
remaining liquid on them when inserted into the buffer. Having this high percentage
of contamination ensures that we test a worst-case scenario.

Analyzing the results, it is clear that there are some challenges with the pH 4,00
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
37
==PAGE:48==
buffer. When contaminated with ethanol or milk, the pH rose by 6,23 and 15,46
percent. The worst contaminant is milk, therefore, the cleaning station must remove
all milk remaining on the pH-probes after the experiment. With ethanol being a part
of the cleaning process, it is important that ethanol is not the last step, and water
must therefore be the last step. In all of the experiments, the demineralized water
created less than pH 0,1 increase in the pH of the buffer. In the test with pH 10,00
buffer, the ethanol and milk again made changes to the pH of the buffers. However,
here the pH had decreased. This occurs as the milk has a pH between 6,60 to 6,80
[16], because of this it raised the pH in the 4,00 buffer and decreased it in the 10,00
buffer.
Figure 7.5: Contamination test in labora-
tory
Figure 7.6: Evaporation test done in fume
cupboard
These experiments demonstrated the importance of thoroughly cleaning all compo-
nents after use. They also highlighted the need to account for potential contamina-
tion when designing and positioning sub-systems.
To address this, the cleaning stations should be placed between the test area and
the calibration area. This ensures that the pH-probes are cleaned before being
stored, rather than passing the storage area on the way to cleaning, which could
allow contaminants to drip into the buffer and storage area.
7.3.4
Evaporation
One thing is contamination of the buffers, and another is evaporation. An important
element to the calibration process is the liquids being heated to experiment tem-
perature, this ensures that they are calibrated based on the right temperature. But
what happens if a heated buffer is left out over longer periods of time? Will the liquid
evaporate and, by that, change the pH of the buffer?
Similarly to the other experiment, 5ml of liquid was used. Again three buffers was
used - pH 4,00, pH 7,00 and pH 10,00. It was poured into a petri dish, this was
used because of its larger surface area allowing more evaporation. Each dish was
weighed before and after the experiment, allowing us to see how much liquid had
evaporated.
The measured changes in the pH show that evaporation is not a problem. Across
all three buffers, the biggest change was with pH 4,00 where a 1% change was
recorded. Based on this we should not be that concerned about evaporation, and
an open calibration tray, should not create any issues. We still plan on pumping
out the liquid once the calibration process has concluded, to reduce evaporation but
also contamination.
