7.4
Inspiration from other machines
To avoid reinventing the wheel and to gain insight into how established systems are
built, we visited the DALSA laboratory to look at several other commercial laboratory
automation platforms. Among the machines we studied were the Tecan Freedom
EVO, Hamilton Microlab STAR and Tecan Fluent. These are professional-grade
liquid handling robots designed for high-precision tasks in clinical and research lab-
oratories.
Our main focus was to study the mechanics behind how these systems perform XYZ
movements - especially the way pipette heads are moved across the workspace.
We paid close attention to the mechanisms responsible for guiding and driving these
movements, noting several patterns and design decisions that informed our own
mechanical layout.
For long-axis movements in the XY plane, the machines typically used rack and pin-
ion systems (fig 7.7). This method likely provides increased stability and positioning
accuracy over extended travel distances, which is important when using stepper mo-
tors that rely on counting steps for tracking. To reduce mechanical complexity and
cost, only one side of the gantry was motor-driven, while the opposite side was sup-
ported by a free-spinning wheel mounted on a guide rail. A smart way to maintain
balance and prevent tilting without needing dual motor synchronization.
Figure 7.7: Rack and pinion option on X-
axis on Tecan Freedom Evo
Figure 7.8: Belt and pulley on Tecan Free-
dom Evo
For shorter XY movements, we observed the use of linear guide rails paired with
belt-driven pulley systems (fig. 7.8). This setup allows for fast, smooth, and quiet
operation, making it well-suited for horizontal motion where high speed and frequent
direction changes are required. Just as seen in modern 3D printers running Klipper
firmware.
For vertical motion in the Z-axis, lead screw mechanisms were the dominant choice
(fig. 7.9). Lead screws offer precise, controlled motion and are well-suited for lifting
pipette heads with consistent force. In some cases, particularly where individual
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
39
==PAGE:50==
pipette channels could move independently, we noticed that a rack and pinion sys-
tem was again used for vertical displacement (fig. 7.10).
In terms of actuation, the machines mostly used Maxon motors, known for their
high quality and precision. These Swiss-made motors come at a premium price
but are widely adopted in high-end automation systems. We also noted the use of
both traditional NEMA stepper motors, as well as more compact coin-style motors
probably due to space constraints. The use of NEMA steppers was particularly
familiar, as they are commonly found in 3D printers and other DIY motion control
setups - making them a practical reference for our own design.
Figure 7.9: Lead screw for Z-axis on Tecan
Freedom Evo
Figure 7.10: Rack and pinion on smaller on
Hamilton
Another interesting area was the fluid handling systems. These machines often em-
ployed positive displacement mechanisms, where small pistons moved back and
forth inside airtight cylinders to draw or expel liquid. This method provides high re-
peatability and fine control over fluid volume, making it ideal for automated pipetting.
While this was outside our immediate scope for Sprint 1, it remains a potential area
of interest for future iterations and automation levels.
By studying these commercial systems, we gained a valuable understanding of both
the mechanical and motor control strategies used in professional lab automation.
Many of the observed design elements directly inspired choices in our own prototype
- particularly in how we planned to build the motion system and select motor types.
7.5
Motor controller
To enable precise and automated movement within the system, a motor controller
is essential. The motor controller is responsible for coordinating the movements
of the different axes, ensuring that the pH-probes are correctly positioned during
calibration, cleaning, and measurement procedures. Without a motor controller, it
would be impossible to accurately control the positioning of the pH-probes, leading
to potential damage to both pH-probes and samples.
For the development of the system, we chose to repurpose a motor controller board
from a decommissioned Anet A8 3D printer. The decision to reuse an existing 3D
printer board was based on several factors:
– Availability and Cost-Effectiveness: Using a pre-existing board significantly re-
duces costs compared to purchasing a new industrial motor controller and per-
haps even a custom built one.
40
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
==PAGE:51==
– Integrated Motor Drivers: The 3D printer board already includes stepper motor
drivers capable of providing precise control of multiple motors, which is going to
be important for moving the probe assembly around accurately at the different
stations.
– Community Support and Documentation: The Anet A8 platform has been widely
used, meaning there is a large amount of community-driven documentation, tu-
torials, and firmware support available.
– Compact Size: The 3D printer board is relatively small and will hopefully be
easy to fit into the products control box later.
Even though the Anet A8 is an old printer we expect it to be good enough for the
proof of concept. It also means that we can get started with the prototyping almost
right away. By using the board, we could drive the stepper motors required for the
X, Y, and Z movements without needing custom motor electronics. This choice also
laid the groundwork for using open-source firmware (Marlin) and control software
(Pronterface), simplifying both the development and future troubleshooting of the
systemA.64.
7.5.1
3D printer board
The motor controller board from the Anet A8 3D printer is based on a typical 8-
bit ATmega1284P microcontroller architecture, similar to many other entry-level 3D
printer controllers. This board was originally designed to control multiple stepper
motors, heating elements, and sensors, making it a good choice for our needs.
Figure 7.11: Anet A8 controller board
The board features several integrated components and interfaces:
– Stepper Motor Drivers: The board includes onboard stepper motor drivers
for up to five motors (X, Y, Z1, Z2, and extruder). These drivers support mi-
crostepping, allowing for smoother and more precise motion. For our system,
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
41
==PAGE:52==
we will primarily be using three of these outputs: X, Y, and Z.
– Endstop Inputs: Each axis has dedicated endstop input, which are used to
define mechanical limits and ensure safe operation. Endstops are essential for
initializing the system’s ”home” position before movement begins.
– Heater and Thermistor Inputs: Originally intended for heated beds and ho-
tends in the 3D printer, these outputs and inputs are not directly required for our
application. However, we will later have a look at the output for the heatbed
and input for heatbed thermistor to see if we also can use these I/O for the
water bath, the milk is heated in.
– Serial Communication: The board communicates via a USB-to-serial bridge
using a CH340G chip, allowing easy connection to a computer or Raspberry
Pi for control and firmware updates. This UART communication is crucial for
interacting with the Marlin firmware and Pronterface.
– Power Input: The board operates on 12V DC power, which is standard for
most desktop systems, and will also lay the foundation for our system. This
voltage is used directly by the motors and internal logic circuits, simplifying
power system integration.
– Expansion Ports: A few additional GPIO pins are available for extensions
such as fans, LEDs, or custom sensors if the firmware is modified, giving the
system flexibility for future development if needed.
Overall, the board offers a balanced mix of simplicity and flexibility. At this time it
seems to be well-suited for our proof-of-concept stage, where rapid iteration, ease of
integration, and basic multi-axis control are prioritized over more advanced features
like closed-loop motor feedback [17].
7.5.2
Marlin
To control the 3D printer board, we chose to use the open-source Marlin firmware.
Marlin is a widely used firmware for 3D printers and CNC-like motion systems, of-
fering a large range of features, flexibility, and community support.
The firmware was flashed onto the Anet A8 board using the Arduino IDE. As a start-
ing point, the official Anet A8 configuration files provided by the Marlin project were
used. These template files define a basic set of settings that match the hardware
layout, pin-out, settings, etc. of the original 3D printer.
After flashing the initial firmware, a series of modifications were made to tailor Marlin
for the specific requirements of our system:
– Movement Dimensions: The default workspace dimensions were adjusted to
match the size of our machine, ensuring that all movements stay within safe
mechanical limits.
– Steps per Millimeter: The number of motor steps required per millimeter of
travel was recalculated and updated. This calibration ensures that physical
motion accurately matches commanded movements.
– Deactivation of Unused Functions: Features related to hotend temperature
control, extrusion, bed heating, and thermal safety were deactivated, as these
are not relevant to our application. Disabling unused features simplifies the
firmware and reduces the risk of unexpected faults.
42
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
==PAGE:53==
– Acceleration and Jerk Settings: Fine-tuning of the acceleration, decelera-
tion, and jerk parameters was performed to ensure that movement is smooth
and controlled. This not only improves the perceived robustness of the system
but also reduces mechanical stress and the risk of vibrations that could harm
the pH-probes.
The flashing and tuning process was iterative, requiring approximately five firmware
updates to achieve the desired motion behaviour. Each iteration involved small
adjustments based on observed movement quality until the robot achieved smooth
and reliable acceleration and deceleration across all axes.
Lessons Learned
During the firmware flashing and tuning process, several practical insights were
gained. Firstly, the importance of systematically adjusting only a few parameters
at a time became clear. In early iterations, multiple changes were made between
flashes, which made it difficult to isolate which modifications affected the system
behaviour. By shifting to a more methodical approach - changing only one or two
settings per iteration - it became much easier to understand the system’s response
and fine-tune it effectively.
Additionally, the smooth acceleration and deceleration was looked into. Even small
changes in some of the parameters in the acceleration profiles led to noticeable me-
chanical vibrations caused by hard stops, step loss, and a lack of perceived control
in the motion. Tuning these parameters slowly not only improved the performance
but also gave the system a much more professional, controlled and robust feel.
Lastly, although Arduino IDE provided an accessible and simple environment for
flashing the firmware, the limited compiler feedback and long upload times were mi-
nor bottlenecks. For future iterations or more complex firmware development, it may
be beneficial to explore alternative development environments such as PlatformIO,
which offer better integration with Marlin and faster development cycles [18], Klipper
controller or other products in the same category.
7.5.3
Pronterface
To control and interact with the motor controller during early prototyping, the program
Pronterface was used. Pronterface is part of the Printrun suite [19] of 3D printer host
software and provides a simple graphical user interface (GUI) to send commands
over a serial connection.
Using Pronterface simplified the process compared to writing and executing Python
scripts for each individual action. Since Pronterface automatically handles the open-
ing and closing of the serial port, it made it much faster to test movements and verify
hardware functionality without needing to program custom communication scripts.
One minor challenge encountered was that the Anet A8 board resets every time
the serial connection is opened. This is a built-in feature of the ATmega1284P mi-
crocontroller and standard USB-serial interfaces like the CH340G chip. It causes a
reboot of the firmware each time a Python script gets started and connects.
Pronterface proved to be very useful for verifying that each motor moved as ex-
pected. Movements could either be commanded by pressing dedicated buttons in
the GUI or by typing G-code commands manually into the command line interface.
This flexibility made it easy to experiment with different movements, test endstop
triggers, and diagnose motor configuration issues. This does not happen when
Pronterface is used, since it keeps connected to the printer all the time.
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
43
==PAGE:54==
Additionally, Pronterface allows the creation of macros - predefined sets of com-
mands that can be executed with a single click. This feature was particularly valu-
able during testing, allowing repetitive tasks such as moving motors back and forth
multiple times without needing to manually input commands between each move-
ment. By using macros, attention could remain focused on observing the mechani-
cal system rather than constantly interacting with the computer.
The program has provided a simple but highly effective tool for verifying the basic
motion capabilities of the system and will likely continue to be useful during early-
stage testing before a fully automated control system is developed.
7.6
Frame design
Most industrial systems use custom-designed frames and chassis, which, while ro-
bust, are typically costly to manufacture and offer limited flexibility once assembled.
This can significantly hinder iterative development processes where design changes
are frequent and rapid prototyping is essential.
To address this, the system will be constructed using modular aluminium extrusion
profiles from Bosch Rexroth.
These profiles follow standardized 20x20, 30x30,
40x40 and 45x45 mm formats, which are widely supported across industrial au-
tomation platforms. One of the main advantages of this approach is the flexibility
it provides: components can be repositioned, added, or replaced without welding,
allowing the system to evolve during prototyping and testing phases.
Figure 7.12: Bosch Rexroth profiles come in many variant and sizes
In addition to flexibility, Bosch Rexroth profiles offer high structural rigidity, compat-
ibility with a wide range of brackets and fasteners, and proven reliability in both lab
automation and manufacturing environments. The modular approach also enables
faster assembly and easier transport or reconfiguration of subsystems [? ].
In this project, it was decided to use 30x30 mm profiles (from here referred to as
3030). These offer an excellent balance between structural strength and compact-
ness. The 3030 format provides sufficient rigidity for mounting components such
as linear rails, probe holders, and electronic modules, while maintaining a small
44
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
==PAGE:55==
footprint-making it ideal for benchtop systems or confined lab spaces. Additionally,
3030 profiles are compatible with a wide range of standard Bosch Rexroth brackets,
connectors, and accessories, simplifying both initial assembly and future modifica-
tions.
7.7
Axis design
One of the most critical features of the system is movement. Movement can be split
into two areas, motor controlled, which was described in the previous section, the
other area is axis design. How is the axis constructed? There are many ways of
designing the axis and when doing so there are also many elements to consider.
The principles of linear motion were described in section 5.4, but stabilization and
part construction also need to be taken into consideration.
The first axis we started working on was the z-axis. As described in section 6.2 it
is the axis lifting the pH-probes up and down. Based on the linear motion principles
but also the Hamilton and Tecan liquid handlers, it was quickly decided to use a
lead screw as motion principle. Additionally, linear rods and bearing blocks was
chosen to stabilize the axis. These are common in the industry, easy to work with
and cheap, making them a good choice. To start understanding how the linear rods,
motor and lead screw should be placed in relation to each other, a simple mock-up
was made, see image 7.13. Based on mock-up the first prototype was designed.
Using 3D-printing, the prototype was made using to parts, on which the motor and
guide rods could be installed on the buttom one, and a top part on which the bearing
blocks was mounted together with the nut for lead screw, see image 7.14.
Figure 7.13: Initial outline for placement of
parts on the Z-axis
Figure 7.14:
First prototype based on
mock-up
Evaluating this concept quickly showed a series of flaws, which is the whole idea
behind prototyping. Firstly this design was constructed mostly to understand how
the different elements should move together. The bearing blocks could not be in-
stalled, because of how their threads are placed, and the whole design was made
in a way that did not allow it to be installed on other parts (see worksheet A.23. But
as mentioned, this was not the reason behind it. It was made to illustrate and test
motion. By doing so we were ready to move on to the next version. Here changes
was made to the system, allowing the bearing blocks to be installed, the bottom part
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
45
==PAGE:56==
was also redesigned to hold pH-probes, and the motor was moved to the top part
to reduce the load (see worksheet A.24).
Moving on from the second iteration, the next axis was included. The new iteration
was designed to also move on the X-axis. This meant an updated design of the top
part, with space for the bearing blocks and belt tensioning on the X-axis. Slowly, the
system began taking shape. The Z-axis remained the same for the rest of Sprint 1.
The fourth iteration introduced the Y-axis. The design from the third iteration was
installed on the Y-axis, this axis similarly to the Z-axis also used linear rods, with the
X-axis being designed to be belt driven.

7.8.1
Axis design
With the second version of the calibration tray, an idea started to develop. What if
only the calibration tray moved, not the entire probe set? With the updated design
for the calibration tray, the system was streamlined making everything fit in one
row. This meant that the system only needed X-axis movement when calibrating.
The idea was then to fix the probe-set in the X-axis and only moving the calibration
tray, this would move some weight lower down in the system, while also making it
virtually impossible to be foully positioned in the washing and testing areas - at least
in X-position. This idea should be tested and evaluated in the second sprint.
Based other products it was also decided that the Y-axis should be updated to use
a better and more precise type of rail. The best option needs to be selected and
ordered, so that it can be implemented in the next sprint. By upgrading to this type
of rail, the system should move more precisely with less play in the bearings.
After talks with the stakeholders at Novonesis, it was decided that an experiment
with different test container sizes should be conducted. The goal of this experiments
is to determine whether the milk inoculation can be tested in smaller volumes (ml).
This reflects need number 12 in table 4.1, which states that the stakeholders would
like to use less milk. The experiment will test whether the inoculations performs
equally in 50 ml falcon tubes, in 250 ml glass bottles and in 250 ml plastic bottles.
The test will be conducted by Novonesis as they have better equipment and have

7.9
Evaluation of sprint
According to Ulrich and Eppinger, evaluating partial system builds through rapid it-
eration is a key component of reducing uncertainty and converging on robust design
solutions [2]. This sprint served as a critical iteration in that regard, building upon
the exploratory work initiated during sprint 1 and transitioning toward more precise
subsystem integration.
To conclude the first design sprint, we went over the different elements of system,
and discussed what should be kept, but also what needed to be changed. This
allows to have a starting point for the next sprint. The first sprint was used to start
exploring solutions for the different sub-systems, because of this a lot of the design
need to be further developed. At the end of this sprint, work has been done, allowing
us to start designing more specific elements and to start dialing in component design
and placement more precisely for the next sprint.
Some of the elements that should be kept are dimensions for the array of pH-probes
- 40 mm by 40 mm center to center. The system dimensions have not resulted in
any conflicts and is kept. The Z-axis design is not done, but for the next sprints the
principle design will be kept.
Elements that need to be changed are the X-axis moment, which as discussed
should be moved into the ”table”. Updated guiderails to increase stability. Better
layout for bearings, as well as more of them. Common for most of these are in-
creased rigidity and stability, as the system need to afford precise movement. It is
important to work on making the system as rigid and stabile as possible, as this will
help when later designing movement patterns.

7.11
Conclusion
Sprint 1 marked the beginning of the design process, transitioning from initial re-
search into early concept development. Based on the functional areas defined in
Chapter 6, the goal was to begin shaping how these core functions, calibration,
cleaning, storage, and testing, could be implemented.
A visit to Novonesis provided valuable real-world insights into how the current sys-
tem operates and confirmed many of our initial assumptions. We observed a range
of manual steps, from probe calibration and cleaning to bottle handling and cable
management, all of which contributed to inefficiencies and practical challenges in
the lab workflow.
Several important developments took place during the sprint. The calibration sys-
tem was rethought, leading to a new combined tray design that reduced the system’s
footprint while enabling multi-buffer calibration. Supporting experiments on contam-
ination and evaporation validated concerns around residual liquids and buffer in-
tegrity, which in turn shaped decisions about system layout and cleaning sequence.
Work also began on the motion system using a repurposed Anet A8 motor con-
troller and Marlin firmware, resulting in a working prototype of the Z-axis and early
integration of the X and Y axes (see figure 7.18).
Although no updates to the design specification were made, a lot was learned about
the basic system layout, movement design, and calibration approach. These in-
sights form the basis for Sprint 2, where more subsystems will be added and key
mechanical and functional elements will be further refined and tested.

8
Designing the system - Sprint 2
8.1
Scope of the sprint
The primary objective of the second design sprint was to iteratively refine and ex-
pand the mechanical structure based on insights gained in sprint 1. The focus areas
were:
– Further development of axial movement systems (X, Y, Z axes)
– Redesign and implementation of a new calibration tray (see Section 7.3.2)
– Integration of movement control into the system
– Replacement of existing rail system with a more robust linear rail type
These goals were aimed at improving mechanical stability and enabling multi-axis
motion. With the iterative cycles of system-level design as described by Ulrich and
Eppinger, successive refinements are made based on evaluation of subsystems and
integration of new constraints [2].
8.2
Calibration
To enable reliable and repeatable calibration of the pH-probes, it was necessary to
develop a system capable of automatically handling buffer solutions in a controlled
manner. The goal was to simulate the manual calibration process used at Novone-
sis, where the pH-probes are stabilised and measured in certified buffer liquids of
known pH - typically pH 4.00 and 7.00, with the possibility of a third (e.g., pH 2.00)
in specific cases.
The buffer solutions are produced in large industrial volumes, which means their
composition - and thereby their pH - is extremely stable across batches. This makes
them ideal for calibration, provided the handling process does not alter the buffer’s
properties. Still we change them out everyday according to Novonesis’ procedure.
One key challenge was ensuring that the buffer’s pH value remains unchanged
when transferred from storage to the calibration area. Even small amounts of cross-
contamination (e.g., from residual water, ethanol, or milk) can shift the pH outside
the tight tolerances required for valid calibration as seen previously. Additionally,
the buffer temperature must match the operating conditions of the probes during
the actual experiment, which means it has to be heated and distributed carefully
inside the machine.
To address these requirements, we initiated the design of a dedicated liquid handling
subsystem. This subsystem will ensure:
– Controlled delivery of buffer solutions to the calibration chambers.
– Minimal volume loss and cross-contamination.
– Possibility for liquid recirculation or flushing between calibrations.
– Compatibility with heating or temperature stabilization where necessary.
Several types of pump technologies were considered, as the transfer method must
not introduce other things, that could influence pH stability. Furthermore, the system
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
51
==PAGE:62==
must allow the liquids to be routed through compact tubing or manifolds integrated
into the machine’s lower compartment.
8.2.1
Liquid pumps
Selecting the appropriate pump technology is a critical aspect of the liquid handling
system. Different pump types offer varying levels of control, precision, and compat-
ibility with the types of fluids used in our setup - in this case, pH buffers, ethanol,
and demineralized water.
Before choosing a pump, several key considerations must be addressed:
– Chemical compatibility: The materials used in the pump must be resistant to
the chemicals they will be exposed to - particularly ethanol, which can degrade
certain plastics and seals over time.
– Contamination risk: For pH buffer solutions and ethanol, even small levels of
contamination can compromise calibration quality or cleaning effectiveness.
It is therefore essential that the pump design allows for clean, isolated fluid
transfer.
– Flow control: Since the calibration process involves small but precise amounts
of liquid, the pump must provide reliable low-flow operation, so we also can
save on the amount we are using.
– Maintenance and robustness: The pump must be serviceable and durable
enough for frequent use, especially during cleaning cycles.
Several pump technologies were reviewed:
– Peristaltic pumps: These use rotating rollers to compress a flexible tube, push-
ing the liquid forward. Since the fluid only touches the tubing, cross-contamination
is minimized, and cleaning is simplified. This makes peristaltic pumps an at-
tractive choice for handling both ethanol and pH buffers.
– Diaphragm pumps: These pumps use a flexible membrane to move liquids.
They offer good chemical resistance and can be very accurate, but they may be
more complex to integrate due to their need for valves and potential pulsation
in flow.
– Gear and turbine pumps: Often used in industrial systems, these provide high
flow rates but are less ideal for small volumes and may introduce shear forces
that could affect sensitive fluids or cause foaming.
– Pressure-based systems: These rely on applying controlled air or fluid pres-
sure to push liquid from a reservoir. While simple and compact, they often lack
the fine flow control required for calibration-grade accuracy [20].
For our system, the cleanliness of the pH buffers and ethanol is of highest priority.
The pump mechanism must ensure that these fluids are not exposed to contamina-
tion from previous liquids or pump internals. In contrast, for rinsing with demineral-
ized water, the requirements are less strict - some minor contamination or backflow
is acceptable, as the water is only used for flushing, not measurement.
At this stage, peristaltic pumps are the leading candidate due to their clean fluid path,
ease of tubing replacement, and suitability for low-volume, precision applications.
Their modularity also aligns well with our system’s prototyping-focused design.
52
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
==PAGE:63==
8.2.2
Peristaltic pumps
For handling pH buffers and other critical liquids where the chemical properties
must remain unchanged, we selected peristaltic pumps as the preferred solution.
Peristaltic pumps are particularly well-suited for this task because the liquid being
pumped never comes into contact with any mechanical part. It flows exclusively
through a flexible silicone tube. This ensures that the liquid path remains clean
and isolated from the pump’s moving components, minimizing the risk of cross-
contamination.
The working principle of a peristaltic pump is inspired by peristalsis in biological
systems, such as how food moves through the digestive system. Rotating rollers
press down on a flexible tube in a sequence, creating a pulse-like motion that drives
the fluid forward. In our case, this allows each buffer type - pH 4.00, pH 7.00, (and
if needed pH 2.00) - along with ethanol and KCl storage solution, to be handled by
separate tubing lines, ensuring their chemical stability is preserved during transfer.
To match the rest of the system architecture, we chose a compact 12V peristaltic
pump. This allows us to power the entire fluid subsystem using the same voltage
as the motor controller and other electronics, simplifying the power supply design.
The motor includes internal gear reduction, providing high pressure at a low flow rate
- ideal for moving small volumes of liquid with controlled delivery. This is relevant
since we then can just turn on the pump for a certain amount of time, to get a desired
millilitre output. Then we don’t need a flowmeter for precise amounts.
Scrap parts
To accelerate early testing of liquid transfer, we explored the use of scrap compo-
nents sourced from SkylabA.55. Specifically, we repurposed parts from a startup
called Tinyfarms, which had discarded small peristaltic pumps they previously used
in their micro-farm setup for pumping fertilizer and irrigation water.
After disassembling the leftover equipment (fig. 8.1), we isolated several pump units
and associated electronics. These were reassembled and connected to a basic test
rig to evaluate their suitability for moving buffer liquids in our prototype system.
Initial tests showed that the repurposed pump units were able to deliver approxi-
mately 65 mL per minuteA.57. While this flow rate is relatively low, it would not
necessarily be a problem in a fully automated system, where the time to fill or flush
lanes is not limited by human interaction. In principle, the pump could run as long
as needed to complete a cycle.
However, several limitations became apparent. The tubing used in the original setup
had aged significantly. It looked like it had darkened, become stiff, and lost flexibil-
ity. We attempted to restore its elasticity by submerging it in boiling water, but this
resulted in only a minor improvement. The flow rate increased by about 2 mL per
minute, which was not enough to justify continued useA.58.
Additionally, the motor driving the pump appeared to run with noticeable resistance
and inconsistency. This raised concerns about long-term reliability and repeata-
bility. Combined with the degraded tubing, the test results suggested that these
scrap components would not meet the stability and cleanliness requirements of our
system, particularly when handling pH buffers or ethanol.
While this exploration provided some useful insights into potential low-cost solu-
tions, it ultimately confirmed the decision to move forward with new, purpose-built
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
53
==PAGE:64==
Figure 8.1: Scrapparts form Tinyfarms
peristaltic pumps for all critical fluid handling tasks.
8.2.3
Pressure pump
To clean the pH-probe after each test cycle, we aimed to eliminate the need for man-
ual scrubbing by instead relying on high-pressure rinsing. The goal was to dislodge
any residual biofilm or milk proteins using only water and afterwards ethanol at suf-
ficient force. This would hopefully reduce maintenance time, improve hygiene, and
lower the risk of damaging the probe surfaces through repeated physical contact.
To evaluate this approach, we sourced a compact high-pressure diaphragm pump
with a rated flow of up to 6 liters per minute and a maximum pressure of 0.9 MPa
(9 bar or about 130 PSI). For comparison, this output pressure exceeds that of a
typical residential household water system (2-4 bar)[21], indicating good cleaning
potential. The hypothesis was that by routing the liquid through narrow nozzles,
we could amplify the local pressure at the outlet point and deliver a focused, high-
velocity spray directly onto the probe surfaces.
The pump is driven by a 12V DC motor with a current draw of up to 6A and is
designed for intermittent operation under varying load conditions. This should be
okay since it is not running all the time. A built-in pressure switch automatically cuts
power when flow is blocked (e.g., when valves are closed), and reactivates once flow
resumes. According to the manufacturer, the pump is suitable for use with water,
mild solvents, and fluids with pH values between 5 and 8 - making it compatible with
demineralized water and potentially also diluted ethanol for cleaning purposes.
In contrast to peristaltic pumps, this diaphragm pump type does allow fluid to con-
tact internal mechanical surfaces, including check valves and a flexible diaphragm
membrane. This might be a concern for further development.
Initial tests showed promising results, where it was quickly just connected to a vari-
able powersupply. Here it almost blew off its own protective rubber plug, that it came
with. Whether the cleaning performance will fully eliminate the need for manual wip-
54
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
==PAGE:65==
Figure 8.2: Solid state relay board with 12V DC motor test
ing remains to be validated through longer-term experiments.
8.3
Relays
8.3.1
Solid state relays
To get started quickly with testing the pump sequences and heating control, we
initially used a solid state relay (SSR) board that was already on hand. This allowed
us to perform early switching tests and verify basic system behavior.
Solid state relays have several advantages. Most notably, they operate completely
silently since there are no moving parts. This makes them ideal for noise-sensitive
applications or for systems where frequent switching might otherwise lead to me-
chanical wear. In our setup, they were used successfully to toggle AC-powered
components during initial tests.
However, the SSR board we used had some limitations. Each channel required its
own dedicated control signal, meaning we needed one GPIO pin per relay. This
quickly became impractical as the number of devices increased.
More critically, we discovered that the specific relays on the board were designed for
switching AC loads only. This meant they could not reliably switch 12V DC devices,
such as the pumps (fig. 8.2). As a result, the solid state relay board was not suitable
for the full range of control tasks in the final setupA.53.
To simplify the wiring and ensure compatibility with both AC and DC loads, we ulti-
mately switched to electromechanical relays that could be controlled via I2C. This
allowed us to reduce the number of GPIO pins required and integrate everything
more cleanly into the Raspberry Pi’s communication structure.
8.3.2
Electromechanical relays
To control the power to pumps, heaters, and other binary on/off devices, we use
relays as electrical switches. These allow us to activate or deactivate individual
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
55
==PAGE:66==
components from the Raspberry Pi, forming the basis for time-based or condition-
based control logic.
Figure 8.3: Relay board with I2C control
Relays used in this system are of the electromechanical type. These consist of a coil
and a physical switch mechanism. When a voltage is applied to the coil, it generates
a magnetic field that mechanically closes (or opens) the switch, allowing current to
flow through a separate high-voltage circuit. This enables the Raspberry Pi to control
devices operating at much higher voltages or currents than it could handle directly,
effectively isolating the low-voltage logic from the high-power components.
For example, peristaltic pumps do not require variable speed control in our setup.
Instead, they are activated for a specific duration to deliver a measured volume of
liquid. By turning the power on or off through a relay, we can manage these doses
precisely using simple timing logic. Similarly, heating elements can be toggled at
defined intervals to maintain a target temperature, with the relay acting as a basic
temperature controller.
To manage these relay signals efficiently, we sourced a 16-channel relay board that
uses the I2C communication protocol. This was chosen to save GPIO pins on the
Raspberry Pi, as each relay can be addressed via a shared bus rather than requiring
its own dedicated digital output.
At the time of purchase, information on the I2C relay board was limited. To mitigate
risk, we also acquired a traditional relay module, where each channel is controlled
directly through its own data pin. Fortunately, the I2C board worked as intended
once it was set up. However, during initial integration, we encountered an issue
with the Python library used to control the board. A naming error in the code caused
the library to fail during initialization - the variable ”address” was mistakenly used
instead of ”addr” in one of the internal referencesA.54..
With help from GitHub Copilot, we were able to locate and fix the problem quickly.
After correcting the name in the library source code, the board responded as ex-
pected and could be controlled through I2C.
We then wrote a simple wrapper in Python to communicate with the relay board and
added logical naming for each output channel. This made the code much easier to
work with and helped avoid confusion when managing multiple relays from different
parts of the system.
56
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
==PAGE:67==
8.4
Heatingpad
To control the water temperature in testing area the calibration bath, a reliable heat-
ing method is required. When we visited Novonesis, we observed that their system
used immersion thermostats from Lauda. These function similarly to a sous vide de-
vice, with an integrated heating element and a small circulation propeller to maintain
a stable water temperature.
Figure 8.4: 220/230V AC heatpads
For our prototype, we decided to pursue an alternative approach. Instead of using
an immersion heater, we installed a large heating pad underneath the water bath.
This hopefully allows for more uniform heat distribution across the entire bottom sur-
face of the container, reducing the risk of localized hotspots and improving thermal
consistency throughout the bath.
To further enhance temperature uniformity, a circulation pump has been added to
the system. This pump will ensure that heat is evenly distributed throughout the
volume of water. The effectiveness of this approach will be evaluated in later testing
phases.
The heating element selected for this purpose is a silicone heating pad. These pads
are flexible, waterproof, and well suited for mounting directly onto the underside of a
flat metal surface. The model we use operates exclusively at 220–230V AC, which
allows us to connect it directly to mains power. This simplifies the power delivery
system, as we can route the incoming voltage through one of the relays, which is
then used to control the heating pad from the Raspberry Pi.
Specifications of the selected heating pad:
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
57
==PAGE:68==
– Material: Silicone rubber with alloy heating wire
– Voltage: 220V AC
– Power density: Approx. 0.4 W/cm²
– Max operating temperature: ≤150 °C
– Safe breakdown voltage: 20–50 kV/mm
– Waterproof and moisture-resistant
8.5
Main computer
At the core of the system, a central processing unit is required to collect sensor
data, make decisions, and coordinate the various subsystems such as motor con-
trol, pump actuation, timing sequences, and user interface functions. This main
computer acts as the brain of the robot, linking together all mechanical and elec-
tronic components into a cohesive, automated process.
There are many different types of embedded computers and microcontrollers avail-
able on the market, each with their own strengths in terms of performance, I/O ca-
pability, and power consumption. For this project, we selected the Raspberry Pi 5
as the main control unit.
By placing the Raspberry Pi 5 at the center of the system, we ensure that the sys-
tem can manage complex tasks such as synchronized movements, timed cleaning
cycles, and automatic calibration procedures in a reliable and maintainable way.
8.5.1
Raspberry Pi
This is a compact yet powerful platform that can run full operating systems like Linux,
making it highly suitable for managing the various tasks required by our automated
system. In this project, we are using Raspberry Pi OS, which comes preloaded with
a wide selection of useful packages and tools, making it easier to get started with
development.
The Raspberry Pi 5 offers a good balance between performance and accessibility.
It features a quad-core 64-bit Broadcom BCM2712 processor clocked at 2.4GHz,
combined with 4GB LPDDR4X RAM. This makes it more than capable of handling
both low-level real-time control scripts and high-level logic such as data logging,
calibration routines, or even user interface control. It also has built-in wireless ca-
pabilities, including dual-band Wi-Fi and Bluetooth 5.0, if this should be needed in
a special case.
The board includes two USB 3.0 ports, two USB 2.0 ports, and a full 40-pin GPIO
header. The GPIO interface makes it possible to control motors, read sensors, and
communicate with microcontrollers via digital signals. The USB ports are useful for
connecting peripherals such as the motor controller board, external sensors, or even
a USB camera.
In terms of user interaction, the Raspberry Pi 5 supports dual 4K HDMI outputs[22],
which could be used to power a touch screen interface in future iterations of the sys-
tem. This would allow direct control and monitoring from the machine itself, without
needing a separate keyboard or mouse.
One limitation of the Raspberry Pi platform is that its GPIO pins are digital-only. This
means it cannot directly read analog signals, such as from certain temperature sen-
58
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
==PAGE:69==
Figure 8.5: Raspberry Pi 5 4GB
sors or fluid level pH-probe. To address this, external microcontrollers or dedicated
analog-to-digital converter (ADC) chips are required. These can either be mounted
on a prototyping board or integrated into the system using standard communication
interfaces like I2C, SPI, or UARTA.59.
Beyond the hardware, one of the Raspberry Pi’s greatest advantages is its strong
and active community. A vast amount of libraries, tutorials, and forum support ex-
ists, which significantly accelerates development and troubleshooting. The technical
details can be viewed worksheet A.71.
8.5.2
Programming languages
Given the range of hardware and tasks involved in the system, a few different pro-
gramming languages are required to meet the specific needs of each component.
On the Raspberry Pi 5, which runs a Linux-based operating system, we have the
flexibility to use virtually any language. However, for the sake of simplicity and fast
development, we have chosen to focus primarily on Python.
Python is one of the most widely used languages on the Raspberry Pi due to its
readability, ease of use, and the vast ecosystem of libraries available for hardware
control. With libraries such as gpiozero, RPi.GPIO, pyserial, and smbus, Python
allows us to control GPIO pins, communicate with serial devices, and interface with
I2C sensors and peripherals without needing to write large amounts of setup or low-
level code manually. This enables us to move quickly from prototype to test without
reinventing basic control logic from scratch.
In addition to Python, other programming languages are necessary when working
with microcontrollers or low-level embedded systems. For example, C and C++
are the standard languages for the Arduino framework, which is commonly used
to program boards like the ESP32. These microcontrollers may become relevant
later in the project, particularly when we need to add analog sensing, offload real-
time tasks from the Raspberry Pi, or implement custom serial or I2C communication
protocols.
Each language serves a specific purpose within the system architecture:
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
59
==PAGE:70==
– Python: Used for high-level coordination, data handling, and hardware control
on the Raspberry Pi.
– C/C++: Used for programming microcontrollers that handle time-critical oper-
ations, analog input, or sensor integration.
In the next section, we will explore the communication protocols used to allow these
components to share data and cooperate efficiently.
8.5.3
Communication protocols
To coordinate the various subsystems in the system, a structured communication
strategy is required. Since we are working with a combination of single-board com-
puters, microcontrollers, and peripheral hardware, there is no single universal pro-
tocol. Instead, we need to select appropriate communication interfaces based on
the role and capabilities of each device.
At the center of the system is the Raspberry Pi 5, which acts as the primary control
unit. It is responsible for receiving data from sensors and microcontrollers, making
decisions, and sending commands to actuators such as motors, pumps, or relays.
For communication with external microcontrollers, such as an ESP32 or Arduino-
compatible board, we use UART over a serial connection. Serial communication
is simple, reliable, and widely supported. To make message parsing easier and
reduce the need for rigid message formatting, we have chosen to send structured
data using A.63. This allows us to refer to data fields by name instead of position,
which simplifies both sending and receiving data - especially as the complexity of
the system grows.
For peripherals such as the relay board, communication takes place over the I2C
protocol. Many off-the-shelf relay modules come with an onboard chip that supports
I2C natively. Once I2C is enabled in the Raspberry Pi’s configuration, it can control
these devices directly using existing Python libraries. This allows us to easily ad-
dress specific relays and switch them on or off as needed without writing low-level
communication code.
The 3D printer controller board, which handles motion control, also communicates
using UART - but in this case, via a USB serial connection. This allows the Rasp-
berry Pi to send standard G-code commands directly to the board, telling it where
to move the probe array during calibration and measurement procedures.
Each communication method is chosen to match the technical requirements and
existing capabilities of the connected device:
– UART (serial): Used for communication with microcontrollers and the 3D printer
board.
– JSON format: Used within UART messages to simplify parsing and improve
reliability.
– I2C: Used for simpler addressable peripherals such as relay boards or sensor
interfaces.
8.6
3D printing
Since we are developing a physical product, 3D design and rapid prototyping have
been essential throughout the entire project. From early conceptual models to func-
60
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
==PAGE:71==
tional mechanical parts, 3D printing has enabled us to quickly go from idea to pro-
totype and then to final component without the delays and complexity of traditional
manufacturing methods.
3D printing makes it possible to test the fit, alignment, and interaction of parts di-
rectly in the physical world. This is particularly important when working with as-
semblies where small tolerances matter. Instead of milling components in metal or
fabricating them using time-consuming methods, we have been able to print softer,
plastic-based prototypes to validate geometry and function. This has allowed us to
iterate designs rapidly and detect any physical conflicts or design flaws early in the
development process.
Figure 8.6: Bambu Lab PS1 printer used for more precise parts
All printed parts in the project were manufactured using Fused Deposition Modeling
(FDM), a widely used additive manufacturing method [23]. FDM printers build up
objects layer by layer by extruding melted thermoplastic filament along a predefined
toolpath. It is a reliable and accessible technology that is well suited for producing
prototypes and functional parts.
For this project, we primarily printed parts in PLA (Polylactic Acid). PLA is easy to
work with, has fairly good stability, and is readily available. While it does not have
the same mechanical strength or thermal resistance as other materials like PETG
or ABS, it was sufficient for most of our prototyping needs, especially in non-load-
bearing components. As the project progressed, some of the parts were reprinted
in PETG, to ensure better strength when needed.
We printed our parts using two main setups, the 3D printing FabLab in Building 413
at DTU, and a Bambu Lab P1S located in the DALSA laboratory.
With these resources we could pretty quickly produce components with consistent
quality and precision during each development sprint.
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
61
==PAGE:72==
8.7
X-movement
8.7.1
Calibration and movement
As mentioned at the end of sprint 1, we wanted to implement a fixed X-axis archi-
tecture, from which the pH-probe were hanging. The reason behind this change
was the updated design for the calibration tray (see section 7.3.2). With the new
design for the calibration tray, the system has been reduced in width, and due to
this, the pH-probe only travel in a straight line along the Y-axis when calibrating.
This will make sure that the pH-probe can only be mispositioned along the Y-axis,
not the X-axis, reducing the risk of crashing the expensive pH-probe and thereby
damaging them. Trying to design the system to be manufactured using more con-
Figure 8.7: Initial render of system for sprint 2
ventional manufacturing processes, the first version of the new X-axis was designed
to be made from bend steel-plates. The thought was that they were inexpensive and
easy to produce. The new design included a plate to hold the bearing blocks, a mo-
tor mount, and plates fixing a Bosch Rexroth profile to the Y-axis rails. As described
in section 7.8 the system was also upgraded to use more robust rail, this was done
in an attempt to add more rigidity. One of the major challenges with this design was
producing the steel parts fast enough to keep up with the changes that occurred dur-
ing the prototyping process. Due to this challenge, we instead tried to 3D-print the
62
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
==PAGE:73==
parts, while keeping the design based on a steel plate. This introduced a new chal-
lenge, when 3D printing parts to simulate steel, you often loose a lot of strength, and
typically you would then increase thickness, but because we specifically wanted to
test the fitment of the steel, this was a challenge. Ultimately, we ended up designing
specifically for 3D printing, but with machining possibilities in mind.
8.7.2
New calibration tray
Based on the updated calibration tray, the movement of the x-axis was moved down
to the calibration tray at the bottom level. What is meant by bottom levels is the
system hight level at which the water bath and the cleaning station is located. The
top level is the level on which the Y-axis is located, it is also the level that the pH-
probes are hanging from.
Due to space limitations it was decided that this level should use a belt drive for
motion control. Lead screws as used for the Z-axis would be more robust and pre-
cise, but is also takes up much more space. With the leadscrews being ridgid it also
allows much less adjustments, and misalignment are a much bigger issue than with
the belt drive.
The subsystem is constructed with two rails with brackets mounting them to 3030
profiles. On these are bearing blocks mounted to a plate creating a sled for the
calibration tray. The sled is moved using a bracket that fixes the belt, which is on
one side ”running”around the motor and on the other side a bearing. (Reference
worksheet A.37)
Figure 8.8: First prototype for the calibration tray movement
On top of the sled is the calibration tray it self. At this stage no liquid handling is
included in the design as we more focused on the movement it self. A full size model
of the calibration tray was on the other hand designed in CAD, helping us design the
sled and all the mounts and brackets. By having the full size model, we can verify
in CAD that all parts should fit. Every thing is afterwards tested with 3D printed
prototypes, but the CAD drawings helps with sanity check as early as possible to
verify the design.
Even though liquid handling was not within scope of this sprint, still being at the back
of our mind, we realized during the design of the sled, that due to the width of the
calibration tray, that there would not be space for any hoses and the sides. This
meant that we had to change the orientation of the lanes in the tray. Making them
go from back-to-front instead of side-to-side.

63
==PAGE:74==
8.8
Y-movement
With the upgraded rail on the Y-axis another issue became visible. The new bearing
block was wider than the 3030 Bosch profiles, the issue with is is that it will make
it necessary to offset the outer panels and thereby make the system wider than
the design specification allows. Therefore, it was necessary to investigate other
methods of installing the rail and blocks. This was done using a principle structure
analysis. Here different mounting options were visually tested (see worksheet A.7).
The test showed that using 6030 Bosch profiles would allow the rails to be shifted
inverts equal to the width of a profile. The 6030 profile being 60mm wide instead of
30mm, can be installed the same way as the 3030, but it allows the change in rail
placement without the need for custom brackets. Because of the simplicity and the
fact that they could be installed in the same way as the 3030 profiles, it was deamed
the easiest and best way to solve the problem.
Figure 8.9: Updated placement for new rails and bearing block
With the new rails installed on a test rig, the design of the motor mount and brackets
for gear rack began. Here we had a focus on making sure the motor placement could
be adjusted. With rack and pinion being the power transfer type, it was important that
they could be adjusted in relation to each other. When using rack and pinion gearing,
misalignment can lead to increased backlash, reduced accuracy, and premature
wear of components [24]. To incorporate this adjustability, the mount was designed
with slids on which the motor can be moved up or down - with that the pinion closer
or further from the rack, additionally the gear can be moved back and forth on the
motor axel, making sure that the teeth are as aligned as possible.
8.9
Z-rail
With same consideration as with the brackets for the Y-axis of being able to man-
ufacture everything with bend sheet metal, new parts was designed for the X-axis.
As the X-axis is no logner a moving part (at least in that direction) it will from now on
be called the Z-rail. In an attempt to make the Z-rail as simple as possible - to make
manufaturing faster and cheaper, the parts was as mentioned based on bend sheet
metal. For this iteration it was constructed based on three parts, a part on which
bearing blocks for the Z-axis are mounted, a part on which the motor and gears lifting
the Z-axis are mounted and a brace for the previous. To help with rigidity these were
installed on a Bosch profile extended between the two bracket mentioned in section
8.8. Before the placement of the components could be determined, it was important
to investigate the product architecture. By creating a rough geometric layouts visu-
64
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
==PAGE:75==
Figure 8.10: Rails, rack, pinion and motor assembled on profiles
ally it was possible to analyse different architectures before building prototypes. In
worksheet A.32 six different concepts are constructed visually and evaluated based
on three parameters, space, rigidity, and ease of construction with the two first ones
being of highest priority.
Based on the architectures explored, one was selected (see worksheet A.35. On
this concept the plate for the bearings and the motor mount was then installed on
each side of the profile. Figure 8.11 illustrates how motor mount was installed onto
the Bosch profile. The brakets and mount were all installed on the profile using t-
nuts. On the other side of the profile is the plate for bearing blocks, due to wrong
hardware spacer blocks was installed on this itteration, but will be changed in the
future if design is kept.

8.10
Evaluation of sprint
8.10.1
Challenges with the new rail design
The new rails was installed to make the system more sturdy, but after installing the
bearing blocks, it was concluded that the friction was higher than expected. This
became even more evident after the installing the rack, motor and pinion (fig. 8.10.
Do to the higher friction, the thin 3D printed mounts bend. These are the mounts
that connect the Z-axis to the rails as well as holding the motor. Being to thin, the
mount would bend as soon as power was sent to the motor, which would result in the
pinion not making full contact with the rack, and because of that skipping gears. This
needs to be solved in the next sprint, as it is crucial for ensuring smooth movement
and optimal functionality.
8.10.2
Z-rail and Z-axis
With much of the focus in this sprint being on the new rail and the calibration tray,
limited time was spend on the Z-rail and updating the Z-axis. In the following sprints
more iterations must be made to these parts. Similarly, with the other brackets,
additional strength is needed for all the parts, and the design therefore needs to be
updated.
8.11
Changes to Design specification
As there were no major discoveries in this sprint, no changes were made to the DS,
and for now it will remain as it is. It will be evaluated again at the end of the next
sprint.

8.12
Conclusion
This sprint marked a significant step toward building a fully integrated system ca-
pable of automating pH calibration, cleaning, and temperature-controlled measure-
ment. While the primary objective was to improve the mechanical design and pre-
pare the platform for multi-axis motion, several parallel efforts contributed to shaping
a more robust and functional prototype.
Major progress was made on the calibration subsystem, where buffer handling, heat-
ing, and movement logistics were mapped out and tested using both new and repur-
posed components. After evaluating multiple technologies, peristaltic pumps were
selected for critical fluid transfers due to their clean internal path and compatibil-
ity with pH-sensitive liquids. For cleaning, a high-pressure diaphragm pump was
introduced to replace manual rinsing, though further testing is needed to verify its
long-term performance.
Mechanically, the system saw several key changes. The Y-axis rail was upgraded
and repositioned using 6030 profiles to avoid interference with outer panels while
maintaining alignment. The Z-rail concept was updated and redesigned using bracket-
mounted sheet metal style parts, and early validation of a belt-driven sled was com-
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
67
==PAGE:78==
pleted for moving the calibration tray.
Relay logic and hardware control were also refined. By switching from solid state
to electromechanical relays controlled via I2C, we reduced GPIO pin usage and
improved control over both AC and DC components. Additionally, the Raspberry
Pi 5 was successfully integrated as the main controller, offering the performance
needed for coordinating pumps, heating, and motion sequences.
Figure 8.13 shows the current system configuration at the end of Sprint 2. While the
design remains under development, this version represents a functional prototype
architecture that introduces movement on multiple axis. The foundation is now in
place for advancing toward a fully automated calibration and measurement setup in
future sprints.
Figure 8.13: Overview of system configuration at the end of Sprint 2
68
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
==PAGE:79==
9
Designing the system - Sprint 3
9.1
Scope of the sprint
The goal for sprint 3 was to further iterate on the discoveries from the previous sprint.
Most of the element in this goal was updating the existing system:
– Update and strengthen brackets on Y-axis
– Update design on Z-rail to hold the Z-axis
– Update to calibration tray
– Integrate liquid handling into calibration tray
– Validation of Falcon tubes by Novonesis
The primary objective of Sprint 3 was to iterate on discoveries made during the previ-
ous sprint by refining existing system elements. Key focus areas included strength-
ening the Y-axis brackets, redesigning the Z-rail to better support the Z-axis, updat-
ing the calibration tray design, and integrating liquid handling functionality into the
calibration setup.
9.1.1
Changes based on previous sprint
One of the major issues discovered in sprint 2 was the friction in the rails on the Y-
axis. With the friction being higher than anticipated, this resulted in the thin bracket,
which fixed all the parts to each other, bending once the motor was turned on. This
was therefore the first focus area for sprint 3.
With much of the focus being on the new rails and the calibration tray, not much
attention was paid to the Z-rail and Z-axis. Similarly to the brackets on the Y-axis,
the parts on the Z-rail were not strong enough, and these will also need an overhaul,
to ensure that they are strong enough.
9.2
New components
During Sprint 3, progress was slowed by the wait for newly ordered components in
Sprint 2. Most of the parts required for the next development phase were sourced
from suppliers in China, primarily due to strict budget constraints. This meant that
nearly all critical components had to be ordered with a minimum delivery time of 2.5
weeks.
While waiting for deliveries, we focused on tuning and refining the parts of the sys-
tem we already had available. This included improving the integration between pre-
viously assembled modules and preparing the system to accommodate the new
hardware once it arrived. By aligning mechanical fits, updating software interfaces,
and testing electrical connections in advance, we aimed to ensure that new compo-
nents could be added with minimal rework.
Although the delay temporarily limited the speed of development, it also provided a
useful window to review design assumptions.
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
69
==PAGE:80==
9.3
Codebase
As the system evolved, it became increasingly important to develop a structured
and maintainable code base. In the early stages of the project, each component
was tested in isolation using small Python scripts. This allowed us to verify the
functionality of individual submodules - such as pumps via the relay board, motors
controlled by the 3D printer board and sensors read by the ESP32 - without needing
the rest of the system to be operational. This approach was simple and effective for
early prototyping but lacked the structure necessary for long-term scalability.
Commit Message

Table 9.1: Commit history for the codebase in the beginning of using git. Each commit is
tracked using a unique hash for reference and rollback.
As the complexity of the system increased, it was important to adopt better practices
for managing and organizing the code. To track changes, coordinate updates, and
document development history, we introduced Git as our version control system.
Git is a distributed version control tool widely used in software development to track
code changes and facilitate collaboration[25]. It enables developers to compare
different versions, roll back to previous states, and work in parallel without interfering
with each other’s changes.
Each time a change is made and committed to the repository, Git stores it as a
”commit” - a snapshot of the project at that point in time, labeled with a unique hash.
These commits can be accompanied by descriptive messages explaining what was
changed and why. If an issue arises after a new feature is introduced, Git makes it
easy to revert to a previous, known-working version of the code. This functionality
proved especially useful as we began introducing new features or integrating multi-
ple subsystems simultaneously, where unintended side effects could otherwise be
difficult to trace.
The code running on the Raspberry Pi was therefore also restructured to follow best
practices for Python projects. Instead of standalone scripts, the project was divided
into logically organized modules and folders, using naming conventions aligned with
Python packaging standards. This modular approach makes the codebase easier
to maintain and reuse. It also allows the entire system to be treated as an installable
Python package - which simplifies deployment, testing, and integration on new or
other devices.
Development was carried out inside a Python virtual environment on the Raspberry
Pi. This made it possible to isolate project dependencies and install external libraries
without interfering with the system-wide Python installation. By combining this en-
70
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
==PAGE:81==
vironment with the Git-based structure, we ensured that the entire software stack -
including code, dependencies, and project history - could be reliably replicated on
other systems with minimal effort.
In table 9.1, an example is shown of the commit activity across a single day in the
beginning and where we discovered some errors in one of the external libraries we
wanted to use. This highlights how changes were made, tested, and versioned
throughout the development process.
9.4
Y-movement
As mentioned in section 8.10 the 3D printed parts were to thin, resulting in issues
where the pinion gear would not intersect with the rack. When this happened the
pinion would ”jump” on rack, making a horrible sound. Besides the fact that this
results in the parts not moving, it also increases wear on the rack and pinion.
With the project still being in the development phase, we did not want to move
towards steel parts, and the solution was therefore to increase the thickness of
the parts to begin with. As mentioned in worksheet A.20 this greatly improved the
strength of the parts. But with the new bracket mounted and the system being able
to move, a new problem became evident. The bolt and mount for the rail was in
the way of the bolts for the motor. After a change of offset on the motor, this was
resolved (see worksheet A.21. As the clarence was fixed, it was then possible to
make the axis move; however, due to the high friction mentioned earlier, it meant
that, when the side with the motor was driven back and forth, the other side would
not. Because the system was not rigid enough, the two sides did not move together,
and the side without the motor would be dragged behind. A new iteration was there-
fore done, this time adding more bracing to improve rigidity and adding a motor to
both sides (see worksheet A.22). This resolved most issues; however more testing
will be necessary to make a full conclusion.
9.5
Z-rail
Similarly to the y-axis, the z-rail also ditched the 3D printed parts, that was imitating
steel parts. As mentioned in section 8.9 the x-axis movement had been removed
when it was fixed to the y-axis brackets, and subsequently named the z-rail. The
next iteration was designed with a bulkier 3D printed part that had the motor inter-
nally mounted, while also being designed to reduce flex (see worksheet A.27).
One of the challenges with this new design was the gears and lead screw moving
around due to too loose tolerances. With the gear just sitting on top of the z-rail
motor mount, and the hole for the leadscrew being too big, the gear could move,
resulting in teeth skipping. In attempt to solve this, a brace was added on top of
the lead screw gear as shown in figure 9.2. This did not help, however. Instead
of making the gears and thereby the leadscrew movement run smoother, it made
everything bind up and created more friction. To combat this, another version of the
motor mount, gears and brace was designed. This time, bearings was added. A
flat bearing under the gear to carry the load of the leadscrew, and a ball bearing in
the bracing to help align the gear (see worksheet A.29). This again highlighted new
issues. With the gears being 3D printed, it became clear that these would need to
be changed our for stronger and more accurate parts in the future, this task was set
on hold, as it was not as critical as other design tasks.
At the end of the sprint, we noticed that the mounting plate for the pH-probe was not
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
71
==PAGE:82==
Figure 9.1: Last iteration of the y-axis brackets
Figure 9.2: In an attempt to make the gear run better, a brace was added
72
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
==PAGE:83==
h
Figure 9.3: The braces helped stabilise and keep the mounting for the pH-probe level
level. Due to some of the parts being soft (as they were made from plastic) and the
mount being heavy, it resulting in he mount not hanging level under the Z-rail. To
solve this we first try to make a test setup, were the bearings blocks were furter away
from the lead screw. This, however, did not help. In theory it should have solved the
problem, but because the bearing blocks were not tight enough, nothing stopped
the rods from still sliding trough, making the mount not level (see worksheet A.30.
As this did not solve the issue, we kept looking for a solution. We then realised that
the plate holding the bearing blocks was flexing due to the load. To try to solve this,
a set of braces was made. These were mounted og the y-axis bracket and on the
vertical part holding the bearings ( see figure 9.3 and worksheet A.31) Implementing
this brace helped a lot, but better solutions should be investigated in the future.
9.6
Calibration
Based on the work in the previous sprint the shape of the calibration tray was found.
Furthermore, first iteration of the movement and mounting of the tray had also been
designed. But liquid handling had not yet been considered. With a requirement for
all the liquids to be replaced every day, it was important to easily be able to do this.
The first challenge with the addition of liquid handling was space. With the cur-
rent design for the calibration tray being 270mm and 20mm extra required for the
movement and only 320mm space available, it led to some challenges. Having only
15mm free space on each side, it was not enough for tubes or other parts to be
installed. This issue led us to rotate the lanes in the tray, going from side to side,
to instead go back to front. By doing so it was possible to have the inlet in the back
and the outlet in the front. The rear mounted inlet can be seen in figure 9.4.
9.6.1
Inlet Block
To make the liquid inlet, a custom inlet block has been designed for integration
with the backside of the calibration tray. The inlet serves as a distribution interface,
allowing controlled delivery of calibration fluids into the tray’s individual lanes.
The block features four separate inlet ports, each connected to a dedicated peri-
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
73
==PAGE:84==
Figure 9.4: Calibration tray with inlet-seal installed
staltic pump. Internally, each inlet is designed to distribute liquid evenly across six
lanes within the tray. The compact layout of the inlet allows it to be mounted directly
onto the rear of the tray without interfering with movement or probe positioning.
The geometry has been optimized for 3D printing, and initial integration tests have
shown good alignment with the tray interface. At this stage, the inlet design still
needs validation.
9.6.2
Outlet Block
To complete the liquid handling loop of the calibration system, an outlet block has
been designed and mounted beneath the opposite end of the calibration tray. Its
purpose is to collect used calibration and storage liquids from all tray lanes and
guide them to a single outlet for removal. The outlet block is positioned on the
underside of the tray and receives fluid via gravity or slight backpressure from each
lane. Internally, it merges the flow paths and directs all fluids into one common outlet
port. (See worksheet A.38)
This port is connected to a fifth peristaltic pump, which actively removes waste liq-
uids from the system. This setup simplifies fluid disposal and ensures that cross-
contamination is avoided during drainage by isolating the intake and outflow mech-
anisms. Like the inlet, the outlet still needs validation.
9.7
Frame
Sprint 3 marked a huge milestone with the frame components finally arriving. After
many months of planning and trying to ensure that everything was accounted for, the
frame components were ordered by specific lengths. There were multiple reasons
for this. Firstly it ensured that did not have to spend time doing it ourselvs, which
meant we could work on other things while it was being done. Secondly it improved
quality, by ordering them from a bigger company, whose main occupation is selling
these aluminum profiles, meant that they had equipment designed for these tasks.
Once the profiles arrived it was only a matter of following the CAD model and as-
sembling the system. The profiles were assembled using corner bracket, t-nuts and
hex bolts making it fast to put together (see figure 9.5). As mentioned in section 7.6
using the Bosch Rexroth profiles makes it possible to easy change the design as
it is a modular system. for this project it meant that we could change the height of
the different levels, as more components were added and changes were needed.
See figure 9.11 in section 9.11for the finished frame. This was early in the assembly
procress, which meant most components had not yet been fitted.

Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
