Figure 9.5: The profiles were assembled using corner bracket, t-nuts and hex bolts.
9.8
Sample container investigation
One of the major changes introduced with this system, compared to both the ISO
standard [3] and the current setup at Novonesis, was the shift to 50 ml containers.
According to the ISO standard, 250 ml glass bottles should be used for milk acidifica-
tion testing. It was therefore important to investigate whether changes in container
volume or material would affect either the temperature profile or the acidification
kinetics.
The goal of the experiment was to assess whether these changes had any impact on
(1) the temperature dynamics during incubation, and (2) the acidification behavior
of the cultures. Since temperature strongly influences acidification, any thermal
deviation due to container characteristics could render the results non-comparable
to the ISO-standard setup.
Due to the unavailability of the required equipment at DTU, the experiment was
facilitated by Kim Olsson at Novonesis, in collaboration with two colleagues from
the Combinatorial Microbiology department in Hørsholm.
9.8.1
Experimental Setup
The experiment was set up as follows:
1. Two Streptococcus thermophilus cultures with different milk acidification prop-
erties (ST1 and ST2) were inoculated in three different containers pre-filled with
milk for acidification: 250 ml glass bottle (as specified in ISO 26323 [3]), 250 ml
plastic bottle, and 50 ml Falcon tube (centrifugal container). Each condition
was tested in triplicate, resulting in a total of 18 samples.
2. pH electrodes for the iCinac system were calibrated using buffer solutions at
pH = 4 and pH = 7. However, the electrodes were not temperature-calibrated.
3. After calibration, one electrode was inserted into each of the 18 samples, and
the containers were sealed tightly to prevent evaporation.
4. All samples were then incubated in a water bath at 41°C for approximately 22
hours.
9.8.2
Temperature
Although the iCinac electrodes were not temperature-calibrated, the data still allow
for an evaluation of the thermal behavior of the different containers over time. For
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
75
==PAGE:86==
both the ST1 and ST2 cultures, the Falcon tubes exhibited more stable tempera-
ture profiles across replicates, and unlike the larger containers, they did not show a
gradual temperature decline during incubation.
Typically, a slight temperature increase is observed during fermentation as the cul-
tures grow and metabolize lactose into lactic acid, releasing heat through exothermic
biochemical reactions. However, the small volume and high surface-area-to-volume
ratio of the Falcon tubes, combined with the thermally conductive plastic material,
facilitated quicker thermal equilibration with the water bath after the exothermic bio-
chemical reactions had leveled off.
In contrast, the larger glass and plastic bottles—owing to their greater thermal mass—
demonstrated slower response to the external water bath and a small but consis-
tent temperature decrease (0.5–1.0°C) over 19 hours. These dynamics are criti-
cal, as microbial activity and acidification rates are tightly linked to thermal stability
[26, 4, 27].
Figure 9.6: Temperature profile for ST1 across container types.
76
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
==PAGE:87==
Figure 9.7: Temperature profile for ST2 across container types.
9.8.3
Acidification
When analyzing the pH measurements, the data for the ST1 culture showed that
acidification in the 50 ml Falcon tubes closely matched that of the 250 ml glass bot-
tles. Interestingly, the minimum and maximum pH values suggest that samples in
the Falcon tubes may have acidified slightly faster than those in the glass bottles.
In contrast, the 250 ml plastic bottles exhibited a slower acidification profile.
For the ST2 culture, the pattern differed somewhat.
The two larger containers,
glass and plastic, demonstrated nearly identical acidification curves, while the Fal-
con tubes showed slightly delayed acidification. However, the average pH profile of
the Falcon tubes remained within the min–max range of the glass bottle samples.
These findings support the feasibility of using Falcon tubes as a viable small-scale
alternative for continuous acidification monitoring in high-throughput applications.
While some cultures may be more sensitive to changes in container volume, this
initial investigation indicates that 50 ml Falcon tubes can produce sufficiently con-
sistent results. Consequently, their use is deemed acceptable within the framework
of ISO 26323, which emphasizes the importance of consistent and reproducible
acidification curves for evaluating starter culture performance [3, 26].
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
77
==PAGE:88==
Figure 9.8: Acidification profile for ST1 across container types.
Figure 9.9: Acidification profile for ST2 across container types.

9.9
Evaluation of sprint
Sprint 3 continued to build on the iterative development process by refining key me-
chanical and functional aspects of the system. Several important improvements
were made, including the implementation of a double motor setup on the Y-axis.
This significantly improved the system’s rigidity and alignment, addressing previous
synchronization issues and making movement more stable under load. Likewise,
the upgraded Y-axis brackets, redesigned to be thicker and more robust, eliminated
the flex and instability observed in earlier sprints. The calibration tray was updated
to inlude the integration of liquid handling. These developments mark a major step
toward creating a fully automated calibration system. On the Z-rail, improvements
were made to reduce flex and increase precision, but challenges remain. The cur-
rent use of 3D printed gears and lead screw mounts introduced mechanical play and
alignment issues. These parts will need to be replaced by higher-precision compo-
nents to ensure consistent motion and long-term durability. Additionally, the Z-axis
probe mount exhibited uneven positioning due to flex in the mounting plate. The
introduction of braces helped improve stability, but a more robust structural solution
is still needed.

9.11
Conclusion
Sprint 3 focused on strengthening and refining key system elements identified in the
previous sprint. Several improvements were successfully implemented and will be
carried forward, including the dual-motor Y-axis drive, the current calibration tray lay-
out, stronger 3D-printed Y-axis brackets, and the initial liquid handling system. The
general design of the Z-rail also showed potential and will be maintained for now.
Sprint 3 also marked to point where the system started looking like a final product,
with the frame being assembled. At the same time, the sprint revealed new areas
for improvement. The liquid handling system needs further development to ensure
robustness and ease of use. The gear system on the Z-axis demonstrated the limi-
tations of 3D-printed parts and should be replaced with metal components in future
iterations. The calibration tray will require minor updates, and the cleaning system
is still to be developed. Overall, Sprint 3 advanced the project significantly, laying a
stronger mechanical foundation, integrating liquid handling, and validating key de-
sign decisions. These results provide a solid starting point for the next sprint, which
will focus on finalizing subsystem integration and introducing additional automation.
The current state of the system can be seen in figure 9.11, that shows the fully

10.2
Calibration Tray
Following the redesign of the calibration tray to include both inlet and outlet blocks,
the sled was also updated to accommodate these changes. The new sled design
enables improved integration of liquid handling components while also introducing
practical enhancements for usability and thermal control. The updated sled features
a cutout beneath the tray to make room for the newly introduced outlet block. This
ensures that fluid drainage is not obstructed during operation and allows for clean
routing of the waste line to the fifth peristaltic pump. To improve ease of use, the tray
is now mounted with a tool-free locking mechanism, enabling quick installation and
removal for cleaning or replacement. This update reduces downtime and simpli-
fies routine handling in lab workflows. Additionally, the new sled includes dedicated
space for mounting a heat pad and temperature sensor underneath the tray. These
components will support thermal control in future experiments, allowing precise cal-
ibration under defined temperature conditions. See Worksheet A.39
Figure 10.1: Updated sled for the calibration try
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
83
==PAGE:94==
10.3
Cleaning
To ensure reliable measurements in the system, we cannot only rely on the cali-
bration sub system. As discovered in the experiment section 7.3.3 cleaning of the
pH-probes are crucial to ensure that calibration and measurements are done cor-
rectly. The experiment highlighted that two different contaminants were especially
bad - milk and ethanol. This meant that we had to ensure that these two would be
removed from the pH-probes after each (relevant) process step.
10.3.1
Water and Ethanol
To follow the process flow described in section 2.5, the pH-probes need to be both
cleaned with demineralized water and ethanol, and then dried off. In the process
flow, it is described that a napkin is used to wipe of the pH-probes, this is to ensure
that any product stuck on them is removed. As manual wiping can be challenging
in a automated setup, we instead set out to try to develop a sprayer solution, that
would be powerful enough to clean the pH-probes without wiping.
In the attempt to design this sprayer setup, multiple different designs were tested.
(see worksheets A.40) The initial goal was to design a setup that could clean all
pH-probes at ones, but while testing the different sprayers, we realised that the
pump would not be powerful enough. With the desired water pressure, the pump
purchased was inadequate, and we therefore had to rethink the concept for the
cleaning station. In an attempt to solve the issues with what parts were available,
we decided that we would create a cleaning solution that could clean 4 pH-probes
at once, and then be able to move allowing it to clean one row at a time.
Figure 10.2: Sprayer concept with two levels of nozzles allowing the use of two different
liquids
As per the process flow, the required steps include cleaning with water and ethanol.
The sprayer was therefore designed with two layers as shown in Figure 10.2. With
this design, the nozzles at the top level can spray with demineralised water and the
bottom level ethanol. When the pH-probes need cleaning, they dip into the cleaning
system, and follow this sequence:
1. Initial water rinse to remove loose residue
2. Ethanol flush to dissolve organic deposits and assist in drying
3. Final water rinse to neutralise ethanol and stabilise pH before the next mea-
surement
Currently, the system includes provisions for routing and pump control, but full cycle
validation is still pending. The ethanol component, in particular, must be carefully
managed to avoid evaporation, ensure safety, and maintain concentration consis-
tency over time.
84
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
==PAGE:95==
Figure 10.3: Assembled cleaning system
Integration with the spray head or cleaning nozzle system is underway. Future it-
erations will focus on synchronising liquid delivery with the mechanical motion of
theph-probes or tray and verifying cleaning effectiveness under operational condi-
tions.
10.3.2
Air
To reduce contamination, we decided to add an air blade-style fan to the cleaning
system. The inspiration for this came from the Hamilton Microlab ApH module [13].
The ApH module uses an air blade to blow off any liquids from the pH-probes after
use.
The first air-blower design was a proof of concept, based on two parts. A hood for
a fan that would guide the air around the electronics, and a fan shroud that would
divide the air around the sprayers. The initial concept proved the idea, but also
highlighted some issues with the design. Firstly, the initial prototype was designed
quickly without much thought to how it should be assembled, which meant it needed
a hand (or two) to stay together. Secondly, even though it was designed to be 3D
printed, it was not optimised for that, which meant it took a couple of attempts be-
fore a successful print of the shroud was done. On the second version, this was
addressed. The design was optimised with assembly surfaces and holes for bolts.
Furthermore, the parts had been optimised to be printed more easily. (See work-
sheet A.45)
10.3.3
Movement
After creating the cleaning and drying parts, it was time to make it move, enabling it
to clean more than one row. This was done using the same principles as the calibra-
tion tray. With most of the parts already designed and tested these was repurposed,
with the exemption of the bracket for the belt drive. Due to the repurposed design,
the cleaning station also utilized belt drive to move. As we faced the same space
limitations, this was againg the simplest option. But because of the different design
of the cleaner compared to the calibration tray, the belt could not be installed un-
derneath the cleaner. This lead us to move the belt fixture to the end of the cleaner
and with this we chose to redesign one of the linear rod fixtures to also hold the
bearings, on the side with no motor.
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
85
==PAGE:96==
10.4
ESP32
To handle input from both analog and digital sensors in the system, we chose to
integrate an ESP32 microcontroller. The ESP32 offers a strong combination of per-
formance, flexibility, and ease of use, making it well-suited for managing auxiliary
sensor tasks and communicating with the main system.
Specifically, we used the ESP32-WROOM-32 module, which is mounted on a stan-
dardized prototyping board with clearly labeled I/O pins (fig. 10.4). The board in-
cludes a wide variety of GPIOs, analog-to-digital converter (ADC) channels, and
serial communication interfaces, all of which are accessible via the development
environment.
Figure 10.4: ESP32 Chip mounted on a development board
Unlike simpler ADC chips such as the MCP3008, the ESP32 is capable of handling
both analog and digital signals directly. This eliminates the need for extra compo-
nents and allows more complex logic - including sensor-specific calibration, filtering,
and communication - to be handled locally on the microcontroller. The ESP32 also
has built-in support for Wi-Fi and Bluetooth, though in our machine only the USB
serial interface is used.
The microcontroller runs simple firmware written in Arduino-style C++, and the struc-
ture of the code is designed to be modular and easy to extend. This setup allows
us to centralize all sensor data acquisition - including temperature and pH signals -
and forward that data to the Raspberry Pi for further processing or logging.
10.4.1
Communication
Communication between the ESP32 and the Raspberry Pi is handled via a USB
serial connection. This approach offers both power and data transfer through a
single cable, keeping the setup clean and easy to debug. Though we still might
need to figure out later how much power is being drawn, to make sure we don’t burn
anything on the Raspberry Pi.
Data is sent from the ESP32 to the Raspberry Pi using a JSON-formatted string.
JSON (JavaScript Object Notation) is a lightweight data-interchange format that
uses key-value pairs to structure information in a readable and flexible way. In-
stead of sending raw, comma-separated values, each piece of information is la-
beled, making it easy to extract relevant data programmatically without relying on a
fixed structure.
In our case, three main commands are implemented on the ESP32:
– GET_PH: returns the analog voltage from the pH sensor, e.g. {"ph_voltage": 1.742}
86
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
==PAGE:97==
– GET_TEMPS: returns readings from all connected temperature sensors, e.g.
{"temp_cd": 24.38, "temp_7c": 23.91, "temp_4a": 24.00,
"temp_22": 24.12, "temp_DF": 23.87}
– PING: returns a simple response (OK) to check that the ESP32 is responsive.
These clear and self-descriptive formats simplify debugging and future develop-
ment. They also allow the Raspberry Pi to selectively access only the data it needs,
and to ignore or extend fields without breaking the communication structure.
If an unrecognized command is received, the ESP32 responds with an error mes-
sage: {"error":"Unknown command"}
This JSON-based approach contributes to a modular and scalable communication
layer between microcontroller and host, so future payloads can easily be added.
10.5
Temperature sensor
To monitor water temperature throughout the system, we selected the DS18B20 dig-
ital temperature sensor (fig. 10.5). This sensor offers several practical benefits, in-
cluding waterproofing, digital output, and a simple communication interface. It uses
the OneWire protocol, which allows multiple sensors to communicate over a single
data line, along with power and ground. In our case, this enabled us to connect five
sensors on the same wire (fig. 10.6), reducing wiring complexity significantly.
Figure 10.5: One DS18B20 connected to
an ESP32
Figure 10.6: Five sensors connected to a
protoboard and the to the ESP32
While it would be technically possible to connect these sensors directly to the Rasp-
berry Pi’s GPIO, we decided to offload this task to the ESP32. This microcontroller
already handles the analog pH signal acquisition and provides a natural location
for additional sensor integration. Delegating the temperature reading to the ESP32
keeps analog and digital I/O responsibilities cleanly separated and reduces the soft-
ware overhead on the Pi.
Each DS18B20 sensor is powered with 3.3V like the ESP32, which fits comfortably
within their operating voltage range of 3.0-5.5V. The data line requires a 4.7 kΩ pull-
up resistor, which is connected to the same 3.3V rail. This resistor is essential for
stable communication on the OneWire bus.
10.5.1
OneWire Addressing and Identification
One unique characteristic of the DS18B20 is that each sensor has a factory-assigned
64-bit address, making it possible to identify and communicate with each sensor in-
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
87
==PAGE:98==
dividually, even when connected to the same data line. In our setup, all five sensors
were connected to a single pin on the ESP32.
To determine which physical sensor corresponded to each digital address, we per-
formed a manual identification procedure. After scanning the OneWire bus and
listing the detected addresses (fig. 10.7), we monitored the temperature readings
while physically holding one sensor at a time. This allowed us to match the rise
in temperature to the specific address printed in the terminal output. For easier
tracking, we labeled each sensor by the last two digits/characters of its address (fig.
10.8).
Figure 10.7:
OneWire readout of ad-
dresses on the bus
Figure 10.8: Each of the temperature sen-
sors gets named for identification
This method allowed us to confidently assign each temperature sensor to its in-
tended physical location in the system. The sensor we used for the water bath
control, for example, could then be consistently associated with its specific probe
address - eliminating guesswork and ensuring accurate feedback for heating con-
trol.
Although the DS18B20 sensors are factory-calibrated (we assume), the manufac-
turer does not specify the exact reference conditions or individual tolerances used
during that calibration. As a result, small variations between sensors may still oc-
cur.
For applications requiring tighter temperature consistency, it is possible to
implement software-based calibration by comparing the sensor readings against
a known, accurate temperature reference - such as a laboratory-grade thermome-
ter or a precision-controlled water bath. This correction factor can then be applied
dynamically in code to improve sensor alignment and ensure higher measurement
fidelity across the system.
10.5.2
Custom PCB (Protoboard)
To simplify the wiring and provide a clean foundation for integrating the tempera-
ture sensors, we assembled a small protoboard. All five DS18B20 sensors were
soldered directly onto this board, and a single 4.7 kΩ pull-up resistor was added to
the shared data line to ensure stable OneWire communication (fig. 10.9). This ap-
proach allowed us to consolidate all temperature inputs into one modular and easily
serviceable unit. It also provided a convenient location to connect all relevant signal
lines to the ESP32. By centralizing the connections on the protoboard, we created
a robust intermediate step toward a future fully integrated PCB, while still keeping
the system flexible for debugging and iteration.
10.6
pH-measurements - PH4502C
To build a system that could realistically compete with existing commercial solutions,
one of the important subsystems was the ability to measure pH accurately and reli-
88
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
==PAGE:99==
Figure 10.9: All temperatures connected and a pull-up resistor on the whole dataline
ably. After evaluating various options, we decided to work with a modular amplifier
board known as PH4502C, paired with a standard pH electrode. This solution is
widely available and cost-effective making it suitable for early prototyping.
The PH4502C module acts as an interface between the high-impedance analog
signal from the pH probe and the ADC (Analog-to-Digital Converter) of the ESP32.
Its main function is to amplify and condition the raw voltage signal from the pH probe,
which would otherwise be too weak and unstable for direct reading. The module also
uses a BNC connector, which is also the same type of connector on the pH-probes
already being used by Novonesis. Implementation of their preferred type can then
easily be done.
The module outputs a continuous analog voltage signal that reflects the current pH
level of the liquid the probe is immersed in. This analog signal is read directly by
the ESP32 using one of its ADC channels. From there, the measured voltage can
be interpreted in software, visualized, and eventually calibrated to match real pH
values.
Using an off-the-shelf interface like the PH4502C allowed us to focus on integrat-
ing the signal acquisition pipeline rather than designing custom analog electronics.
While it is not a lab-grade solution, we hope it offers sufficient precision and re-
peatability for proof-of-concept testing, especially when paired with proper calibra-
tion techniques. Then tests can be done where it is compared one-to-one with their
current setup to test reliability.
10.6.1
Hard calibration
Before a pH module can provide reliable readings, it must undergo a basic hardware
calibration - referred to here as hard calibration. This is performed by adjusting the
onboard potentiometer on the PH4502C amplifier module.
The idea behind hard calibration is to define a known reference point that aligns the
voltage output with a neutral pH level (pH 7). This is achieved by short-circuiting the
probe input (fig. 10.10) - connecting the internal and external terminals of the BNC
connector using a small jumper wire - and then adjusting the potentiometer so that
the output voltage reads mid-scaleA.61.
Since we are using an ESP32 (which reads voltages from 0V to 3.3V and has a 12-bit
ADC resolution of 4096 steps), the target reference voltage is 1.65V, corresponding
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
89
==PAGE:100==
Figure 10.10: Inner and outer part of the BNC connector is shorted then tuned to 1.65V
to the midpoint of the measurable range. This value represents pH 7, and at this
point, the amplifier outputs zero millivolts from the probe input.
To assist with this process, the ESP32 continuously reads analog input using the
following function:
1
float readPH() {
2
int raw = analogRead(PH_PIN);
3
return (raw / 4095.0) * 3.3;
4
}
Listing 10.1: Arduino-style pH reading function
By observing the voltage in the serial monitor, we manually adjusted the potentiome-
ter until the reading stabilized around 1.65V. This step sets the baseline, ensuring
that future pH readings are centered properly for further calibration and conversion.
10.6.2
Soft calibration
Soft calibration is the next step in aligning raw voltage readings with actual pH val-
ues. Unlike hard calibration, which sets a fixed zero point, soft calibration involves
measuring the sensor’s response to known buffer solutions and computing the re-
lationship between pH and voltage - commonly referred to as the pH step size.
To do this, the probe is immersed in standardized buffer solutions (e.g., pH 4.01,
6.86, and 9.18) and allowed to stabilize. Once stable, the voltage corresponding
to each buffer is recorded just as seen on the iCinac. These data points are then
used to calculate a linear regression, which models how the sensor’s voltage output
changes with pH.
Although this process has not yet been fully completed, the system is already pre-
pared for testing. The firmware supports dynamic voltage capture, and the neces-
sary buffer solutions are available. Once completed, the result will be a conversion
function that allows real-time translation from voltage to calibrated pH.
This approach will also allow us to verify linearity across the usable range of the
sensor, evaluate the quality of the probe, and potentially compute the R2 value of
the fit for assessing measurement reliability.
90
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
==PAGE:101==
10.6.3
Visualisation
To visualize the collected pH and temperature data, we use the Matplotlib library
- a plotting toolkit in Python. It allows us to generate live, real-time plots that up-
date continuously as new data is received from the ESP32. During development
and testing, this live feedback has been invaluable for monitoring system behavior,
verifying incoming pH voltage and spotting anomalies on the temperature sensors.
In our current setup, the live graph displays data within a sliding time window (fig.
10.11) - typically showing the last hour of measurements during machine tuning and
test runs. This provides a clear view of trends such as thermal stabilization or sensor
drift over time. For finalized experiments or longer procedures, the graph can easily
be extended to show the entire measurement session, making it a flexible tool both
for real-time observation and post-analysisA.62.
Figure 10.11: Live visualization of data coming into Raspberry Pi
10.7
Data logging
To enable later analysis and traceability, all relevant system data is continuously
logged and stored in a CSV file. This format - Comma-Separated Values - is widely
supported and already in use by Novonesis and is the output from the iCinac. It is
both human-readable and easy to parse using common tools such as Excel, pandas
(Python), or MATLAB, making it a practical choice for storing large datasets.
On the Raspberry Pi, all values measured or calculated during operation are written
to this file. This includes both raw sensor inputs (like pH voltage and temperature
readings) and internally derived values (such as PID output or heater state (fig.
10.8.2)). This data forms the basis for post-test analysis, debugging for us, and
performance evaluation. The structure also allows easy extension - additional fields
can be added at any time without disrupting the logging routine.
An example of the current data log format is shown below:

10.8
Waterbath controller
To maintain a stable temperature in the water bath during testing, we needed both
a heating element and accurate temperature sensing. As mentioned earlier, the
heating is provided by a large silicone heating pad mounted beneath the metal tray.
To monitor temperature behavior within the system, five temperature sensors were
placed in random positions inside the water bath insert, specifically inside the falcon
tubes in the holder (fig. 10.12).
To enhance heat distribution, a small circulation pump was also also used. It draws
water from the surface and pushes it across the heated base, increasing the effective
thermal exchange rate and helping to eliminate temperature gradients across the
container.
10.8.1
Bang-Bang Controller - Initial Version
The first version of the control system was implemented as a simple bang-bang con-
troller in Python. This controller turns the heater on when the temperature falls below
a defined lower limit and switches it off again when it exceeds the upper threshold. In
our case, the target temperature was set to 40°C, and an initial tolerance of ±0.3°C
was used. We did choose the the temperature sensor CD (black in the figures) as
92
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
==PAGE:103==
feedback for the controller.
During the first test run, starting from room temperature (~24°C), the system was
able to heat the water to 40°C in approximately 50 minutes. However, due to the
thermal inertia of the heating pad, significant overshoot was observed. Even after
the heater was turned off, residual heat continued to transfer into the water, pushing
the temperature well above 40°C. The result was a slow and repetitive oscillation,
as the controller would toggle the heater on and off too late to maintain a stable
temperature (fig. 10.13.
Figure 10.13: First version of the bang-bang controller
The tolerance band was eventually removed to reduce fluctuation, which did help
somewhat. We were able to stabilize the temperature within a range of ±0.5°C10.15,
but this was still not ideal for precision testing.
Figure 10.14: Second version of the bang-bang controller with tolerances removed
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
93
==PAGE:104==
10.8.2
PID Control
To improve accuracy and eliminate the overshoot caused by the bang-bang ap-
proach, we turned to PID control (Proportional-Integral-Derivative). One team mem-
ber had previous experience researching PID systems in a ventilation control project,
and we decided to apply similar principles here.
A PID controller continuously adjusts the heater power based on three components:
– KP (Proportional): Reacts to the current temperature error.
– KI (Integral): Accumulates past error to eliminate steady-state deviation.
– KD (Derivative): Anticipates future error based on rate of change.
Initially, several tuning attempts were made manually, but the temperature still tended
to stabilize at 42-43°C - a clear indication of overcompensation or incorrect gain set-
tings. After some guessing and a lot of waiting we found some parameters that fitted
okay. Still we wanted to dial it in correctly.
Figure 10.15: PID control with semi-tuned parameters, therefore still overshoot. Also
shows the PID output that determines when to turn on heater
10.8.3
Ziegler-Nichols Tuning
To systematically find suitable PID parameters, we applied the Ziegler-Nichols tun-
ing method[28]. This involved first setting KI and KD to zero and gradually increas-
ing KP until the system began to oscillate consistently around the target tempera-
ture. The ultimate gain (KU) and the oscillation period (TU) were then used in the
Ziegler-Nichols formulas to compute all three PID gains:
Kp = 0.60 · Ku
(10.1)
Ki = 1.2 · Ku
Tu
(10.2)
Kd = 3 · Ku · Tu
40
(10.3)
94
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
==PAGE:105==
In the following graph it can be seen that the temperature was fluctuating quite a bit
in the beginning, where it was run for multiple hours. The oscillation period could
then be found so we could tune the other parameters. To show it in detail we only
focus on temperature sensor (fig. CD10.16).
Figure 10.16: High oscillation in the beginning, but after tuning, it smoothed out
After implementing these calculated parameters, the PID-controlled system was
able to hold a target temperature of 40.00°C with only minor deviation (e.g., dipping
to 39.94°C at times (fig. 10.17), which matches the resolution limit of the sensor).
This level of stability was deemed highly satisfactory for the precision required in
our testing setupA.70.
Figure 10.17: Focus on the part where the parameters got dialled in
Through iteration and controller tuning, we transitioned from a basic but unstable
bang-bang controller to a much more precise PID-based solution.
This change
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
95
==PAGE:106==
significantly improved the reliability of the water bath and ensured the consistency
needed for calibration and measurement tasks. The final PID implementation be-
haves similarly to commercial immersion thermostats, such as those used in Novone-
sis’ lab setups.
10.9
3D printer board
After successfully setting up of the 3D printer board for motor control, and devel-
oping a controller that translates Python commands into serial-based movement in-
structions, we began to observe several limitations in the setup that impacted both
performance and usability.
One of the first issues we noticed was the noise generated by the board. When
the stepper motors were idle, they emitted a high-pitched whining sound. This is a
known issue in low-cost stepper motor driver implementations, where poor-quality
components and outdated driver chips leave the motors under constant holding volt-
age, creating audible vibrations. In a typical working environment, especially a lab-
oratory, this kind of high-frequency noise can be quite unpleasant (It already was in
our small office).
Furthermore, the board was also relatively loud during motion. The type of waveform
generated by the motor drivers lacked smoothness, causing a form of mechanical
resonance often referred to as ”salmon skin” in the 3D printing world. This phe-
nomenon, which results from uneven microstepping or low-quality current control,
is commonly seen in older or budget 3D printer setups.
We also found the homing process to be cumbersome. Each endstop had to be
manually wired back to the board, and the Anet A8 controller only supported three
endstop inputs - one for each axis. Our system, however, requires at least four:
one for each X, Y, axis plus one for the calibration tray and an additional one for the
pH-probe holder’s own home position. This meant that from a purely I/O standpoint,
the board was already at its limit.
Another important missing feature was the ability to detect step loss or collision.
With standard stepper motor control (as on this board), there is no feedback loop
- meaning that if the system skips steps or hits a mechanical stop, the controller
remains unaware. This introduces potential failure modes where the probes may
be misaligned without the system knowing, compromising test reliability and safety.
All of these factors led us to re-evaluate our motor control solution. While the 3D
printer board served well for early prototyping and allowed us to explore motion
planning and integration quickly, it became clear that a more robust and feature-rich
alternative would be needed for the final version.
10.9.1
New board
To address the limitations of the original 3D printer board, we selected a new, more
advanced motion controller: the MKS Monster8 V2 (fig. 10.18). This board is de-
signed with flexibility and modularity in mind, clearly targeted at makers and ad-
vanced hobbyists. It provides an extensive set of features that open up new possi-
bilities for our system architecture.
The board supports a wide range of communication protocols, including I2C, UART,
and CAN, making it highly compatible with our existing Raspberry Pi-based infras-
tructure. It also includes power output for a 5V rail, which allows the Raspberry Pi
96
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
==PAGE:107==
Figure 10.18: MKS Monster8 V2.0 board withouth TMC2209 drivers installed
to be powered directly from the board - reducing cabling complexity. Additionally,
it offers connections for external fans, addressable LED strips, WiFi modules, and
either onboard or external stepper motor drivers.
One of the biggest advantages of this board is that it supports both Marlin and Klipper
firmware. For the current stage of the project, we plan to continue using a custom
version of Marlin firmware, as it integrates well with our current control code and al-
lows reuse of existing G-code-based movement commands. In the future, the option
to switch to Klipper could provide increased speed and smoother motion control.
Unlike the Anet A8 board, the Monster8 offers much better I/O flexibility, higher qual-
ity components, and room for expansion. While integration and firmware flashing
are still pending at the time of writing, we see this board as a major step forward in
enabling a more reliable, quieter, and maintainable motion platform.
10.9.2
Stepper drivers TMC2209
One of the key features of the new board is its compatibility with TMC2209 step-
per drivers, which offer significant advantages over the basic drivers used in our
previous setup.
TMC2209 drivers provide current sensing and stall detection, enabling features such
as sensorless homing. This allows the system to detect mechanical end positions
without needing physical limit switches. When a stepper motor encounters resis-
tance above a defined threshold - such as hitting the end of an axis - the driver
registers a stall condition, which can be used to trigger a home event or full stop.
This has several benefits:
– Fewer wires: We can eliminate the need for dedicated endstop switches and
their associated cabling.
– Improved feedback: The system can detect unexpected collisions or step loss
during operation.
– Better quality: Quiter operation while in use and also while holding still leveling
up the comfort.
Additionally, the TMC2209 drivers are much quieter and smoother in operation.
They use more advanced stepping waveforms compared to older drivers, eliminat-
ing the high-pitched whine and vibration we experienced with the previous board.
This contributes to a more professional and less disruptive user experience.
Great documentation for both the MKS Monster8 and TMC2209 is available online,
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
97
==PAGE:108==
which greatly simplifies setup and troubleshooting. With these drivers, we expect
improved reliability, accuracy, and a more elegant control system overall.
10.10
Architecture diagram - Electronics
To provide a clear overview of how the system’s electronic components are intercon-
nected, we have included a final architecture diagram. This illustration maps out the
full electronic structure of the machine, including power supply routing, microcon-
troller connections, sensor inputs, motor outputs, and communication links between
subsystems. The diagram offers a visual summary of the design decisions made
throughout the project and reflects how the modular elements - such as the Rasp-
berry Pi, ESP32, relay boards, sensors, and stepper motor drivers - are integrated
into a cohesive and functional system. Please see appendix A.73.
10.11
Changes to sample size
Late in the design process, Kim Olsson from Novonesis, that there was a wish from
novonesis that would like the system to also be able to use the bottles used in
the current setup. To accommodate the wish we brainstormed a possible solution,
and found one that would not require a full redesign of the system. The reason
behind this was based on a need from one of the departments within Novonesis.
This department needed the remnants of the samples after the experiment ended
for further testing. When performing these additional tests, the samples are diluted
10-100.000 times over two to three steps. And for these tests, they need more that
50ml of sample.
Figure 10.19: System with module for bottles.
With our system being modular, the solution was found by inserting a bottle holder
into the water bath used for testing. This fixture then allowed the use of regular
milk bottles by ensuring their placement in the bath. Due to the size of the bottles
however, it was not possible to fit all 24 bottles, but only six. A limiting factor resulting
in the six bottles was the distance between the pH-probes. With the probes being
spaced 40mm x 40mm apart and the bottles being 65mm in diameter, there was
simply not enough distance between the pH-probes. This could of couse be solved
using another fixture for the pH-probes, but this would (at this point) mean longer
98
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
==PAGE:109==
changeover times and result in increased hands-on time. With this design, however,
the lab technician only has to remove the probes not used, and place the bottle
holder in the system as shown in figure 10.19. Once this is gone the system will
function as normal. (Reference worksheets ??
10.12
Novonesis presentation
Towards the end of the project, we were invited to Novonesis to present our work-
ing prototype, concept, and system design. The presentation was held for approxi-
mately 10 participants, including Kim Olsson, colleagues from his team, and several
of his managers. This session served both as a technical demonstration and a feed-
back forum to evaluate the industrial relevance of our solution.
The reception was strongly positive. The team at Novonesis expressed genuine
surprise and enthusiasm over the progress made within just four months. They
were particularly impressed by the level of integration achieved.
Figure 10.20: Visit at Novonesis
Figure 10.21: Visit at Novonesis
In addition to their positive response, we also received constructive feedback and
valuable insights. This included suggestions for areas to refine further, potential use
cases beyond the originally scoped environment, and ideas for making the system
even more user-friendly in a lab context. Several attendees highlighted how such
a system could simplify their daily workflow, especially in terms of calibration time,
sample handling, and automation.
Overall, the meeting served as both a validation of our technical direction and a
motivational conclusion to the development phase. It confirmed that our approach
is aligned with real-world industrial needs - a strong indication that the system has
potential for further development and practical use beyond the scope of the thesis.

10.13
Evaluation of sprint
After discussions with Kim from Novonesis, it was decided that the system should
both be capable of internal and external data management. This would allow the
system to both collect and handle data on its own, while also making it possible to
use the existing equipment (iCinac).
Sprint 4 marked the final and most intensive phase of the project. While it was highly
productive, it also came with its own set of challenges - particularly related to the
delayed arrival of key components ordered from overseas suppliers. These delays
compressed the integration phase significantly, putting pressure on the timeline.
However, the preparatory work done during Sprint 3 proved crucial in mitigating this
issue. By laying out much of the code structure and mechanical planning in advance,
we were able to move quickly once the parts arrived.
During this sprint, a wide range of electronic components were brought into use.
These included the new MKS Monster8 board, sensor interfaces like the PH4502C,
the ESP32-based sensing unit, and the full sensor suite for temperature and pH
measurements. Each component was individually tested, and their integration into
the system was handled quite easy thanks to the modular hardware and code struc-
ture we had developed.
Mechanically, several final decisions were made as well. The structure of the probe
holder, the positioning system, and the tubing and pump configurations were all
finalized and assembled into a working prototype. Combined with the firmware up-
dates, communication protocols, and the addition of logging and visualization tools,
the result was, given the circumstances, a quite good result.
Despite the time constraints, Sprint 4 was a success to us - both technically and
as a validation of the project’s feasibility. It was the sprint where everything came
together, and it demonstrated that the system not only worked in parts, but also as
a whole. Now refinements only needs to be done before the defense.

10.15
Conclusion
Sprint 4 marked the culmination of our development efforts - and served as a real test
of how well our planning, prototyping, and technical integration had been executed.
The sprint was intense, especially as we awaited delayed shipments of critical com-
ponents ordered from abroad. Despite this, the preliminary structure laid down in
previous sprints allowed us to integrate, test, and finalize large parts of the system
within a short time frame.
From the start, this project was designed not only as a technical challenge but
also as an application of systematic product development. Our process has been
grounded in the framework presented by Ulrich and Eppinger in “Product Design
and Development” [2], where development flows through structured phases such
as planning, concept development, system-level design, and prototyping. While
our approach was sprint-based, each sprint effectively mirrored a phase from this
model - combining rapid iteration with disciplined decision-making and validation.
As described in our Methods section, we focused on continuously refining the con-
cept through early functional models, prototyping key subcomponents (like the pH
system, motion control, and fluid handling), and converging toward an integrated
solution. The use of sprints enabled us to structure our work into manageable mile-
stones, while also ensuring that each cycle contributed directly to the next. In that
sense, Sprint 4 was not just the endpoint, but the natural outcome of a methodical,
polytechnical development process.
Technically, the system now features a fully integrated architecture spanning sensor
networks, microcontroller communication, motion control, a water bath regulation
loop, and real-time data logging - all supported by a modular and scalable software
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
101
==PAGE:112==
Figure 10.23: The version of the machine in the end of sprint 4
backbone. Mechanically, the machine is complete enough to demonstrate realis-
tic workflows, and is already close to what could be field-tested in a real industrial
setting.
From a learning perspective, the project has been both challenging and highly re-
warding. It allowed us to work across domains - from biology and electronics to con-
trol systems and mechanical engineering - and to develop a deeper understanding
of real-world system integration. Even though some fine-tuning remains and not all
functions are yet production-ready, we think we have successfully delivered on our
main objective: building a fully functional and promising prototype within a limited
time frame.
Importantly, the completion of the report does not mean the end of development. Our
engagement with the project remains high, and one or both of us plan to continue
refining the system through a special course in the coming semester. The ultimate
goal is to prepare it for real-world testing at Novonesis, possibly within a GMO1
laboratory setup.
In short, Sprint 4 concluded the first cycle of a structured product development effort
- one that followed academic theory, applied engineering, and produced tangible
results that we believe can be carried forward into the next phase of innovation.
To visually summarize the result of Sprint 4, the image below shows the current state
of the machine as it stands at the end of the development phase. It brings together all
the core components. Including the mechanical structure, probe positioning system,
water bath and electronics into one cohesive prototype. While there is still room for
refinement, the system as shown reflects a functional and integrated solution that
embodies the design goals set at the beginning of the project.

Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
==PAGE:113==
11
Discussion
11.1
Challenges
11.1.1
Budget and time
One of the significant challenges we encountered was the reduction in our antici-
pated budget. This required us to seek more cost-effective solutions for sourcing
components, leading us to order parts from AliExpress. While this approach helped
maintain our budget, it also introduced longer lead times, as many suppliers were
based in China. As a result, the delays in receiving components meant that we had
less time to test and troubleshoot, which ultimately impacted the overall progress
and the amount we were able to achieve within the original timeline.
11.1.2
Budget and Testing Constraints
Another challenge we faced due to the limited budget was the necessity of making
certain compromises in our design choices to ensure that we could test the system
before the project’s conclusion. For instance, we initially selected a pump for the
cleaning station that ultimately proved to be less powerful than needed to clean all
the probes simultaneously. This decision was made to ensure that we had a func-
tional system to test within the project’s timeframe. In other circumstances, we might
have chosen to scale down the system or select different components, but the prior-
ity was to have a testable prototype ready in time. This approach, however, added
complexity to the system, such as the need to incorporate a motorized mechanism
to move the cleaning station between different rows of probes.
11.1.3
Prototyping vs. Manufacturing
The transition from prototyping to manufacturing presents a unique set of challenges
and considerations. During the prototyping phase, the focus was on flexibility, rapid
iteration, and testing various design concepts. For example, many components
were 3D printed, allowing for quick modifications and cost-effective testing. How-
ever, when shifting towards manufacturing, considerations such as material dura-
bility, production scalability, and regulatory compliance become paramount.
Another crucial factor in the context of a laboratory environment is the cleanability
of components. Prototyping often prioritizes functionality and speed of assembly,
which may not fully address the ease of cleaning and sterilization required in a lab
setting. In the manufacturing phase, it becomes essential to select materials and
designs that can withstand repeated cleaning and sterilization processes without
degrading. This ensures that the final product is not only durable and efficient but
also compliant with laboratory hygiene standards.
11.2
Validation trough experiments
While we are used to validate design through iterative work and prototyping, in this
project we tried something new. At this project was about automation within life
science biology, some of our validations had to be done in a biology laboratory. This
was a new experience for the both of us, having not done experiments at this scale
before. None the less it was a great opportunity to get a better understanding of
what the day as a lab-technician look. Trying some of the equipment that is also
used in process where are trying to change really showed us why our product is
relevant.
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
103
==PAGE:114==
104
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
==PAGE:115==
12
Future Work
12.1
Finishing Sub-systems
Being a designed in a short time frame, many of the functions and sub-system are
far from finished. Most of them still need more validation, ensuring functionality,
quality and reliability.
12.2
Components
At the end of this project, the final product is made from 3D printed parts, Bosch
Rexroth Aluminum profiles and cheap electronic components. For an iterative de-
sign process this is great, but looking ahead, some of these components will have
to be replaced.
12.2.1
3D printed parts
Being able to quickly make changes to a component is an important part of prototyp-
ing and product development. This freedom allows designers to move quickly and
discover issues or challenges, without having to wait for an expensive machined
part, just to realize that a tolerance is off or two holes are placed 5mm to close to
each other. This is some of the advantages, and in this project it has been indis-
pensable, for some of the parts upwards of ten versions have been designed and
tested. Some might say that it is a waste, but often, a CAD model cannot show how
small a tolerance is in the real world or whether a joint is rigid enough. These things
have to be tested, and it cannot be done using paper or cardboard.
But the reality is that at some point, the printed parts have to be replaced, and often
with steel parts. Especially when designing for laboratories, PLA is not a viable
material. Here cleaning is done with liquids that would destroy the printed parts, and
therefore more resilient parts will be needed in the future. Both to ensure rigidity but
also to ensure compliance with the regulations related to the use environments.
12.2.2
Aluminum profiles
The Bosch Rexroth Aluminum profiles used in the system are again great when
prototyping, and are widely used in laboratory and production environments to build
test rigs and production setups. The profiles offers high levels of modularity and
customization, but this can also be one of the downsides. If our system is to be
mass produced or at least in a bigger series than one or two systems, it might be
beneficial to make a specific structures with holes in the right locations and supports
where they are needed. This will ensure faster assembly as the person putting it
together won’t have to measure before installing all the parts.
12.2.3
Cheap Components
Being a master project, the main focus from the beginning was to make the system
work. With a limited budget this sometimes means buying cheaper parts. These
parts work, but it is not always guaranteed for how long or how well. And that is
fine, at least for now. Looking ahead, it will be important to test and validate all the
components, which should reveal any reliability issues. For a prototype, a lower
quality linear rod and bearings is fine, it does not matter if it binds up a little bit, it is
annoying, but as long as it works it is okay. When manufacturing for a costumer this,
however, is not acceptable. When reaching this points, parts need to be reliable and
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
105
==PAGE:116==
well functioning. This will mean less component malfunctions, resulting in less down
town, and most companies are willing to pay more for reliable products, especially
in situations where they have to run every day, and short periods of downtime can
result in big delays.
12.2.4
Machine Directive
Finally it will be important to ensure that the components and the overall machine
comply with any regional requirements, and especially safety requirements. In Den-
mark, one of the main directives to follow is called Maskindirektivet (the machine
directive), and is a list of requirements, that need to be complied with in order to be
used at Danish job sites. This is to ensure that nobody is hurt either as a product of
using the machine or interacting with products related to the machine (e.g.: food or
beverages). Some of the requirements are to make sure users cannot access mov-
ing gears while the system is in operation, not to have any exposed sharp edges
that operators can cut themselves on, and of cause when working with consum-
ables, that the system does not contaminate it. These are all very relevant for our
system, and it is therefore important to ensure compliance.
12.3
iCinac
Another thing we want to look at for future development involves enabling compat-
ibility with the iCinac system, which remains a widely accepted standard in the fer-
mentation monitoring industry. Instead of replacing the iCinac entirely, we could also
integrate it as an optional module in the workflow. In this setup, our machine would
handle physical movement of pH-probes, including automated rinsing, positioning
into calibration buffers, and repositioning after measurements, while the iCinac re-
tains responsibility for signal acquisition and interpretation. To achieve this, we will
need to investigate whether iCinac offers a usable API or external control interface.
Ideally, communication would be bidirectional - for example, the iCinac could send
a request to calibrate in buffer pH 4, and our system would respond by positioning
the electrodes accordingly and confirming readiness. Such a hybrid system would
offer the best of both worlds: automation of handling and preparation combined with
an already validated and regulatory-compliant measurement platform.
12.4
Cable management
An important aspect to address in future development is cable management, particu-
larly if iCinac integration is pursued. Since the iCinac system still relies on individual
physical pH-probe connections, it would reintroduce a significant number of cables
- one per channel - into the system. In that case, we would likely need to add an
additional cable carrier on the opposite side of the motion assembly to organize and
protect the signal wires as they move with the probe array. For our own system,
however, there is a clear opportunity to streamline this. By placing the signal acqui-
sition interface directly on the moving probe array, we can locally digitize the sensor
data and send it down to the Raspberry Pi via a single digital communication cable.
This would drastically simplify the wiring, reduce mechanical complexity, and im-
prove serviceability. Future iterations of the hardware should take this into account
to optimize both functionality and maintainability.
12.5
Cleaning station
One of the more significant improvements we plan to make in future iterations is a
redesign of the cleaning station, transitioning from the current moving setup to a sta-
tionary configuration. In the current version, we encountered limitations in achieving
106
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
==PAGE:117==
sufficient water pressure at the spray nozzles. To reach the desired cleaning per-
formance, we would have needed more powerful and therefore more expensive
components - something that was outside the scope of our available budget. One
alternative considered was the use of solenoid valves to wash the probes sequen-
tially rather than moving the cleaning head. This would allow for efficient redirection
of water pressure without requiring movement, but the necessary valves were not
available in time once we discovered the issue. In a finalized version of the sys-
tem, the most straightforward solution would be to invest in a high-pressure pump
and possibly a compressed air unit capable of cleaning all probes simultaneously.
Though, we have also heard from Novonesis, that they have integrated pressure
lines running in the lab. The machine could then be hooked up to this instead, but it
might not be the same scenario for all, so it needs further investigation. This would
not only simplify the mechanics but also increase the robustness and reproducibility
of the cleaning process.
12.6
User interface design
One of the key areas we aim to improve in future development is the user interface.
From our conversations with lab technicians and researchers in the DALSA labo-
ratory, we learned that current systems - such as those from Hamilton and Tecan -
often suffer from one of two issues: either they are highly flexible but difficult to use,
or they are somewhat user-friendly but lack the full functionality required for daily
workflows.
To address this, we plan to develop a simple and intuitive user interface for the
system. The goal is to make the machine easily accessible even to users with limited
technical background - such as many biologists, who are often hesitant to adopt new
automated equipment due to unfamiliarity or concerns about complexity. We want
to reduce the integration and learning time as much as possible, so that users can
feel confident operating the machine with minimal training.
The long-term vision is to build a touchscreen-based interface directly on the de-
vice or offer remote access through a connected tablet or web-based interface.
This would make the system more self-contained and further reduce the need for
command-line interaction or separate scripts. But to fully settle on a solution we
need to do more research in this area to figure out what is needed.
12.7
Data handling
Another area we intend to explore further is automated data handling. In the future,
we want the system to not only log data but also process it - either live on the ma-
chine or as part of the final output file generated after an experiment. Currently, we
do not have full insight into the exact type of post-processing Novonesis performs
beyond basic plotting and graphical interpretation. This makes it necessary to con-
duct additional research to understand their analytical workflows, so we can develop
features that align with their needs and potentially automate those steps. Ideally,
most of the data processing could be handled locally on the Raspberry Pi, but this
will require further testing to evaluate both feasibility and performance.
12.8
Up-scaling
In section 6.4, the automation levels were analysed, and here it was concluded
that within this project, a lower level than what actually scored the highest would
be the goal. This choice was made based on the feasibility and complexity of the
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
107
==PAGE:118==
higher levels (that actually scored higher) being too much for a project of this scale.
But when looking ahead, these might become relevant. Especially level 9 becomes
interesting, with the ability to run multiple sets of experiments. Talking to Kim Olsson
from Novonesis, needing a cleaning and calibration system for each sample set
would not be cost efficient enough, which makes sense, because why would you
want to have three or four identical system next to each other, if you could have one
big system that could handle the same amount of experiments but with only one
calibration and cleaning station. In the process of up-scaling the system, it would be
necessary to create a bigger system, based on the already designed subfunctions
and adding the ability to test multiple sets of samples at once.
108
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
==PAGE:119==
13
Conclusion
13.1
Time plan
Throughout the project, the time plan played a central role in structuring our work.
We created and maintained the Gantt chart in OpenProject and referenced daily -
both digitally and through printed copies at our workspace. The plan was divided
into sprints, each tied to a milestone, allowing us to always have a clear sense of
direction and progress. One of the strengths of the time plan was that it not only
mapped out the core phases of the development process, but also took external
obligations into account, helping us avoid unnecessary delays.
In practice, we followed the plan closely. Adjustments were minimal - typically shift-
ing a sprint deadline by a day or two - and not significant enough to require updates
to the actual Gantt chart. Some meetings, fieldwork, and lab sessions were sched-
uled on short notice and therefore not explicitly listed in the plan, but the timeline
still provided enough flexibility to accommodate them. Overall, the time plan proved
both realistic and useful. It gave us the structure needed to manage a complex,
interdisciplinary project and helped us make the most of the time we had available.
We consider it a key factor in being able to deliver a complete and integrated proto-
type within the time frame. A final version can be found in the appendix A.72.
13.2
Reflection on Learning Objectives
The design of the system has, to the greatest extent possible, adhered to Learn-
ing Objective 1 from Section 1, with a strong focus on applying user-centric design
methods. Although the system is primarily mechanical in nature, considerable effort
has been made to integrate user, customer, and stakeholder perspectives into the
design process, in alignment with Learning Objective 2.
A functional prototype was built and tested to the highest feasible degree within the
available project timeframe, addressing Learning Objectives 3 and 4. Furthermore,
the design has been documented to a substantial extent in accordance with Learning
Objective 5. However, since the final design has not yet been completed, detailed
assembly drawings and manuals have not been finalized and may evolve with future
iterations.
While the product is still under development, relevant safety and regulatory consid-
erations from the Danish “Maskindirektivet” have been taken into account, though
additional work will be required to ensure full compliance, as outlined in Learning
Objective 6.
Product architecture principles have been actively used to support a modular and
customizable system configuration, fulfilling Learning Objective 7. Throughout the
project, a variety of methods have been employed to facilitate an iterative, structured
design process, in line with Learning Objectives 8 and 9.
In summary, all learning objectives have been addressed to varying degrees during
the development of the Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification.
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification

==PAGE:120==
13.3
Design Specification
A recurring element throughout this project has been the design specification and
its ongoing evaluation. Design specifications are a critical tool for design engineers,
ensuring that all requirements are addressed and fulfilled throughout the develop-
ment process. In this project, 17 specifications were formulated, covering aspects
such as user ergonomics and compatibility with various liquid containers. Upon re-
viewing the specifications, it is evident that not all have been fully met. However, in
most cases, this is due to limited time for validation rather than design shortcomings.
Specifications 1 and 2, concerning supported volume ranges and container types,
have been fulfilled, with the system accommodating both standard glass bottles and
Falcon tubes. In contrast, Specifications 3, 4, 5, 6, 11, and 12 could not be validated
within the project timeframe but remain in scope for future development sprints and
eventual product testing. Specifications 7 and 9, which relate to cost and sourcing,
cannot yet be evaluated due to the absence of a finalized bill of materials. Specifi-
cation 8 has been fulfilled, as the system supports three buffer solutions in a custom
calibration tray. Specification 10, which involves if the Software should be open-
source, cannot be conclusively assessed at this stage, as it will depend on future
business decisions. Specification 13, regarding heating time, currently meets the
marginal value, though improvements could be made through additional insulation
of the water bath. User ergonomics (14) have been preserved, and the system is
expected to improve walk-away time (15). Finally, the sample capacity (16) meets
the defined marginal requirements, and the system dimensions are considered ideal
(17).

13.4
Final Conclusion
This project has brought together the full spectrum of what it means to develop
a product from concept to functional prototype within a polytechnical engineering
context. Over the course of five months, we have taken an initial idea - rooted in an
industrial need presented by Novonesis - and transformed it into a working system
capable of real-world relevance. From initial research to field studies, from hardware
110
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
==PAGE:121==
prototyping to firmware calibration, from system architecture design to integration
and testing, this project has required a broad and multidisciplinary effort.
Our development process has followed an iterative, sprint-based structure inspired
by established product design methodologies, particularly those described by Ulrich
and Eppinger in Product Design and Development [2]. Each sprint corresponded
roughly to key phases in the product development lifecycle: concept development,
system-level design, detailed design, integration, and validation. This structured ap-
proach gave us a foundation for making informed design decisions while still main-
taining the flexibility to respond to unexpected issues - such as delayed components,
design pivots, or integration challenges.
Throughout the project, prototyping played a central role. Early functional models
of individual subsystems - such as the pH measurement circuit, motion system,
and temperature regulation - allowed us to explore feasibility and uncover practical
limitations. These prototypes gradually converged into a single integrated machine,
tested and tuned to perform real experimental procedures. Much of this was made
possible by accessible tools such as 3D printing, modular electronics, and open-
source software - all of which allowed for rapid development and experimentation.
At its current state, the machine is capable of automated sample handling and pH
measurement, complete with temperature control, real-time data logging, and ex-
pandable communication between its main computing unit and peripheral micro-
controllers. The system’s modular software structure allows for easy modification
and upgrades, while the hardware design - although still in its prototype phase - has
proven to be stable and functional. The fact that we were able to demonstrate the
system and receive positive validation from Novonesis is a strong indicator that the
project is on the right track.
That said, this is not a finished product. Several design criteria - such as full-scale
end-user testing, robustness validation, advanced data processing, and long-term
reliability - remain incomplete, primarily due to time constraints. Additionally, cer-
tain assumptions made during the design process (e.g., sample volume handling,
user interface design, and sensor drift over time) require further investigation before
the system can be confidently deployed in a production laboratory. Some aspects,
such as hard and soft calibration of the pH sensing module, were implemented in a
functional way, but will need to be validated through repeated and extended trials.
Despite these limitations, the outcome represents a significant milestone for us and
product-wise. We have not only built a system that works, but one that is extensible,
reproducible, and built with real-world constraints in mind. The balance between
academic exploration and practical engineering has been central to our work - and
we believe the result reflects both a deep engagement with our discipline and a
strong potential for future application.
Looking forward, our motivation to continue working on the system remains high.
This project has not only challenged our technical skills but also reinforced the value
of cross-disciplinary collaboration, iterative design thinking, and problem-solving un-
der constraint. Whether pursued through a special course, a future thesis, or an
industrial collaboration, we see clear paths for improvement and deployment - po-
tentially leading to a tool that helps automate tedious but essential processes in
bioengineering labs and beyond.
Ultimately, this project has served as a culmination for our time as engineering stu-
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
111
==PAGE:122==
Figure 13.1: Final Concept
112
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
==PAGE:123==
dents at DTU. It has brought together the tools, theories, and methods we have
encountered over five years and tested them in a real, applied context. Time and
budget were limited, but creativity, structure, and persistence carried us through.
And while this chapter is now closing, the journey of this product - and what it could
become - is still very much ongoing.

Project Outline:
På Novonesis/X-Chr. Hansen måler vi rigtig mange forsuringskurver (pH-kurver) når vi skal måle hvor hurtige vores produkter er til at fermentere mælk til youghurt/ost. En
”dims” vi mangler i laboratoriet, og som ikke kan købes, er en multikanals pH-elektrode/multimeter der kan passe ned i en mikrotiterbakke.
Den nuværende industri standard er iCinac systemet som er et stort og ret manuelt system der basalt set står af 32 almindelige pH-elektroder der er koblet til et multimeter.
Det er derfor 32x (antallet af systemer vi har) af pH-elektroder der skal vaskes, vedligeholdes og klargøres hver gang før/efter brug: https://www.kpmanalytics.com/
products/chemical-clinical/icinac-l-a-b-fermentation-monitor-series
Hvad jeg, meget forsimplet, forestiller mig. Er 12-24 af de her elektroder der er sat sammen i en 3D-printet holder der passer ned over en MTP eller DWP, men elektroden i
linket er bare et forslag, der findes måske bedre/billigere: https://www.fishersci.dk/shop/products/orion-9826bn-micro-ph-electrode/10558361#?keyword= der kommer en
hel del forslag ved en hurtig google søgning: https://www.google.com/search?q=micro+pH+glass+electrodes
Fra toppen af mit hoved ville en URS for systemet bl.a. involvere følgende punkter (til inspiration):
·         Skal gerne passe i en 96 eller 384 bakke, en 24- eller sågar en 12- brønds DWP vil også være interessant, og vil nok være det der vil blive arbejdet med til et
indledende PoC projekt – men perspektivet må gerne gå hele vejen (Jeg ved ikke nødvendigvis selv hvordan 96-/384-kanaler ville kunne gøres på en måde der også giver
økonomisk mening da hver af ovenstående micro-pH elektroder koster 5000dkk/stk.
·         Systemet skal (på sigt) indeholde følgende komponenter:
o    Modulet/Holderen med alle pH-electroderne
§  Modulet må gerne være udformet således at elektroderne er beskyttet mod stød/slag ved håndtering da de er meget følsomme.
o    Multimeter-modul til at håndterer data fra elektroderne.
§  Dette behøver ikke være live-data, men kan være målinger hver 5-10 sekund således at dele af opsætningen kan være baseret på kanal-skiftere hvis det gør noget
nemmere. Såfremt at signalerne passer sammen, kan det være at multimeteret fra en iCinac kan bruges indledningsvis til at håndterer input fra kanalerne med.
o    Vaske/Vedligeholdelses/Opbevarings-station (Kan godt være 3 enkelt-stående komponenter:
§  En del af arbejdet er rengøring/vedligehold af de mange elektroder. Almindeligvis skylles de rene med vand og tørres med papir, dette bliver i nogle systemer håndteret
med en ”høj”tryks vandsprøjte efterfulgt af blive blæst tørre.
§  Herudover skal de også gerne kunne kalibreres parallelt, og det skal være simpelt at sikre KCl i elektroderne (hvis nødvendigt).
·         Det skal gerne være pH-glas elektroder der bruges da de er industristandarden.
o    Såfremt der er forskellige pH-måleområder, så er den relevante pH range fra cirka 3.5-8.5.
o    Alternativt kan ISFET overvejes, såfremt det kan påvises at de ikke bliver påvirket af matrix-effekt eller andet.
o    Kemiske optoder har vi erfaringer med fra biolector systemet, og de er ikke egnet til denne type forsuringskurver/QC arbejde.
o    Hvis der bruges andet end glas-elektroder, vil systemet nok kun kunne bruges forsknings-mæssigt, og ikke til QC arbejde.
·         Skal leve op til maskindirektivet.
·         Skal gerne være billigere at bygge/producere end en iCinac.
·         Selve elektrode-modulet skal gerne kunne håndteres af en robotarm/Liquid handler gripper.
