A.1 Novonesis problemdefinition
120
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
==PAGE:131==
Title 
English: Development of automatic pH-measurement system for fermentation. 
Danish: Udvikling af automatisk pH-målesystem til fermentering. 
 
Project description 
In collaboration with the DALSA Lab at DTU and Novonesis (formerly Chr. Hansen and Novozymes), 
our project aims to develop an automatic multi-channel pH measurement system designed for 
fermentation monitoring. Currently, the industry standard for measuring acidiﬁcation curves (pH 
curves) in dairy fermentation processes is the iCinac system, a bulky and manual setup consisting of 
multiple individual pH electrodes connected to a, in simple terms, multimeter. This system requires 
extensive maintenance, including washing, calibration, and preparation of each electrode before and 
after use. 
Our goal is to create a compact and automated alternative: a multi-channel pH measurement module 
that integrates directly into a microtiter plate (MTP) or deep-well plate (DWP). The envisioned system 
consists of 12-24 micro pH electrodes mounted in a 3D-printed holder (to begin with) designed to ﬁt 
standard laboratory plates. This should reduce manual workload while increasing measurement 
efficiency. 
Key technical considerations include: 
• 
Compatibility with 96- or 384-well plates, as well as 12- and 24-well deep-well plates for initial 
proof-of-concept development. 
• 
Electrode protection, ensuring that the delicate pH electrodes are safeguarded from damage 
during handling. 
• 
Data acquisition, does not require real-time measurements but should record pH values at 
intervals (e.g., every 5-10 seconds). The system may utilize channel switchers for efficiency, 
and existing iCinac multimeters could potentially be repurposed for initial data handling. 
• 
Cleaning, maintenance, and calibration module, incorporating automated rinsing, drying, 
and calibration procedures to simplify upkeep. Parallel calibration of electrodes and easy 
reﬁlling of KCl, potassium chloride, (if necessary) should be included. 
• 
Measurement technology, with a preference for glass pH electrodes, as they are the industry 
standard for quality control. ISFET sensors could be considered if matrix effects can be ruled 
out, while chemical optodes have been ruled out based on previous experience from 
Novonesis. 
• 
Regulatory compliance, ensuring the system meets relevant machine directives and industry 
safety standards. 
• 
Cost efficiency, with a design that is more affordable to produce than the iCinac system. 
• 
Automation compatibility, allowing integration with robotic arms or liquid-handling systems 
so the system can be used in a modular way. 
• 
Data management, enabling easy but robust management of the collected data.  
 
 
A.2 Project description and learning objectives
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
121
==PAGE:132==
Learning objectives 
• 
Use user centric design-methods to research and develop solutions.  
• 
Understand the needs of the involved stakeholders.  
• 
Prototypes should be designed, build, tested and evaluated, to determine how the future 
solution should be shaped.  
• 
The ﬁnal prototype should be functional to the highest possible degree within the time frame.  
• 
The design of the ﬁnal products should be documented, in a way that allows for production of 
additional units, depending on the functionality and success.  
• 
The ﬁnal product should fulﬁll the Danish standard known as “Maskin direktivet” 
• 
Understand and use product architecture to create modular and customizable solution. 
• 
Select and apply relevant methods for an iterative design process.

Subtitle: Interview notes from Novonesis visit
Date: 26/02/2025
Development of automatic pH-measurement system for fermentation
Page: 1/1
Andreas Juul-Hansen | s201171
Mathias Rud Jønsson | s203772
Meeting with Kim Olsson 25/02/18 at novonesis
 
Visiting department Process/project development
 
Optimizing acidification in small scale (200-250 ml)
                  Effectivization
                  Better taste
Microliter -> mL -> m^2
 
 
Material used for test: skimmed milk powder
 
The pH buffer is color coded. Red is pH 4 and green is pH 7.
 
Water bath is 30°, 37°, 40° and 43°.
 
When working with L.A.B. a S-curve is produced, this can help understand how 
the products is changing.
 
The product grows based on levels of pH
 
Once one sugar has been depleted a bump in the curve might appear as the 
glucose is used up by the bacteria and lactose is instead begone being 
consumed.
 
We should check wether det iso standard references a pH measurement 
resolution (measuring at 0,1 or 0,01)
 
Notes from laboratory workers:
 
-              Lids on milk glasses are though to open.
-              Don’t see any issues with smaller volumes
-              Much milk is wasted, therefore smaller sample volumes would be great.
-              Color of pH is not comparable (more info needed, but require NDA)
-              Full set of samples is 64 and takes all day to setup.
-              Issues with lowering the temp of water bath.
-              Only QC department uses temp propes in the water bath.
-              Between each buffer the propes are only flushed with demineralized 
water and shaken.
-              The water in the bath is demineralized water
-              Buffer is changed every day.
-              KCl for storage of probes is filled when needed.
-              Often issues with the propes not passing calibration – test cannot be 
started before all propes are cleared. Probe can be cleared manually.
 
Process:
1.        Turn on and preheat waterbath to required temp.
2.        Print list of batches
3.        Check if there is milk
Milk only arrives on thuesdays
Only the ordered amount
The test amount for the day is typically 3 frames with 17 
sample bottle per frame
4.        Double check if there is enough milk for the desired 
test.
5.        Calibration of electrodes
Only the ones used are calibrated.
All probes are calibrated at once.
a.        Temperature stabilization (±1° of probe compared to 
waterbath)
b.        Calibrate after pH 4 (about 1 min)
c.        Rinse with demineralized water and shake
d.        Move to next buffer solution – wait for probes to 
stabilize again (about 10 min)
e.        Calibrate after pH 7 (about 1 min)
f.           Rinse with demineralized water and shake
If the slope value (%) of the electrode is below 97% or above 
103%, it is not cleared.
6.        Insert into sample
7.        Cleaning of probes
Either:
o   Demineralized water -> Ethanol -> Napkin
o   Ethanol 70% soaked napkin (Chicopee veraclean polish 
plus)

In the process of designing the y-axis, one 
issues occurred. Looking at the width of 
the bearing blocks for the linear rods, it 
became clear that they were wider that the 
3030 Bosch profiles we intended to use. 
We therefore started investigation how the 
system should be assembled. 
If the profiles are 
mounted along each 
other the SCS8UU 
bearing blocks are to 
wide to allow for outer 
panels. 
One way to solve this 
is to use a custom 
bearing block with a 
standard LM8UU 
bearing, the challenge 
here is that standard 
components is 
preferred.
The profile holding the 
y-axis could also be 
placed internally to 
give more space to the 
bearing block, this 
however reduces 
space internally. 
The bearing block 
could also be offset to 
accommodate its 
width, this however 
might make it more 
difficult to install the 
rail. 
Lastly the bearing 
could be rotated 90° 
to this will give more 
spacial freedom and 
have less obstruction,  
however, the x-axis 
will then have the bolts 
as the weak point not 
the bearings.

Leadscrew (Lead Screw or Ball Screw)
Principle: 
A leadscrew converts rotational motion into linear motion. A threaded shaft 
(screw) rotates, causing a nut to travel along its length, pushing or pulling 
a load.
Variants
³ Acme Leadscrew: Standard threaded screw with high friction but low 
efficiency©
³ Ball Screw: Uses ball bearings inside the nut to reduce friction and 
increase efficiency.
Common Applications:
³ CNC machine
³ Linear actuator
³ 3D printer
³ Robotic arms
Advantages: 
 High precision 
 Good load-carrying capability
Disadvantages: 
 Slower than belt systems 
 Requires lubrication and maintenance

Belt-Driven System
Principle: 
A flexible belt transmits power between pulleys, creating linear 
or rotational movement. It is commonly used in linear 
actuators where a stepper or servo motor drives the belt.
Common Applications:
s Conveyor belt
s 3D printer
s Robotic arm
s Automated positioning systems
Advantages: 
 Fast motion 
 Quiet and smooth operation 
 Low maintenance
Disadvantages: 
 Lower accuracy than leadscrews 
 Belt stretch over time requires tensioning

Rack and Pinion
Principle: 
A rotating pinion gear (circular) meshes with a linear rack 
gear (toothed bar), converting rotational motion into linear 
motion.
Common Applications:{
¯ Steering systems
¯ CNC machines{
¯ Rail-mounted robotic arms.
Advantages: 
 High load capacity 
 High-speed motion capability 
 Simple and effective for straight-line motion
Disadvantages:
 Needs lubrication 
 May have backlash (small mechanical play between teeth)

Pneumatic (Air Cylinder or Actuator)
Principle: 
Uses compressed air to move a piston inside a cylinder, 
producing linear or rotary motion. The force is generated 
by air pressure differences.
Common Applications¸
ª Pick-and-place robot
ª Factory automationv
ª Medical device
ª Industrial pressing systems
Advantages: 
 Fast response time 
 Simple and reliable 
 No electric components (safe for explosive environments)
Disadvantages: 
 Requires an air compressor and pneumatic lines 
 Limited force and accuracy compared to electric actuators

 High-speed motion

 Can be expensive and energy-intensive

==PAGE:146==
Subtitle: System Automation level
Date: 05/03/2025
Development of automatic pH-measurement system for fermentation
Page: 1/2
Andreas Juul-Hansen | s201171
Mathias Rud Jønsson | s203772
In an attempt to understand how the systems complexity 
increases as it slowly evolve into a more complete product 
we defined 10 steps/concepts. These are made to help 
understand how complex the system should be in order to 
solve the problems and adhere to the given requirements. 

The levels are graded based on the marker in the matrix.
To evaluate each level a set of markers is created
¦ Ergonomic
¦ Amount of probe
¦ Level of automatio¢
¦ Complexit
¦ Feasibilit
¦ Go away tim
¦ Includes data collectio¢
¦ Ease of cleanin
¦ Ease of use
Level 1: Total Manual
As seen in the current situation, no automation, everything is done by hand one probe at a 
time.

Level 2:  Frame Manual
Similar procedure to level one, but the probes are mounted in a frame that lets the lab tech 
handle all probes at once.

Level 3: Frame Manuel with support
Similar procedure to level two but with a support holder to help the lab tech, increasing the 
ergonomics. 

Level 4: Frame on rail
Similar to level 3 but with a rail system that allows it to moved from station to station 
without the lab tech having to lift it.

Level 5: Clean/cal station
At this level an automated wash and calibration system is introduced. This system, can 
clean and calibrate the probes when the frame is inserted. It assist the user when moving 
as with level 4.

Level 6: Motorized
This level is similar to level 5, but the system is motorized. With this the system can move 
the frame back and forth from station to station based on given positions.

Level 7: Motorized with data collection
Same features as with level 6, but the system can also handle data collection. The probes 
are here connected to a system that both controls movement and handle data.

Level 8: Total system - single rack
A total system, that can clean, calibrate and also includes water bath and data collection. 
A single unit that does it all. 

Level 9: Total system - multi rack
A system similar to level 8, but with this it can handle multiple racks and thereby multiple 
tests. This allows lab techs to have one system that handle all their experiments even if 
some test need different temperature or test times.

Level 10: The complete system
This system does the same as level 9, but this time it can also prepare the tests. It fills the 
test bottles, adds the cultures, and when done it empties every thing out. This system 
does not need a lab tech for anything else than uploading the test plan.

A.15 Automation Level 1
136
Design of an Automated pH Measurement System for Milk Acidification
==PAGE:147==
Subtitle: System Automation level
Date: 05/03/2025
Development of automatic pH-measurement system for fermentation
Page: 2/2
Andreas Juul-Hansen | s201171
Mathias Rud Jønsson | s203772
How the Weights Are Decided
The weights reflect the relative importance of each criterion in evaluating the system design 
levels. For example, criteria like Level of Automation and Feasibility are given higher weights 
because they significantly impact efficiency and implementation practicality. Weights are 
assigned so that their total adds up to 1.0, ensuring a balanced and normalized evaluation.

How the Levels Are Evaluated
Each system level (from Level 1 to Level 10) is rated on a scale from 0 to 5 for each criterion, 
where 0 means the feature is not present at all, and 5 indicates excellent performance. These 
scores are then multiplied by the corresponding weights, and the results are summed to 
compute a Total Score for each level. This allows for an objective comparison of how well 
each system configuration meets the defined evaluation criteria.
This weighted decision matrix evaluates ten system levels based on key criteria such as ergonomics, 
automation, feasibility, and ease of use. Each criterion is assigned a weight according to its importance, and 
each level is scored from 0 to 5 for how well it fulfills each criterion. The final score for each level is calculated 
by multiplying the scores with the weights, providing an objective basis for comparing different design options. 
Among the top three scoring levels, Level 8 stands out as the easiest to build, offering a strong balance 
between automation, data collection, and practical feasibility. This makes it a highly attractive option for 
implementation in a first-generation system.

¨ Number of Channels: Indicates the maximum number of simultaneous measurements the

v¨ Communication Protocols: Specifies the methods used by the system to communicate

processes or workflows, which is essential for high-throughput or industrial applications¨

most effectively utilized, such as laboratory research, industrial fermentation, water 
treatment, etc¨
¢¨ Approximate Price: Provides an estimated cost of the system.

We need to change the design to make the parts stronger, and also ensure that the parts are better 
fixturesd. The design works, but the parts need to be updated. 
Based on the prototype, it is clear that some of the parts need to be stronger. They are designed to 
be made from steel, however, as the parts are not finally defined, they are at this point 3D printed. 
Because of this they are not strong enough. There is also som challenges with some of the parts 
not being able to be fixed properly. 
Quality and placement of parts. If all the parts are good and fit each other, we 
are ready to move along. 
By building a prototype any problems should become clear. We want to see if the 
part fit each other, and if the parts are strong enough.

This version marks a substantial leap in both functionality and robustness. The adjustments 
addressed nearly all outstanding mechanical challenges, including those related to alignment, 
rigidity, and friction. With these improvements, the design is approaching a level of maturity 
suitable for more consistent and extended testing.
The bracket was thickened to reduce flex, and a cutout was added to allow easy access to the 
pinion set screw. An extra brace was mounted to further stabilize the X-axis. However, due to 
persistent friction in the system, a second motor was installed on the opposite side to balance the 
drive and ensure reliable movement.
These changes collectively led to a significant improvement. The increased 
thickness and bracing noticeably reduced flex, the cutout made the pinion easy 
to tighten, and the addition of a second motor resolved the friction-related 
limitations in movement. The system now moves more evenly and with 
improved responsiveness.
The updated bracket was tested for accessibility improvements using the new 
cutout, increased rigidity due to added thickness, and the structural effect of 
the second X-axis brace. Furthermore, the ability to maintain consistent motion 
was assessed after adding a second motor on the opposite side of the axis.
This version aimed to simplify assembly—particularly tightening the pinion—and 
to create a more stable and rigid platform capable of handling system loads. The 
goal was also to ensure reliable motion despite friction in the X-axis.
To resolve issues identified in the previous version by improving accessibility to 
the pinion set screw, increasing bracket rigidity, and further reinforcing the X-
axis structure. Additionally, the aim was to address movement limitations 
caused by friction in the system.

The design direction—simplifying the structure with folded geometries—is valid, but the use of 
printed plastic parts as stand-ins for steel proved insufficient. For meaningful mechanical testing, 
stronger materials or reinforced print designs are required. The concept should be developed 
further with either hybrid components or early trials in actual metal to validate the approach more 
accurately.
The printed parts offered a fast and flexible way to iterate on the concept. Their design was 
modeled closely after traditional bent sheet metal parts, with the hope that they could serve both 
as functional components and placeholders for future metal versions.
While the approach helped visualize the final geometry, the printed parts were 
not strong enough. The structure flexed under load, and the mechanical 
stability fell short of what would be expected from actual steel components. 
The assembly did not meet expectations in terms of strength and rigidity.
The prototype was evaluated for mechanical stiffness, print quality under load, 
and how well the 3D-printed geometry held up under conditions typically 
expected of folded steel brackets. Attention was also given to overall 
integration and mounting stability.
The goal was to validate whether 3D-printed parts, shaped to resemble bent 
steel plates, could provide a compact and stable mounting solution when 
integrated with an aluminum profile frame.
To redesign the Z-axis mounting in response to the decision to fixate the X-axis. 
This version explored a new construction approach using 3D-printed parts 
intended to mimic the geometry and function of bent steel sheet components 
mounted on an aluminum profile.

Redesigning the components with printing in mind proved to be a major step forward. The 
structure is now considerably more functional and much closer to a production-ready solution. 
Further iteration is needed to refine details and ensure long-term durability and consistent 
performance, but the general design direction is confirmed to be correct.
Abandoning the imitation of steel parts allowed for a more robust design. By embracing the 
specific strengths and constraints of 3D printing, the parts could be made thicker, better supported, 
and more suited to the expected loads without unnecessary compromises.
This approach resulted in a much stronger and more stable prototype. The parts 
performed significantly better under load and contributed to a more rigid overall 
system. However, despite the improvements, there are still areas that require 
refinement, particularly around tolerance control and further reducing minor flex 
under specific conditions.
The prototype was tested for improvements in stiffness and load resistance. 
The new print-optimized parts were evaluated in terms of printability, ease of 
assembly, and their ability to maintain structural integrity under operational 
forces.
The goal was to create a mechanically stronger and more reliable structure by 
taking full advantage of the design freedom offered by additive manufacturing, 
rather than copying geometries intended for sheet metal.
To address the structural weaknesses identified in the previous version by 
moving away from imitating bent steel parts and instead designing components 
specifically for 3D printing.

While the idea of adding support to the gearing remains valid, the current implementation of the 
brace introduced new issues. It highlighted the importance of flexibility and tolerance in gear 
alignment. The brace design needs to be revisited—either adjusted to allow micro-movements or 
reworked to align more precisely with the existing components. Further testing will be necessary 
to confirm a solution that provides stability without introducing friction.
The addition of the brace provided a visibly more stable mount for the gearing, and the alignment 
appeared more consistent during idle and start-up. The expectation was that this would reduce 
shifting during movement and help maintain performance over time.
Unexpectedly, the new brace introduced additional friction in the system. This 
was likely caused by over-constraining the gear assembly or slight 
misalignment introduced by the brace itself. As a result, system movement 
became less smooth, and more effort was required from the motor to maintain 
motion.
The updated system was tested for gear engagement consistency, mechanical 
stability during movement, and any change in system resistance caused by the 
added support.
The intention was to stabilize the gear interface between motor and lead screw 
to ensure consistent alignment, minimize backlash, and improve long-term 
reliability.
To further improve the structural rigidity of the system by adding a dedicated 
brace to support the motor gearing and reduce deflection under load.

The introduction of bearings was a clear step forward and validated the current geometry of the 
system. While the structural concept now performs acceptably, the next major upgrade will focus 
on improving the quality of the drive system—specifically replacing the 3D-printed gears with more 
durable and precise alternatives to ensure stable long-term operation.
Adding bearings significantly improved motion. The gear assembly became smoother, and the 
system responded better to motor input. The improvement confirmed that the earlier friction was 
related to overconstraint and lack of proper rotational support.
The modification was successful in addressing the major issue from the last 
version. Movement was smoother and required less force. However, the 3D-

and are a likely source of noise, wear, and minor backlash. For long-term

This version resolved one of the final critical mechanical issues. With the braces in place, the Z-axis 
offers the stability required for precise probe positioning. While minor refinements may still be 
needed, the structure is now reliable enough to support testing workflows and integration with 
the larger system.
While the geometry seemed promising in theory, the test setup revealed that the bearings used 
were not suited for the intended purpose. They allowed too much play in the rods, as they were 
designed for smooth, low-friction motion rather than providing a rigid constraint.
The test setup provided valuable insight and helped avoid unnecessary changes 
to the full system. While the widened geometry is conceptually sound, it is only 
effective if supported by tighter or preloaded bearings. Future iterations will 
focus on improved bearing selection to fully leverage the benefits of the new 
layout.
Despite the altered layout, the probe mount continued to tilt during movement. 
The loose fit of the bearings meant the rods were not adequately fixed, and the 
full benefit of the improved geometry could not be realized.
The intention was to reduce tilting and improve stability by gaining better 
mechanical leverage through wider rod placement. The goal was to validate this 
approach in a controlled test before committing to a complete redesign.

to the full system.

This version resolved one of the final critical mechanical issues. With the braces in place, the Z-axis 
offers the stability required for precise probe positioning. While minor refinements may still be 
needed, the structure is now reliable enough to support testing workflows and integration with 
the larger system.
The added braces significantly stiffened the overall structure. Most notably, they prevented the 
tilting that was previously observed during vertical motion, especially when the system was under 
load or at higher speeds.
The part holding the pH probes now remains horizontal throughout the entire 
motion range. Flexing has been visibly reduced, and the movement feels more 
controlled and predictable.
The braces were tested under operational conditions to assess their effect on 
horizontal stability. Measurements were made visually and manually during 
probe travel to check for tilt, flex, or vibrations.
The goal was to ensure that the part holding the pH probes remained horizontal 
and stable throughout the entire Z-axis range, improving both accuracy and 
mechanical consistency.
To improve overall structural rigidity by adding a set of braces designed to 
minimize flexing in the Z-axis assembly, with particular focus on keeping the 
probe holder level during movement.

The proof-of-concept was successful in grounding the design and uncovering early issues. Moving 
forward, the design will incorporate thicker walls and improved support geometry. The printed 
model served as a key step in transitioning from theory to functional prototyping.
Printing the tray provided immediate value. While the general concept held up, it became clear that 
the wall thickness between lanes was too thin, raising concerns about mechanical strength and 
fluid separation. The model also gave a more intuitive understanding of how the system would 
work in practice—something not fully conveyed in CAD or paper sketches.
Although not suitable for final testing, the prototype validated the overall 
layout. It identified critical weak points early and confirmed the need for thicker 
dividing structures and potentially a more rigid base.
The printed model was used to visualize and physically assess the tray’s internal 
lane structure, spacing, and potential weak points. Focus was placed on the 
thickness of dividing walls and general layout compatibility with the probe array.
The aim was to verify the feasibility of the tray design, assess printability, and 
evaluate the separation between lanes before moving into more detailed 
iterations. Key considerations included access for pH probes and leakage risk 
between lanes.
To evaluate the basic concept for a multi-lane calibration tray through a physical 
proof-of-concept model. The goal was to move from digital design to a tangible 
prototype to better understand spatial layout and functional requirements.

The sled and drive system work as intended, and this version validated the mechanical architecture 
for moving the tray. Future iterations will need to integrate liquid handling features and consider 
how movement interacts with fluid containment and sensor positioning. This prototype lays the 
foundation for those refinements.
The sled concept proved effective. The bearing blocks provided consistent, low-resistance travel, 
and the belt-driven motion system operated reliably. The rigid tray mount also gave better control 
over positioning, enabling early movement testing and calibration sequence simulation.
This version successfully demonstrated controlled tray movement. It marked a 
step forward in system development by transitioning from static trays to 
motion-enabled configurations. However, this prototype did not account for 
liquid handling, such as filling, draining, or containing fluids during motion—an 
essential consideration for functional calibration.
The sled was mounted and connected to a motor and belt system, allowing 
horizontal movement across bearing blocks. Tests were conducted to evaluate 
smoothness, precision, and mechanical stability under motion.
The goal was to create a stable, low-friction movement platform that could later 
support automated calibration procedures. This iteration focused solely on 
mechanical movement—liquid handling was not yet integrated.
To develop and test a movement system for the calibration tray using a sled-

belt-actuated system mounted on bearing blocks.

The redesign successfully brought the tray closer to a functional prototype ready for full system 
testing. With the heating and sensing infrastructure in place, and geometry optimized for fluid 
movement, this version prepares the foundation for upcoming experiments with real calibration 
liquids and thermal conditions.
Downsizing the tray required altering parts of the internal layout and geometry to maintain usable 
lane volume. These changes were necessary to make room for additional hardware and to ensure 
better thermal transfer and control. The smaller footprint also improved integration potential 
within the full system layout.
This version fit well on the existing sled and accommodated the new

behavior in the next iteration.
The new tray was printed and assembled with mounting features for a heat pad

The goal was to create a more compact calibration tray that could support

This iteration of the sled is fully compatible with the current calibration tray and prepares the 
system for fluid and thermal testing. With all mounting and interface features working as intended, 
future efforts will focus on validating performance under operational conditions.
The updated sled features an open underside for routing the outlet and waste fluid line, and a 
revised mounting interface that allows the tray to snap in place without tools. Dedicated recesses 
for the heatpad and temperature sensor were successfully integrated into the base platform, 
ensuring direct thermal contact with the tray bottom.
The prototype fulfilled all structural and spatial requirements. The tray installs 
and removes easily, and the outlet line can be routed without interference. The 
base provides sufficient surface area for future temperature regulation. No 
critical issues were identified during assembly or dry-fitting.
The new sled was printed and assembled with the updated tray. Tests focused 
on verifying clearance for the outlet block, evaluating ease of tray mounting/
dismounting, and checking whether space and alignment for thermal 
components were adequate.
The goal was to create a mechanically stable platform that accommodates new 
hardware components—particularly the outlet block—and simplifies day-to-day 
handling through tool-free installation of the calibration tray. In addition, the 
sled should provide space for a heatpad and temperature sensor beneath the 
tray.

functionality.

The concept helped visualize the layout and connection scheme, but its performance was limited 
by internal flow losses. To improve efficiency, future iterations will need to address pressure drop—
possibly through optimized internal geometry, use of higher-pressure input, or by rethinking the 
distribution mechanism altogether.
The manifold-inspired design provided a straightforward approach for distributing fluid, but it 
revealed a key limitation during testing: significant pressure drop occurred along the internal 
channels, especially toward the outermost nozzles. This resulted in uneven cleaning performance.
While the form factor was compact and easy to assemble, the pressure 
distribution proved inadequate. The spray was noticeably weaker at the outlets 
furthest from the inlet, leading to poor coverage and inconsistent cleaning 
effectiveness.
The prototype was evaluated based on spray reach, uniformity of distribution 
across outlets, and overall pressure retention. Particular attention was given to 
whether sufficient cleaning force could be maintained at each spray point.
The goal was to create a compact and simple manifold-like structure capable of 
delivering pressurized cleaning fluid to multiple locations with consistent flow 
and spray coverage.
To test an initial concept for a cleaning sprayer inspired by automotive intake 
manifolds. The design aimed to distribute cleaning fluid evenly across multiple 
outlets by branching from a single inlet.

The concept of using slotted pipes remains valid for achieving a low-cost, manufacturable cleaning 
head, but slot size and spacing need to be carefully optimized. Future designs should use smaller 
or more precisely shaped openings to maintain pressure and ensure effective fluid distribution.
The design was simple to assemble and well-suited for low-complexity manufacturing. However, 
the slots in the pipes turned out to be too large, causing a rapid loss of pressure along the length 
of the tubes.
While the sprayer functioned and the overall concept was promising in terms of 
manufacturability, the oversized slots severely reduced pressure and led to 
weak spray at all outlet points. As a result, cleaning performance dropped below 
acceptable levels.
This prototype was tested for spray coverage, manufacturing feasibility, and 
pressure retention. The effect of slot dimensions on flow rate and cleaning force 
was closely monitored.
The intention was to achieve a uniform spray distribution through a pair of 
parallel pipes, each fitted with multiple slots to release pressurized cleaning 
fluid.
To explore a cleaning sprayer design based on simple pipe construction, aiming 
for a solution that is easy to manufacture using standard tubing and minimal 
machining.

This iteration confirmed that shortening the flow path improves performance, but also highlighted 
that fluid dynamics in small branch manifolds are highly sensitive to geometry and layout. The test 
also indicated that simply increasing inner diameter is not enough to solve the pressure imbalance. 
Future designs will need to consider flow balancing features or active control to ensure consistent 
cleaning force across all outlets.
The use of six short branches showed improved pressure compared to earlier designs, especially at 
the central outlets. However, outer branches still showed noticeable pressure lag. Surprisingly, the 
increase in internal diameter between versions did not result in a significant performance 
difference.
This version delivered more stable pressure to the central outlets but failed to 
provide uniform spray across all branches. The hypothesis that increased 
diameter would improve flow was not supported by the results. All three 
versions performed similarly, and outer nozzles remained underpowered.
Each of the three prototypes was tested under the same water pressure. The 
inner diameters were scaled progressively, with each version doubling the 
previous one. The test focused on pressure uniformity across all six outlets and 
general cleaning force at each location.
The goal was to achieve more balanced pressure across all spray outlets by 
reducing the length of each channel and testing whether increasing internal 
diameter could help maintain flow to the outer branches.
To improve spray distribution by replacing two long slotted pipes with six short 
branches in a manifold-like configuration. Three different versions were 
produced to test the effect of varying internal pipe diameter.

This version proved that targeted cleaning using a circular jet arrangement can be highly effective. 
While it currently only cleans one probe, the concept could be expanded into a modular or rotating 
system. The design direction is highly promising and opens up possibilities for probe-specific, high-
efficiency cleaning systems.
Although this design only supported cleaning one probe at a time, the circular geometry created a 
strong, directed spray that enveloped the probe effectively. The inspiration from rocket nozzle 
shaping helped produce a consistent and centered flow pattern.
The cleaning performance was the most promising so far. Despite its single-
probe limitation, the design showed excellent force distribution, minimal 
pressure loss, and a well-contained spray zone. It also demonstrated clear 
potential for additive manufacturing due to its complex but compact form.
The prototype was 3D printed and tested under consistent water pressure. 
Observations were made regarding spray coverage, cleaning force, and any 
turbulence or stagnation around the probe surface. Visual cleanliness and 
repeatability were also considered.
The goal was to achieve a controlled, high-velocity spray concentrated around a 
single probe, testing whether a focused cleaning mechanism could outperform 
previous multi-outlet designs in terms of effectiveness and fluid efficiency.
To evaluate a new cleaning concept using a circular spray pattern inspired by

manufacturing and focused on cleaning a single pH probe with high precision.

The design confirms the viability of scaling the concept to multiple probes, but it also highlights a 
trade-off between flow rate and cleaning pressure. Future improvements should focus on 
optimizing hole size and possibly introducing flow control elements to balance water volume with 
cleaning effectiveness.
The larger-scale implementation successfully retained the geometric benefits of the original 
circular design. However, increasing the spray hole diameter led to noticeable pressure reduction 
across the system, affecting spray sharpness and cleaning force.
The system worked and was capable of cleaning multiple probes in parallel, but 
the increased water volume came at the cost of reduced pressure. The overall 
cleaning effect was weaker compared to the previous single-probe version,

The prototype was tested with multiple probes mounted in place. Observations

the increased hole size affected internal pressure and performance.

The concept proved valid and demonstrated the viability of incorporating airflow into the cleaning 
process. Moving forward, the design must be revised with a strong focus on assembly, mounting 
stability, and integration with other components. Future iterations will also explore modularity and 
serviceability.
Functionally, the blower setup confirmed that airflow could be effectively channeled away from 
electronics and redirected toward the cleaning zone. However, the design had not accounted for 
ease of assembly. Mounting the components was cumbersome, and tolerances between the hood 
and shroud were not optimized.
The prototype performed as intended in terms of airflow behavior. The dual-
component layout allowed for directional control and coverage across the 
sprayer area. However, the lack of assembly planning made the installation 
process inefficient and fragile.
The prototype was printed and assembled with a fan mounted in the hood. Basic 
airflow direction, strength, and interaction with the surrounding components 
were observed. Special attention was paid to airflow path around the sprayers 
and exposure to electronics.
The goal was to confirm that a low-pressure airflow system could assist in 
drying the probes after cleaning without interfering with electronic components 
or surrounding structures.
To explore a proof-of-concept for an air-based drying system as part of the 
cleaning process. The design consisted of two main components: a fan hood to 
direct airflow away from nearby electronics and a fan shroud to distribute air 
evenly around the cleaning sprayers.
1.0
Drying

 We will look into other relay boards and might need to have in mind that we need to control a lot of 
different components independently. Therefore we might consider a relay board with more than 8 
channels. We will see if we can find a board that is communicating via e.g. I2C Awesome other 
protocol that uses less wires. Also if we can still get them in solid state type we will prefer that to 
avoid the clicking sound when a relay is turning on and off.
It was easy to connect this type of relay board to the Raspberry Pi. One downside is that you need 
to have one wire hooked up to each channel in order to control them independently.
It was easy to control the relay board from the Raspberry Pi just by turning on 
and off the different outputs on the 40 pin header. Unfortunately  It was found 
that the solid state relays only worked for 230 Volt AC and not 12 Volt DC. After 
more research it was found that the solid state relays need to be specifically 
designed for either alternating current or direct current. We therefore need to 
look into other ones in order to make it work with our 12 Volt components.
 We will connect the relay board to the Raspberry Pi. From the pie, it will, in this 
test scenario, just receive its power from that. tear all the different relay 
channels needs its own separate wire that will also connect to the Raspberry Pi. 
The systems both work on 3,3 volts, so that's nice.
We hope that we can control the 12 volts going from the power supply to the 
different devices can be controlled by the solid state relays reliably.
In order to control the different pumps, fans and other stuff we need relays to 
turn them on and off. We had laying around a solid state Relay board that is 
silent when it operates. .We want to test that and see if it works.

We will use this relay board to control the different peristaltic pumps high pressure water, air blade 
fan, heating elements and so on. Now we have also found the error in the library and can continue 
without any hassle.
This is a great relabel that only takes two wires on the Raspberry Pi. It works great and there's a 
total of 16 channels in all. Also the low and high voltage areas are separated by optocouplers so if 
something goes wrong it won't fry all the electronics.
There was quite a lot of difficulties with a found library to control the I2C chip 
on the relay board. First, we needed to create a virtual environment where we 
then installed different libraries in, which is the new way on Raspberry Pi’s and 
also the proper way to do it.  Afterwards we got a lot of errors to begin with and 
couldn't control the relays. With some help from Chat GPT we could then figure 
out what was wrong in the library and where the error code came from. After 
finding a naming issue, inside one of the library files, we could easily change it 
and then it worked perfectly. This did take a some time though.
We will connect the new relay ball to the Raspberry Pi. Even though a technique 
only needs two wires we also take the power from the Raspberry Pi and also 
share their ground.
We hope that we can turn on and off the relays and then control the 12 Volt 
current going in and then out to the different components.
We received a new relay board this time with electromechanical relays Instead 
of solid state relays. The good thing about this one though is that it has a total 
of 16 relays and talks via I2C. That means it actually only takes up two wires on 
the Raspberry Pi.

The PH4502C module was successfully brought into operation with the ESP32 despite early 
hurdles. It now delivers a stable signal at reference conditions, allowing us to move on to soft 
calibration and long-term pH testing.v
While the module eventually provided reliable data, the process underscored the need for clearer 
documentation and interpretation guidelines. Interfacing analog sensors without standardized 
outputs poses a challenge, particularly for non-standardized modules.
The sensor produced consistent readings after adjustments. Initially, the 
voltage response in KCl buffer was confusing, and documentation was sparse, 
requiring several experiments to understand its behavior and tune the onboard 
potentiometers effectively.
The module was wired to the ESP32 and hard-calibrated by shorting the BNC 
input. Serial output was monitored via custom code. The calibration was fine-
tuned so that when placed in a pH 7 liquid, the voltage would align with 1.65 V. 
Various trial-and-error iterations were used due to limited documentation.
Obtain a stable and interpretable voltage output from the module, centered 
around 1.65 V at pH 7, corresponding to the mid-point of the ESP32 ADC range.
To integrate the PH4502C pH sensor module with the ESP32 and perform an 
initial calibration to prepare for accurate pH measurement.

The full input system is now integrated and operational. While calibration is pending, the core

This prototype confirms reliable communication and visualization. It forms the foundation for 
future interface development and real-time monitoring.
Using JSON made data parsing simple and scalable. Live feedback improved our ability to debug 
and interpret system behavior directly during testing.
The communication worked seamlessly, and the plot updated live. Temperature

The DS18B20 is a suitable candidate for distributed temperature sensing in the project. It offers 
simple integration, reliable readings, and flexible expansion. Future steps should include 
understanding its internal calibration or performing external calibration with a reference 
thermometer.
The DS18B20 sensor was very easy to integrate and use with the ESP32 platform. The 1-Wire bus 
system also allows multiple sensors to be connected on a single pin, which is promising for our use 
case. However, it is currently unclear what calibration or reference the sensor uses internally, 
which could become important for accuracy-critical measurements.
The sensor was detected immediately, and its temperature was printed to the 
terminal. Temperature readings were stable and showed a smooth increase 
when the sensor was held by hand. The serial monitor confirmed continuous 
output every second. Output formatting and response time were both 
acceptable for use in a real-world setup.
The prototype must show that the DS18B20 can provide readable and stable 
temperature data through an ESP32. It was built using a breadboard, ESP32, and 
a pull-up resistor. Basic code was written to detect the sensor and stream values 
to the serial monitor. Evaluation was done by observing live terminal output.
The sensor should be successfully detected by the ESP32 and return stable and 
realistic temperature values via the serial monitor. The sensor’s 1-Wire address 
should also be identifiable for use in multi-sensor configurations later on.
To evaluate whether the DS18B20 digital temperature sensor can be correctly 
wired, initialized, and read using an ESP32 microcontroller. This includes testing 
the 1-Wire interface, verifying proper data output, and identifying the sensor’s 
unique address.

The test confirmed stable and addressable communication with all five sensors. The method of 
identifying and naming the sensors worked well..
The 1-wire protocol works well for multiple sensors on a single line. Labeling based on address 
suffixes proved an efficient way to keep track of sensors. The system is scalable and requires 
minimal wiring, which is ideal for compact setups.
All five sensors were successfully located with distinct addresses. As each 
sensor was manually heated, its corresponding temperature rose clearly in the 
terminal, confirming accurate mapping between address and physical sensor.
Five sensors were wired to one 1-wire bus using an ESP32 on a breadboard with 
a pull-up resistor. A script was written to scan and list sensor addresses and 
display temperatures in real time. Heating each sensor by hand allowed visual 
validation of identification and response.
To verify that multiple DS18B20 temperature sensors can be connected to the 
same 1-wire bus, identified, and individually read by an ESP32.

The soldered version is a clear upgrade over the breadboard prototype. It’s stable, easy to connect, 
and ready for integration into the system’s electronics enclosure.
Moving from breadboard to protoboard improved reliability and usability. The physical robustness is 
now sufficient for use inside the full system. Cable relief is crucial when many leads are bundled.
All five sensors were successfully read with stable data. No connection issues 
were observed, and the mechanical support worked well under cable movement.
All sensors were moved to a protoboard and soldered along with a shared pull-

This setup proves our physical heating system works as intended. Fine-tuning of the control logic 
will be needed to stabilize the temperature within tighter tolerances.
The heating pad is powerful enough, and the system responds well. However, bang-bang control 
causes fluctuation due to thermal inertia, showing the need for PID refinement.
The water was successfully heated from room temperature to the desired 
range. The simple control caused small overshoots and undershoots but 
demonstrated overall heating functionality.
The heat pad was attached under the steel bath, a circulation pump added, and 
five submerged temperature sensors placed in falcon tubes. Data was visualized 
live on a Raspberry Pi to monitor system response.
Verify that the system can heat water to a target temperature using a simple 
bang-bang control loop, and observe the temperature behavior through live 
monitoring.
To test if a silicone heating pad and a circulation pump are sufficient to heat and 
maintain the temperature in a stainless steel water bath.

The integration of PID control brings the thermal system to a functional and precise state, 
confirming its suitability for fermentation experiments requiring accurate temperature conditions.
PID control significantly improved the water bath’s stability. Residual heat effects were minimized, 
and temperature control is now consistent enough for testing procedures.
The system stabilized around 40 °C, with fluctuations well below ±0.5 °C. PID 
output modulation clearly tracked thermal demand across the test period.
A PID loop was implemented and tuned using empirical testing and Ziegler-
Nichols-inspired adjustment. Sensor placement followed a predefined layout to 
ensure representative feedback across the bath.
Maintain a stable temperature of 40 °C with reduced overshoot and oscillation, 
within the tolerances defined in the report.
To improve temperature regulation in the water bath by replacing bang-bang
